The author of this book says: When I have completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is a part of the science of medicine in its entirety, and have reached the limit of its clarity and explanation, I saw that I should complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the practical work.
Because practical work is completely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that its knowledge is almost extinct and its effect has ceased. And only a few outlines of it remain in the books of the ancients, which have been copied by hands and marred by mistakes and confusion, until its meanings have become obscure and its usefulness distant.
Therefore I saw fit to revive it and to compose this treatise on it in the way of explanation, clarification, and brevity, and to present pictures of cauterizing irons and other surgical instruments, since this is an aid to explanation and a guarantee of what is needed.
The reason why there is no skilled craftsman in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first practice in the science of anatomy, which Galen described. until he understands the functions, forms, temperaments, connections, and separations of the organs, and the knowledge of the bones, nerves, muscles, their number, origins, the pulsating and non-pulsating vessels, and the locations of their origins.
Therefore Hippocrates said that physicians in name are many, but in deed few, especially in the craft of surgery, and we have mentioned a part of this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable in what we have mentioned of anatomy will inevitably fall into error, killing people thereby, as I have often witnessed in those who imagined themselves skilled in this science and claimed it without knowledge or expertise.
And that is because I saw an ignorant physician who incised a pig-like tumor in a woman's neck, and severed some of the neck's arteries, so the woman bled until she fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician who proceeded to remove a stone from a man who had a fistula, and the stone was large, so he was reckless and removed it with a piece of the bladder substance. So the man died after about three days, and I had been summoned to remove it, and I saw from the size of the stone and the state of the patient that I could have assessed it.
And I saw another physician who earned his living practicing medicine with some of the brothel keepers of our city.
Then it happened to a black boy who had a fracture in his leg near the heel with a wound, and the physician, in his ignorance, hastened and bound the fracture over the wound with bandages and splints very tightly, and did not leave room for the wound to breathe.
Then he indulged his desires, then left him for days and ordered him not to untie the bandage until his leg and foot swelled and he was near death.
Then I was summoned to him, so I hastened to untie the bandage, and he found relief and was freed from his pains, except that the corruption had become entrenched in the limb and I could not prevent it.
Then the corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
And I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, and it ulcerated after days until the suffering of its owner greatly increased. That is because if the cancer is purely of black humor, then it should not be treated with iron at all.
Unless it is in a limb that can be entirely excised.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual operations are divided into two parts: an operation accompanied by safety, and an operation accompanied by harm in most cases.
And I have warned in every place in this book where this comes up about operations which are risky and dangerous, and you should therefore avoid and reject them.
Lest the ignorant find a way to criticize and attack, therefore take caution and circumspection for yourselves, and gentleness and deliberation for your patients.
And use the best method leading to safety and a favorable outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure diseases.
And keep yourselves clear from what you fear might cast doubt upon your religion and your worldly life; for this is more lasting for your reputation and higher for your status in this world and the next.
For Galen said in some of his precepts: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become a bad physician.
This chapter has been divided into three sections.
The first section.
On cauterization with fire and with strong medicine, methodically ordered from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments and cautery irons, and everything needed for the work by hand.
And the second section.
On incision, puncture, bloodletting, cupping, wounds, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all methodically ordered, with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third section.
On setting, reduction, and the treatment of dislocations and the like, methodically ordered from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first section.
On cautery.
And before we mention its use, it is necessary to mention its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
And I say that discussion of the benefits and harms of cautery is lengthy, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and a number of wise men have spoken of it.
And they differed in it, and I have shortened their words for fear of lengthening.
And I say that cautery generally benefits every bad temperament that is with a material thing and without a material thing, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without a material thing and the dry temperament without a material thing.
As for the dry temperament with a material thing, they differed concerning it. Some said that cautery is beneficial in it, and others said the opposite, that cautery is not suitable for a disease that is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
And it is impossible to cure a hot, dry disease with a hot, dry medicine. And he who says the opposite says that cautery with fire may benefit from a hot, dry disease that occurs in the bodies of people. Because when you add the body of man and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you affect the body of man with coldness.
And I say as he says, because experience has revealed that to me many times, except that one should not imagine that matter except one who has trained and practiced in the matter of cautery to a great extent. And he has observed the differences in the temperaments of people and the state of diseases in themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and their duration.
But as for the other temperaments, there is no fear for you from them, especially cold, moist diseases; all the physicians have agreed on them and have not differed in the benefit of cautery in them.
And know, O my son, that among the secrets of cautery with fire and its superiority to cautery with a burning medicine is that fire is a simple element whose effect does not exceed the member which is cauterized, and it does not harm another member connected to it except slightly.
And cautery with a burning medicine may extend its effect to other members, and it may cause a disease in the member which is difficult to treat, and it may even kill.  Fire, because of its nobility and the excellence of its essence, does not do that except if one overdoes it.
And this has become clear to us through experience, long service, attention to the craft, and understanding the realities of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discussion. If it were not unbecoming of this book of mine, I would have presented to you concerning fire a hidden secret and the manner of its action in bodies and its expulsion of diseases with philosophical, demonstrative discourse too subtle for your understanding.
And know, O my sons, that they have differed concerning the time suitable for cautery, and they have considered the best time to be springtime.
And I say that cautery is suitable at any time, because the harm caused by the time is consumed by the benefit which is brought about by cautery. Especially if the cautery is for strong, urgent, necessary pains which do not tolerate delay, because of what is feared from them, that a calamity might follow which is greater than the slight harm arising from the time.
And let it not occur to you, O my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cautery which cures a certain disease will never return, and they make it a reproach.
And the matter is not as they suppose, because cautery is only like a medicine which changes the temperament and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
Except that cautery is preferable to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
And it is possible that the disease may return after a time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength of his disease, and what is prepared in his body of an accumulation of humors, and his negligence in acquiring them from foods and the like of causes.
Except that the disease in which cautery is used is a mild disease and in a member with few humors and moistures. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like; for it is possible that that pain may not return, and that is the least.
And as for the common people's saying also that cautery is the last of medicine, it is a true saying, not to what they go to, for they believe that no treatment benefits with medicine or otherwise after the occurrence of cautery, and the matter is contrary to that.
And the meaning of cautery being the last of medicine is only that when we use types of treatment in a disease and those medicines do not work, then we use the last thing, cautery, and it works; from here it happened that cautery is the last of medicine. Not in the meaning to which the common people and many ignorant physicians went.
The ancients mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said that because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and they said that the place of cauterization does not suppurate; and that is not absolutely so.
Because I have tried that and found that it only does that in some bodies and not others, and cauterization with it is better and more excellent than iron, as they said; except that if you heat the cautery iron in the fire, made of gold, you will not know when it is heated to the degree you want, due to the redness of the gold.
And because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase the heat on it, it melts in the fire and is spoiled, and the artisan falls into trouble because of that; therefore, cauterization with iron is, for us, quicker and closer to correctness in practice, God willing.
And I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections which I have organized from head to foot, to make it easier for the student what he wants from it, God willing.
The first section.
On cauterizing the head, one cauterization.
This cauterization benefits from the predominance of moisture and cold on the brain, which are the cause of headache and frequent colds from the head to the area of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, and toothache and throat pain. And in short, for every illness that arises from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, aphasia and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is that you first order the patient to vomit with a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, depending on the strength of the patient, his age, and his habit.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him before you cross-legged, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then you place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes; where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery, which is of this form.
If the bone is exposed to the extent of the head of a toothpick or the size of a carob seed, lift your hand; otherwise, reapply your hand with the same instrument or another if it has cooled. Until you see of the bone what I mentioned to you. Then take some salt, dissolve it in water, soak a cotton swab in it, place it on the spot, and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter to it and leave it there until the eschar from the cautery falls away. Then treat it with the quaternary ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they have said that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it discharges, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have mentioned cauterizing the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the thickness of the bone is burned, then removing what was burned from the bone, and then treating it.
Others said it is necessary to exaggerate with the cautery so that it strongly affects the bone, until it falls from the bone in the shape of a carat or small flake. They claimed that this vents the vapors of the head from that spot, and the wound should be left open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals. I do not see these two types of cautery at all except in some people and in a haphazard way.  In my opinion, leaving it untreated is better, and if it is done, it is safer, for the head weakens when its natural connection is broken, as we have seen in other members.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it, so use it and you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
Also on cauterizing the head.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and it persists in the patient, and applications, collyria, snuffs, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if a single cauterization, as we have described, has been performed without benefit, then look. If the patient's head is naturally strong and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize it again a little above the previous cauterization. Then cauterize each temple of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone we described is exposed. And cauterize him in the back of his head in the place known as the hatchet, and lighten your hand in this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will feel severe pain from this unlike the pain of all other cauterizations of the head, and I will mention this cauterization in its place.
And the cautery with which the temples and the back of the head are cauterized should be gentler than the cautery with which the middle of the head is cauterized, and this is its image.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing non-chronic migraine.
If pain occurs in a fissure of the head along with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then let the patient vomit with head-purifying medicines and use all the treatments I mentioned in the sections on illnesses; if that does not work, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, take a single clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides, then incise the painful area of the temple with a wide scalpel until there is a space under the skin large enough for the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears, then bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it for about fifteen hours, then unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Dress it with cotton soaked in butter until the area suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. And if you wish, do this with some of the burning medicines which I mentioned in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is in this manner: heat the cautery iron, which is pictured thus:
And it is called the nail cautery because its head is like the head of a nail, with some perforations and a small projection in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while you rotate the cautery iron little by little, and the amount you burn should be about half the thickness of the skin. And lift your hand so that you do not burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding, then soak cotton in salt water and apply it to the area and leave it for three days. Then dress the cotton with ointment and then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this headache with the knife-like end of the cautery iron, and be careful not to cut the artery in this chronic headache especially.
Chapter Four.
Cauterization of the chronic headache.
If you treat the headache with what we have mentioned of the preceding treatment and what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and you see a cause for which what we mentioned of the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is not sufficient. Then it is necessary that you heat the knife-like cautery iron until it glows white, after you have marked on the place of the pain a line the length of half a finger or thereabouts, and you leave your hand for one moment while you pull it so that you sever the artery and reach towards the bone.
Except that it is necessary for you to be cautious of the contact of the jaw which moves during mastication, lest you burn the muscle or the nerve which moves it, and so cause tetanus.
And be cautious and watchful of the bleeding of the artery which you have cut, for there is danger in its being cut. Especially for one who is ignorant of what he is doing and has not been a trained and experienced person; and to leave the work is better.
And the treatment of accidental bleeding from the artery will be mentioned in its proper place in the book, God willing; if you see from the condition something which this cauterization does not overcome.
And you saw the body of the patient able to tolerate it, cauterize him in the middle of the head as we described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cautery which we mentioned in the chapter on the opening of the artery with the knife-cautery, for it is a better and more effective cautery than this.
Chapter Five.
In cauterizing the pains of the ears.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold and it is treated with laxatives and other treatments which we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not go away, then heat the cautery which is called the point, whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, prick with it around the whole ear, as it goes around, or around both if the pain is in both. And keep the cauterization a little distance from the root of the ear, after marking the place with ink. The cauterization should be about ten points in each ear, or thereabouts. Then treat the places until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
Cauterization of Paralysis.
The paralysis treated by cauterization is that type which arises from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
And one should avoid cauterizing the type which arises from dryness and spasm of the nerve.
When you have treated this type of paralysis with plasters, snuffs, and gargles and your treatment has been ineffective, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the base of the ear, the second a little below his temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
And make your cauterization on the opposite side from the diseased side, because the relaxation occurs on the side which appears healthy.
And the image of the cauterization is that you cauterize with a cauterization opposite the upper tip of the ear, a little below the horn of the head, and another in the temple, and its length should be the length of the thumb.
Lower your hand with the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin.  This is the image of the cautery; it is a type of scalpel, the image of which has preceded, but it is a little gentler, as you see. The scalpel should be a little thicker than usual, then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
If the apoplexy becomes chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned, and your treatment is not effective, and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize him in four places. One cauterization on each temple, one in the middle of the head as previously mentioned, and one at the back of the head as before.
The application of the cautery is as previously described.  A cauterization may also be applied to the pit of the stomach, which will be more effective, then treat as before.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
If it is caused by phlegm, the patient should first be given strong purgatives and brain-cleansing pills.
Then his whole head should be shaved and a mustard plaster, as described in the chapter on plasters, should be applied to the back of his head several times, for this is a kind of cauterization.
Do this in the same manner as I have described there; if he is cured by that, well and good; otherwise, cauterize him three times at the back of his head, in a row from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Make the distance between each cauterization the width of a finger.  Then treat the cauterization as described above. If you wish to increase [the treatment] and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle area.
If you wish to increase [the treatment], cauterize the temples, then treat it until it heals. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterizing paralysis and general body relaxation.
It is necessary that you begin by purifying the head with purgatives and as we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, then cauterize: one cauterization in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in the case of relaxation of the body, you need more than that, and the patient can tolerate it and the disease is strong and persistent, then cauterize with four cauterizations on the vertebrae of his back. Cauterize until you burn most of the skin, then lift your hand, and then treat him as previously mentioned until he is healed, God willing.  The cautery should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterizing epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is due to phlegm is cauterized.  It is necessary to first cleanse his brain with strong purgatives and with all the other treatments we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and able to take medication.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medication, then use gargles and masticatories that cleanse the brain for many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then his head is shaved, then one cauterization is performed in the middle of the head as described before, and another at the back. And one cauterization on each temple. If the patient is strong and able to withstand it, perform the cauterizations described for the patient with paralysis and bodily relaxation, on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back; and the cautery should be olive-shaped, as described previously.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery mild in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, perform the cauterizations that we mentioned for the patient with paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess tending towards black bile and the patient's body is moist, purge what cleanses his brain as was previously mentioned in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tightly woven linen cake in the shape of a circle, then place it in the middle of his head while the patient sits cross-legged holding it on all sides, then take one pound of old sheep tallow.
Then heat it over a fire to a moderate temperature, as much as the finger can withstand when inserted, then pour it into the middle of his head inside the circle and leave it until it cools.  Do this for the patient once every week along with other good treatments until he is cured, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures, without holding the cautery iron, but rather by applying it lightly; for this type of cautery moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton wool pad soaked in tallow or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing the watery discharge in the eye.
If the watery discharge in the eye becomes apparent to you through the signs I mentioned in the classification, then act quickly, and purge the patient of what pollutes his head and purify him of all humors, and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize in the middle of the head, then cauterize twice on the temples if the onset of the watery discharge is in both eyes, or on one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin, and let the cauterizations be lengthwise across the temples, and safeguard against bleeding.
If you see something of it, cut it off immediately with whatever treatment is possible for you.  And we shall come to the wisdom in cauterizing and cutting the arteries and safeguarding against bleeding.
And it may be cauterized at the nape of the neck below the two bones with two deep cauterizations.
Chapter Thirteen.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and are from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, from outside the head, and you are certain that it is from cold, thick, phlegmatic superfluities, then cauterize it with the cauterization which I described in the beginning of the section on epiphora. A cauterization in the middle of the head, and two cauterizations on the temples, and two cauterizations at the nape of the neck below the two bones. And if you need to increase [the treatment], then cauterize one cauterization on each side of the tail of the eye at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
Concerning the cauterization of a foul-smelling nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the classification and the treatment is not effective, then quickly purge the patient with colocynth for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle with an olive-shaped cautery iron. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery iron, two cauterizations above the eyebrows, slightly below the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it. And this is the image of the nail-shaped [cautery iron].
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the cauterization of a relaxed eyelid.
If an eyelid becomes relaxed due to illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with one cauterization using this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize a little above the eyebrows, two cauterizations on each side, and keep them distant from the temples, and let the length of each cauterization be the length of the eyebrow.
And do not exaggerate with the cauterization, but only to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned, and the shape of the cautery iron is like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its margins are inverted inwards so that the eye is injured, then cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medicine. As for cauterization with fire, you should order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelids alone, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and even.
If he plucks them while they are growing, then bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow.  When they have grown and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, then mark the eyelid with ink, a mark in the shape of an aspen leaf.
The beginning of the mark should be near the margins. Then place under the eyelid cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard. Then heat a cautery iron, its shape is this.
Then cauterize according to the mark you made, little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the aspen leaf, is entirely burned, especially its surface. A sign of the correctness of your work is that you see the eyelid curled and the hair raised from the flesh of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days, then apply cotton with butter to it until the scab comes off, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If some of the hair returns after a time and the eyelid relaxes,
repeat the cauterization in that place as you did before. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a burning medicine, it is that you instruct the patient to leave the margins until they grow long and even.
Then make from paper the shape of an aspen leaf. Then take of the usual soap and of unslaked lime, of each one a drachm weight or so. Crush them both together well and do this quickly lest it dry. Then spread some of it on the paper which you made in the shape of an aspen leaf and place it on one or both eyelids. Place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head in your lap.
And place your index finger on top of the medicine and press it slightly, moving it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medicine, for he will feel a burning sensation. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. When the burning ceases, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And look; if you see that the eyelid has risen as it rises when cauterized with fire or with cutting, then do otherwise.  Repeat applying the medicine to the place where the medicine did not take effect and did not blacken, until you complete your work and the eyelid is cauterized. Then place cotton with fat on it until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm ointment or another until it heals.  It is necessary for you during the procedure to be extremely careful that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after some days and something like hair is suspected in the eye.
Then reapply the medicine to that place of the eyelid which has relaxed, specifically as you did before, then treat it until it heals. This is the image of the aspen leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size, so let your cauterization be according to that. The correct way is not hidden from one who has experience in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the ulcer that appears in the canthus of the eye.
If you treat the ulcer with what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is not effective, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap, and let a servant hold his head in front of you in such a way that he does not move or shake his head.
Then place a cotton ball moistened with egg white or the saliva of a frog on his eyes, then heat the cautery which is of this shape.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather on the one side which is used for cauterization, and if you wish it may be perforated on the other end, and if you wish it may be solid like a nail, except that the hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then squeeze the fistula if it is open and remove its contents and dry it, or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus; then place the cautery on it while it is very hot, holding it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
And move your hand a little away from the eye towards the nose during cauterization, lest your hand slip or the patient become restless and the cautery fall into the eyelid and damage it. If you reach the bone with the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization a second time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with butter and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply a strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and remove it as will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a fistula, mentioned by some of the ancients: you aim at the location of the fistula, incise it, then place in the incision a thin cone, its image is as follows.
Pour into it the amount of one dirham of molten lead, and hold your hand on the cone firmly, clamped down, and the patient must not move at all, lest the molten lead flow into his eye.  It is advisable to place on the patient's eye a cotton ball moistened with egg white or water.
For the lead will burn the fistula and heal it wonderfully. If the fistula heals by the cauterization and treatment mentioned, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to use the perforation of the nose and return the fistula to the nasal passage, as will be explained in its specific place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing fissures of the lip.
Fissures often occur in the lip, called sha`rah, especially in the lips of children; it occurs frequently. If you treat this fissure with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective, then heat a small, knife-like cautery of this form.
Its hollow should be like the edge of a knife, then apply it, heated quickly, into the fissure itself until the cauterization reaches the depth of the fissure, then treat it with kyrotos until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing fistulas occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling appears at the root of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the molars, then it suppurates and bursts, and from the running pus a fistula results, then if you treat it and the treatment is not effective, it is necessary to heat a cautery to the extent that it fits into the fistula.
Then insert it, heated, into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and end.  Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned of treatments until it heals, God willing.
If the matter ceases and it heals, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to examine the place and remove the decayed bone, according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On cauterizing loose molars and gums.
If the gums are relaxed due to moisture and the molars move, and you have treated them with medication but it has not worked, then place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery iron, whose image comes after this, after you have placed the tube on the tooth.
And quickly insert the hot cautery iron into it, and hold your hand for a short time until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then raise your hand, then repeat the cautery iron several times as you wish, then the patient fills his mouth with salt water.
And he holds it for an hour and then spits it out; for the loose tooth becomes firm, and the relaxed gum tightens, and the foul moisture dries.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is from cold, or if there are worms in it, and treatment with medication is ineffective, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fat, or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with fat, you take cow's fat and boil it in an iron spoon or in a shell, then you take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, then dip it in the boiled fat and quickly place it on the aching tooth, and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat it several times until the strength of the heat reaches the root of the tooth. And if you wish, you can dip wool or cotton in cold fat and place it on the aching tooth, and place the heated iron on top of it until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, you take a copper or iron tube, and it should be somewhat thick in its body, lest the heat of the fire reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the cautery iron which is depicted, and place it on the tooth itself, and hold your hand until the cautery iron cools. Do this several times.
Then the pain will go away, either that same day or the next day. After the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good fat and hold it for an hour, then spit it out. This is the image of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, and as much as possible. This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-two.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If the fistulas are caused by phlegm and cold humors and do not respond to ripening medications, and you desire rapid ripening, then heat a hollow cautery iron, which is depicted here, perforated at both ends.
So that the smoke exits at the other end during cauterization. Place it gently on the tumor itself, once or twice if necessary, until you reach the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, make the cautery iron proportional to the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in butter to it until what was burned by fire is removed. Then treat it with ointment and lint until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors prevail over the windpipe, especially if it is accompanied by coldness of temperament, then the patient should first be purged with laxative medicines, then cauterize with a cautery iron in the hollow of the throat at the base of the gullet in the lower part.
Be careful not to reach the gullet with the cautery and do not burn more than half of the skin. Then apply another cautery at the joint of the neck at the end of its last vertebra, and let the cautery iron be nail-shaped in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what has been mentioned until he is cured, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterizing lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, then apply two cauteries above the clavicles in the low, soft, empty places, and another cautery in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
And the cautery should be nail-shaped, in the manner previously described.  And if you wish, let your cauterization be punctate with the cautery called the point, the image of which has been previously shown in puncturing earache; and let the points number from thirty or thereabouts.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers. You may also make a cautery with three branches, in this manner, and hasten the cauterization with it, because you cauterize three places at once with it. This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
In cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated because of slippery humors, or if it does not remain in place when it is reduced from its dislocation so that this becomes a habit, then it is dislocated at the slightest movement as we have witnessed, then it is necessary to reduce the joint first. Then the patient lies on his back or on the healthy side, then the skin inside the armpit is raised upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then heat the cautery iron with two points, the image of which is this.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cautery is four cauterizations.  It may be cauterized with a cautery iron with three points, in which case the shape of the cauterization will then be six cauterizations, and the points will be on the thinness of the rose.
And this is the image of the cautery iron with three points.
And this number may be increased by one, making eight cauterizations. Then, the pounded balls with salt are applied to the cauterization, then the patient is kept quiet and does not move the limb for a time until it strengthens. And if the dislocation is upwards, and this is rarely the case.
Then cauterize above the shoulder, one good nail-shaped cauterization, or many cauterizations by puncturing; for the joint becomes firm, and the moisture departs, and the patient is healed.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If there occurs in the stomach coldness and much moisture, to the extent that it removes it from its temperament and there are many catarrhs to it, and it is treated with various treatments but they are not effective, then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back and extend his legs and hands, then cauterize with three cauterizations. One cauterization below the clavicle, the size of a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauterizations below on the sides of the one cauterization, so that the shape of the cauterizations is triangular, and further apart from each other lest they unite if they suppurate; and the depth of the cauterization should be two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and that should be the shape of the cauterizations.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach in this manner.
And the cautery with which you cauterize this single cauterization should be in the shape of a circle, in this manner.
And the stomach may be cauterized by puncturing for whomever is apprehensive of this cauterization; and that is that you mark points on the stomach to the extent that you wish with ink, then cauterize them with a punctate cautery, then treat it with the treatment that has preceded until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the cold cauterization of the liver.
If there arises from the liver pain due to coldness and dampness, or due to a subtle wind, so that it has departed from its natural temperament excessively, and the patient has been treated with what we mentioned in the division, and that has not availed, Then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, and three cauterizations should be marked with ink in this form and this exact amount on the liver, below the diaphragm, where the elbow of a person ends.
And after each cauterization, there should be a cauterization of the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization should be along the body, straight; and do not press down firmly with the cautery.
Let the amount of thickness of skin that is burned be about half, no more.  And let the patient stand on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him be lying down with his legs extended and his arms raised. And this is the image of the cautery.
And you may be able to cauterize these cauterizations with the knife-cautery if you have gentleness and skill in the craft, and be cautious lest you overdo the cauterization, burning all the skin, burning the abdomen and reaching the intestines, for the skin there is thin; so know this.
Chapter twenty-eight.
In cauterizing a swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know if that swelling is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will find heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be intense sharpness with the pain, and you see that the physicians have been unable to treat it, then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, then you should mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire; it is the cautery that resembles a needle, and this is its image. And cauterize it with one cauterization until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the peritoneum until all the matter comes out, then treat it with the treatment of abscesses until it heals.
And this type of cauterization should not be used except by one whose training in the art of medicine has been long and who has experienced these diseases repeatedly; then he may undertake such work, but leaving it is better in my opinion.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with the roots of rhubarb for a cold fistula in this manner: that you take one long, dry root of rhubarb, the longest you can find, and it should be about the thickness of a finger, then dip it in oil and heat it in the fire. Then cauterize with one cauterization between the juncture of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations slightly below the nape of the neck, inclined toward the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts between the third and fourth ribs.
And two cauterizations also between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backward, and another cauterization in the middle of the chest and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations from behind: one between the shoulders and two on the sides of the spine, lower than the cauterization which is between the shoulders.
And you should not deepen your hand with the cautery, but it should be a superficial cauterization of the skin.  Some of the ancients mentioned that some people used an iron cautery like a spatula; they would heat it and insert it between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
And it expels the matter, as we mentioned in the tumor of the liver. And in this method of cauterization there is great danger; either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula develops at the site which will not heal.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If you treat the disease of the spleen with the treatments mentioned in the section and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is done in three ways, all of which are correct. One is to cauterize it with three or four cauterizations aligned along the length of the spleen.
In the form of the liver cauterizations whose form we have described previously, and between each cauterization and the next should be the width of a finger or slightly more. The nature of the cautery should be as we described in cauterizing the liver, and do not deepen your hand in the cauterization. The patient's position is lying on his back.
And the other method is in the flank, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left flank ends, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left flank ends, and your lifting of the skin should be across the body so that the cauterizations fall along the length of the body.
Then you insert the two cautery irons very carefully until you pass them through the skin on the other side, then you remove the cautery irons, so that the cauterizations are four. And if you wish, you may cauterize with the other cautery iron with three points.
Then you treat the site of the cauterization after you leave it to drain pus for many days; this is more effective than all the previously mentioned treatments.
Chapter thirty-one.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in ascitic dropsy specifically. If you treat the dropsical patient with the types of treatment which we mentioned in the classification, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize him with four cauterizations around the navel and one cauterization on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, a cauterization on the spleen, and two cauterizations on his back between the vertebraeâ€”one opposite his chest and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then you leave the cauterization open to drain pus for a long time.
And do not neglect the patient after cauterization with what is necessary to combine the two benefits for him, so that healing will be hastened. The shape of the cautery iron with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as its image has preceded.  That with which the abdomen is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot in the depression between the little finger and the ring finger.
And place your hand with the cautery iron and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for the yellow water will ooze out, and the cautery iron is of this shape.
Then cauterize on the legs, two cauterizations on each leg, and the cauterization should be with the sharp end of the cautery iron, and the cauterization should be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below it towards the middle of the leg, and on each thigh, two cauterizations.
And leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, so that the water oozes out, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On cauterizing diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and moisture, so that it weakens the retaining and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and this has been treated with various treatments and it has not healed, and you see the patient able to tolerate cauterization and strong, then cauterize him with a large cauterization on his stomach. As was previously mentioned in cauterizing the stomach with the circular cautery iron and four cauterizations around the navel with a gentle, nail-like cautery iron, and a large cauterization on the cotton above the coccyx, or two cauterizations.
If you see the moisture abundant and the patient able to tolerate it, then cauterize him on the pubis and cauterize him on each flank, and perhaps add two small cauterizations on the stomach near the large one, for this is a successful treatment whose benefit does not fail, God willing.
Chapter thirty-four.
In cauterizing the hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are chronic, numerous, or single hemorrhoids in the anus, and they are from thick, cold humors or corrupt moistures, and they were treated with what we mentioned in the classification but the treatment did not work, then cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the sacrum, slightly below the table, in a triangular shape.
And a cauterization below the navel by the length of two fingers. And if you estimate that his stomach has become cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cauterization as mentioned before and a cauterization on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery.  Leave the cautery open for a while, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the same cut wart and deepen your hand until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with suitable ointments until they heal.  And if you cauterize a large area with cotton wool, it will be more effective.
Chapter thirty-six.
On cauterizing fistulas which are in the anus and its surroundings.
If the patient does not consent to the incision and the procedure we described in its place and shrinks from that, then perhaps he will heal by cauterization. If a fistula occurs, and pus and foul moisture flow from it for a long time, then the first thing you should do is to measure it with a thin probe.
Then ascertain its depth with the probe, then heat the cautery iron which resembles a needle, then insert it, hot, into the fistula itself, along the course of the fistula to the depth the probe reached, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt tissues are burned, once or twice or three times according to your need.
And be careful not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a large vein, and if the fistula opens into the body of the bladder or into the body of the intestine.
Guard against all these places.  You should only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy location and you have estimated that it is not penetrating. Then treat the area until it heals. If the area closes and the flow ceases and remains so for a time, know that it has completely healed.
And if the flow does not cease, know that it is penetrating, or that there is a decayed bone in its depth, or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cauterizing the kidney.
If pain occurs in the kidney from cold or a strong wind, and the patient's sexual function is thereby diminished, then it is necessary to cauterize it over the loins, on the kidney itself, a cauterization on each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery previously mentioned.  Perhaps you may cauterize it a third time in the same place, so that three cauterizations are made in a row; this will be more effective.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder from cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize it with one cauterization below the navel on the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and one cauterization to the right of the navel and another to the left.
The cauterization on each side should be the size of a thumb joint, and cauterize one or two applications on the lower back if that is needed.  The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On cauterizing the womb.
If a substance from cold and dampness occurs in the womb, and it is thereby prevented from conception, and its temperament is spoiled, and its courses are difficult, or pain occurs to it upon their arrival, then it should be cauterized with three cauterizations around the navel, as we mentioned in cauterizing the bladder. One or two cauterizations on cotton on the lower back, and the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Erroneous humors may descend to the hip joint, and be the cause of its dislocation.  Its sign is that the leg is longer than the other when they are measured against each other, and you will find emptiness in the place of the dislocation. Then it is necessary to cauterize the patient on the hip joint itself with a semi-circular cauterization.
After you have drawn a circle with ink around the joint, so that the joint itself falls in the middle of the circle. The cautery whose image is presented in the cauterization of the stomach; and if you do not have this cautery, then cauterize with three cauterizations using the large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization deep enough to reach the entire thickness of the skin, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter forty-one.
On cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint and its cause is cold and moisture, and you have treated the patient with what we mentioned in the classification, and the treatment has not been effective, and the condition has become chronic, then it is necessary to purge the patient of thick humors with the fetid grain or prickly pear fruit, or something similar.
Then shave it and apply cautery in two ways: either cautery with burning medications or cautery with fire. Cautery with fire can be done in many ways; one of them is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
Make them of a suitable depth, and let the distance between each cauterization be the width of a finger. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped.  You may also cauterize one in the middle, on the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations. Or if you wish, cauterize it with the circle previously mentioned in cauterizing a dislocated hip, as a single cauterization.
So that the circle surrounds the entire hip and burns the entire thickness of the skin. This is the method of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two areas on the thigh at the point where the patient indicates pain, and one above the heel, four finger-widths towards the outer side, slightly. The cautery iron should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with a pointed cautery iron, three or four times, or more if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
In all your cauterizations, take care not to burn a nerve or a large artery, lest you cause the patient a serious or lasting injury.
I have seen one and another who were cauterized above the heel, and the cauterization was excessive, so that the leg became gangrenous, the gangrene reached the foot, and it became completely perforated, and the whole leg became corrupted, and then diarrhea and death followed.  If the pain is in both sides, cauterize both in this same manner.
Some learned physicians have mentioned a cauterization of the hip of this sort:  A cup-like instrument is made of iron, its diameter half a span, and its mouth the thickness of a date pit, or a little less; and inside this cup is another cup, and a third.
The distance between each cup is the width of a thumb joint; the cups are open on both sides; their height is about one or two finger joints; and a handle of firmly attached iron is made for them.  This is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, then it is placed on the hip joint, the patient leaning on the healthy side; three round cauterizations are made at once, then it is left for three days, and dressed with butter, and the wound is left open for some days.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The author of this book says: We have seldom used this type of cauterization because of its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, and because of the few who can endure it; however, it is a good cauterization for those who endure it and for whom its application is appropriate.
As for cauterization with burning drugs, two cup-like rings are made, similar to those through which the door hinges descend, of copper or iron.
Their rims have a height of about two finger joints or so; the distance between them is the thickness of a finger; they are open at the bottom and open at the top; and some are joined to others in this manner.
Then it is lowered onto the hip joint, the patient lying on his healthy side, and your hand is firmly held; then, between the circles, pour some sharp water, slightly warmed by the fire, and hold it for the duration of an hourglass; and let the patient endure its stinging and burning, for he will feel a burning like fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then raise your hand with the instrument, and wipe the sharp water from the entire hip with fresh water; then leave it for three days, applying butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears, and leave it for days so that pus may flow from it, for this is more effective.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it that retains water, in the same manner as was done for the hip.  This is the description of the sharp water, which I have already mentioned in the chapter on the preparation of medicines.
Take one part of potash and one part of unslaked lime, crush them, and put them in a new pot which has a small hole in its bottom, large enough for a probe to enter; and place another glazed pot under the bottom of the pot.
And pour fresh water over the potash and lime to cover them by a finger's breadth, after having pressed them firmly with your hand; and leave the pot until the sharp water descends into the glazed pot below; then collect all that water and pour it over more fresh lime and potash.
For then it will be extremely sharp, useful in many medical procedures and in cauterizing various parts of the body, because it acts exactly like fire.  There are other medicines that also cauterize the hip, such as thapsia, honey with mustard seeds, and lime with soap, well mixed.
Galen recounts a treatment for hip pain and sciatica from an ancient man, and greatly praises it, claiming that no other treatment is needed and that it cures in a single application.
So much so that a man might enter the bath carried and leave cured. This is done by taking fresh green rue, or if fresh is unavailable, recently dried rue, finely grinding it with a little fat, and applying it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg, or to the thigh.
Then it is bandaged and left for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside. Then he enters the bath, and when his body is damp he is immersed in the bath. The pain will cease and he will be healed, God willing. If it is not healed, repeat the application after ten days.
Then, God willing, he will be healed. Know that cauterization with these medicines should not be used unless the body has been purged. Dioscorides mentioned that goat hair, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
Cauterization is performed as follows: wool is soaked in old oil and placed on the deep area between the thumb and forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take a dry goat hair.
Ignite it with fire until it becomes an ember, then place it on the wool and leave it until it extinguishes. Then take another and continue doing so until sensation reaches from the middle of the upper arm to the hip and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called goat-hair cauterization.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning the cauterization of back pain.
Pain in the back may occur from many causes, either from a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like.  It is caused by the effusion of a cold, moist substance, and cauterization is only performed in this type alone.
That which is caused by the effusion of a cold substance, it is necessary, after the patient has vomited with hellebore and the like, that he be cauterized on his back where the pain is, three rows across the width of the table itself, after marking the location with ink. In each row, five cauterizations or more, according to the patient's tolerance and strength.
The cauterization should be done with a pointed cautery; and if you wish, cauterize it three or four times with a medium-sized nail-shaped cautery, in this manner.
Chapter forty-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a hump.
This ailment often affects young children, and its initial sign in a child is shortness of breath when standing and moving, and you will find at the end of his dorsal vertebrae a bead that has protruded, standing out from the other beads.
If you see that and want to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery of this shape. Let the cautery take hold on each side of the vertebra evenly; and if you wish, cauterize it around the vertebra with a pointed cautery in two or three rows, and let the points be close to one another. Then treat the area until it heals, as we have mentioned.
And be careful not to use cautery in a hump that is due to a nerve spasm.
Chapter forty-four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are due to cold humors that settle in any member of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, then it is customary for physicians to call that gout specifically.
If gout whose cause is cold is treated with the types of treatment we mentioned in the division and the pain does not go away, then cauterization will remove it.  This is done by cauterizing around the joints of the legs with many cauterizations after evacuation. The cautery should be olive-shaped and medium-sized, in this manner.
And if you need to cauterize the surface of the leg, do so with a pointed cautery. If the pain ascends to the knees or to the other jointsâ€”and this often happensâ€”then cauterize each knee three or four times on each side with this same olive-shaped cautery.
If you need more cauterization than this, cauterize, but do not make your cauterization deep, but rather only to the thickness of the skin. If the pain ascends to the hips or to the back, then use what we mentioned concerning cauterization in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, cauterize around the forearms as if circling two rows. If the pain remains in the fingers, cauterize each knuckle with a point and the metacarpus. If the pain ascends after some days to the elbows or to the shoulders,
cauterize them on each side.  The patient should not be without good management and the taking of medicines, for if he improves his diet and evacuates phlegm, he will be cured with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter forty-five.
On the cauterization of hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some intestine and fat descends to the testicle, and this is recent and incipient, then you should order the patient to abstain from food that day and to use laxatives to evacuate the bowels.
Then he should lie on his back before you, and you should order him to strain himself until the fat or intestine protrudes, then you should return it with your finger. Then mark with ink below the hernia on the pubic bone a mark resembling a semicircle, its extremities toward the upper part of the body.
Then heat a cautery of this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or fat to the abdomen. Then have an assistant place his hand on the area lest the intestine protrude.  You will have separated the patient's legs and placed a pillow beneath, and another assistant will bind his legs.
And another on his chest holding his hands. Then lower the cautery onto the same mark, your hand holding the cautery upright and steady, and hold it until it reaches the bone, or repeat it again if it does not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And take care to prevent the intestine from protruding while you cauterize, lest you burn it, which would cause the patient either death or a great calamity. And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed. And the cautery should be gentle for children according to their size, and for adults according to their size.
Then treat the cauterized area after three days with butter until the scab from the fire is gone. Then treat it with other ointments until it heals. And let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound closes.
And you should make his diet during his treatment such that it softens his bowels, lest the intestine protrude during straining and defecation. Then, if he wants to get up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days.  And let him reduce exertion, overeating and drinking, and loud shouting.
For if this treatment is used in this way, he will heal completely, God willing. And I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its chapter, God willing.  But as for a hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is recent, if you wish to prevent it from increasing, cauterize a circular area around it. Let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds of it. Then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing. And some of the ancients mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you mark the hernia with ink, making the horizontal line at the top of the hernia and the other line downwards, and you place a single nail-shaped cautery in the middle. The first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If pain occurs in some member from a fall or blow, and that pain persists for a long time, and treatment with medicine does not benefit it, then it should be cauterized with a point cautery.  The cauterization of each member should be according to its size, whether small or large, and according to its weakness or strength.
And if the pain and fistula persist, then if it heals from the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization upon it, for it is the nature of these pains to move from one member to what is near it. Therefore, one should follow them with cauterization until the patient is cured.
Chapter forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that is caused by the corruption of phlegm and black bile.
If you intend to cauterize it, look: if the leprosy is beginning, and you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification, and it has not diminished nor stopped, and you fear for the patient that the corruption will take over his entire constitution, then cauterize him on the head with five cauterizations. One in the usual middle of the head, the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, and two on the temples, and one from behind on the nape of the neck.  And prolong your hand with the cautery a little until it affects the bone slightly.
And scales will be removed from it, so that the thick vapors may easily escape from it.  And cauterize it also with a cauterization on the spleen itself, as mentioned before. But if the leprosy has persisted on the patient and appeared clearly, then it is necessary to cauterize these cauterizations which we mentioned on the head. And a cauterization on the tip of the nose, and two cauterizations on the cheeks, and two cauterizations on the vertebrae of the neck, and six on the vertebrae of the back, and one large one on the coccyx at the end of the spine, and another above it in the same place, and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee, and two on the shoulders, and two on the elbows, and two on the breasts, and cauterize on each joint of the joints of his fingers and toes with a cauterization, and on each heel of his feet and forearms.
And be careful of the nerve that is on the back of the heels, lest you burn them.  And a cauterization may be done on the pubic bone, and another on the stomach, and another on the liver. And know that the more cauterizations you do, the more beneficial and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel the pain of the cautery as the healthy person does, because his body is numb. And the cauteries should be large and small according to the organs and joints, as mentioned before in the descriptions of the cauteries.
Then treat the cauterization with barley flour mixed with honey and other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness: When a limb becomes numb and is treated with medications, ointments, and bandages, but does not heal, then cauterize the numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb, whether large or small. Let your cauterization be deep, penetrating slightly into the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  And some numbness that affects the hand and foot in the vertebrae of the back, at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb, may be cauterized, and the numbness will go away.
And let none undertake this except one who is knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of vitiligo.
If vitiligo is longstanding and no medical treatment has been effective, cauterize it slightly, to the depth of the skin's thickness, so that the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil,  plantain leaves, and pigeon or turtledove blood; one part of each. Mix everything together, apply it to a cloth, and bind it to the area until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is incipient and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it spreads. Some wise men have mentioned that a strong cauterization should be done in its center, but I do not agree with this, because I expect it to ulcerate, and I have witnessed this many times.
The correct method is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of the fistula.
If a fistula occurs and is slow to mature, either because of the causative factor, or because of the patient's age if he is old and anemic, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the fistula, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations, sparsely.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.  If you wish to stop it with cauterization, heat the cautery iron, the picture of which is this.
And insert it into the middle of the fistula until it penetrates the skin. Let the cauterization be from what is below, so that the pus may flow easily. Then treat it as necessary until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing the ulcer.
An ulcer is a corruption that spreads in the member, eating it as fire eats dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, heat many small and large, nail-like cauteries according to what is suitable for that place where the ulcer is.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing remains at all. Then leave it for three days and apply to the cauterized areas powdered sulfur with oil until all the eschar and all the corruption are removed.
Then treat it with ointments that promote the growth of flesh. If after three days you see that the flesh grows healthy without corruption, that is good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization on what remains of the corrupted areas.  The ulcer may also be treated with a caustic medicine.
For it acts in place of fire, more quickly beneficial. And I have mentioned its treatment with caustic medicine in the section; take it from there when you need it.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the cauterization of inverted and non-inverted nails.
This condition frequently occurs on the soles of the feet; it is a rough thing that pains the person.  Cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a caustic liquid.
As for cauterization with fire, you heat a hollow cautery iron, similar to an eagle's feather, made of iron to the size that surrounds the nail on all sides, and it should have a thin edge; then you apply it hot to the nail, and then you rotate your hand with the cautery iron around the nail until the cautery iron reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it for three days, during which it will suppurate, then you dress it with pounded wild lettuce mixed with salt, and leave the dressing on it for a night; then it will separate from its roots, and then you treat the wound site with a flesh-growing ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
This frequently occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet.  You should take a tube of copper, iron, or eagle's feather and place it over the nail or wart, then pour a small amount of caustic liquid into the tube.
Hold your hand as you rotate it, gently moving your hand to allow the edge of the tube to affect the root of the nail, and the caustic liquid will find its way to penetrate to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging of the caustic liquid for a short time.
Then leave it, for the nail will separate from its roots.  Do this one by one until you have treated all of them on the body. Then treat the areas after they have separated with ointments that grow flesh.
Chapter fifty-four.
On cauterizing the  falling sickness.
If someone suffering from falling sickness experiences a cold in the nerves or a quartan fever or something similar, then it is necessary to cauterize him four or five times on the vertebrae of the back, with one cauterization between each vertebra, and one cauterization on his chest. And one cauterization on his stomach with the olive cautery, for the falling sickness is calmed and the cold illness is hastened to ripen.
Chapter fifty-five.
On cauterizing pustules occurring in the body.
Ugly pustules may erupt in the body, arising from cold, thick, corrupt materials.  At their first appearance, it is necessary to cauterize each pustule gently with a cautery stick whose end has been lit with fire, or with a long root of ziraund, or with a lenticular cautery.
And boils may be cauterized at their first eruption in this manner, so that the active principle causing them does not increase, and the patient recovers from them. However, this should be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter fifty-six.
On cauterizing bleeding that occurs when an artery is cut.
Frequently, bleeding occurs from an artery that has been severed during a wound inflicted from the outside, or during the incision of a tumor, or the cauterization of a member, and so forth, making it difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery and press it firmly to confine the blood beneath your finger so that nothing escapes. Then place several small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire and heat them until they are very hot.
Then take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the place where the artery has burst, and apply the cautery to the same vein after quickly removing your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If, when you lift your finger from the mouth of the artery, the bleeding resumes and the cautery is extinguished, quickly take another cautery from those in the prepared fire, and continue to do this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve that is there, lest another affliction befall the patient. And know that if blood flows from an artery, it cannot be stopped, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said; or by amputation, if it has not already been amputated; for if it is amputated, its ends contract and the bleeding stops; or it may be tightly bound with threads; or medicines that stop blood flow may be applied to it, and it may be firmly compressed with compresses.
But whoever attempts to stop it with a bandage, or by compression with cloths, or by applying cauterizing agents and the like, will not succeed in stopping it at all, except rarely. If this happens to someone and no physician or medicine is available, he should quickly place his index finger on the mouth of the wound itself, as we have described.
He should press it very hard until the blood is constricted and flows from above the wound and onto the artery. The finger should not be removed; constantly apply very cold water to it until the blood coagulates, thickens and stops. During this time, you should consider what cauterization or medicine is needed.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, punctures, bloodletting, wounds, and the like.
Khalaf said: In the first chapter we mentioned every disease suitable for cauterization with fire and caustic medicine, its causes, its reasons, its instruments, the types of cautery irons, and we made that into chapters from the head to the foot.
And I follow in this chapter the same method, to make it easier for the student to obtain what he seeks.
Before I begin with that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more subtleties than the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, caution should be more stringent, because in the procedures of this chapter, there is often a risk of losing blood essential to life when opening a vein or incising a tumor, lancing an abscess, treating a wound, removing an arrow, incising a stone, and the like, all of which are accompanied by danger and fear, and often result in death.
And I advise you against undertaking what is doubtful, for in this craft various kinds of people will come to you with various kinds of illnesses; some of them are so weary of their illness that death is indifferent to them.
