Because of the severity of their illness and the length of their suffering, and because of the established signs of death in their illness, and some of them give you their money and enrich you with it in hope of health, and their illness is fatal, you should not help at all anyone who comes to you who is in this condition.
Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness, and do not proceed with any of this except after certain knowledge that assures you of a favorable outcome.  And in all the treatment of your patients, use foreknowledge and warning about what leads to recovery.
For in that you have help in gaining praise, glory, mention, and thanks. May God inspire you, my sons, with His guidance and not deprive you of correctness and success, for that is in His hand; there is no god but He.
And I have arranged the chapters of this section as in the preceding section on cautery, from head to foot, so that what you want from it may be easier for you, God willing.
Chapter One.
In the treatment of the water which collects in the heads of children.
This ailment frequently occurs in infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head without gentleness, and it may also occur from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than infants, and all those I have seen among them have quickly died; therefore, I considered leaving treatment, and I have seen an infant whose head was full of water and whose head grew larger every day. until the infant could not sit up because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
And this moisture either gathers between the skin and the bone, or gathers under the bone on the dura mater.  The procedure in this case, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the swelling is small, is that one should make an incision in the middle of the head, transversely, the length of the incision being about two fingerbreadths, until the moisture flows out. This is an image of the scalpel.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling is larger, then make two intersecting incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the boneâ€”and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side, and the water subsides if you press it with your hand inwards, and this is not hidden from youâ€”then you should make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture will come out, then tighten the incisions with cloths and bandages.  Then dress it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day. Then untie the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments. Do not leave off binding the head moderately, and nourish the patient with every dry food, low in moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals. Another description of the incision is that you look where the bone of the swelling and the gathering of water appear, for it may be in the back of the head more, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left.
Then aim the incision where the swelling and the fullness of water appear to you, and incise it as much as you can, and be careful not to cut an artery, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding, along with the evacuation of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the "al-á¸¥asÄ«sayn".
When someone is afflicted with acute attacks in the eyes or chest, and this persists and the treatment of medicines does not work, then the best treatment for that is to cut these two arteries.
Then, if you wish to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery, then tighten the patient's neck with the excess of his garment, then look where the pulse beats and their locations, the two low-lying places behind the ears, and they rarely hide except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then cut them with a lancet, a clean cut to the bone; this should be done across the head.  And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and cut upwards, and the length of the cut should be about two joined fingers.
If the artery is cut, the blood will gush out in a pulsating manner, leaping forward in successive throbs. If the artery is not palpable, you should measure three finger-breadths from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise to the bone. What should be...
...sent from the blood six ounces at once, and perhaps more or less is sent, all according to the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries that appears to you. Then look at the wound; if something of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it away.
Lest a hot swelling occur, then bind the wound with a thread of linen rags, then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In the ligation of the two arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute attacks from acrid humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headache and the like, and he has been treated with various medical treatments without success, then we have tried in these diseases the ligation or cauterization of the arteries of the temples as we have described.
And the method of ligating them is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the artery appearing in the temple should be sought, for it will be evident to you from its pulsation, and it rarely hides except in a few people.
Or in severe cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with the excess of his garment, then you rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly and obviously. Then take the scalpel, whose image is this. Then you incise the skin gently with it until you reach the artery, then you throw a hook into it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes under it from every side; and if the artery is thin, then twist it with the end of the hook. Then cut off a portion of it to the extent that its ends are separated and constricted, and do not cause bleeding; for if it is not severed and cut off, the blood will not flow at all. Then it is evacuated of blood from six ounces to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with strong doubled thread; and let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings, lest decay quickly reach it before the wound heals, and bleeding occurs.
Then cut what is between the ligatures; do this at that time or after another time. And if you wish, cauterize it with a knife-like cautery to the bone until its ends are severed; this performs the same function or better, as we said.
Unless the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, for cauterization is among the things that help to consume humors, and it is more certain in benefit.  After ligating the arteries, it is necessary that the place be packed with worn cotton and that firm bandages be applied. After healing, it is treated with dry medicines which generate flesh, and with bandaging, until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your work, quickly cut it, either by cauterization or by filling the place with alum and tightening your hand until the blood stops. If you do not have any of that, place your finger there until the blood clots.
Then wash the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and bind it appropriately.  What is easier and simpler than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery, after marking the two places with ink.
And lower it, very hot, until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that its ends are separated, for it will not reunite with this cauterization at all.  This is an image of the cautery.
The blades should be sharp, resembling small awls, except that they should be much less sharp than a knife, because if they are as sharp as a knife, they cool more quickly and the flesh will not be cut quickly. If they have some thickness, the heat of the fire will be retained in them.
And the flesh is cut quickly. This procedure is better than any other, and lighter and easier.  Let the distance between the blades be the width of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of the constant flow of hot tears into the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the outside of the skull and nothing from medical treatment with drugs has benefited, and you see the patient's face has reddened. And he feels a crawling on his forehead like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are weak and moist, their eyelashes are eroded and his eyelids are drawn together from the severity of the tears.  Therefore, the ancients in treating it resorted to this procedure, and they call it treatment with the sword, which is that you order the patient to have the hair shaved from his forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions in the forehead along the length of the forehead. The length of each incision should be about two fingers, one incision parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second a little distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws.  The distance between each incision should be about three joined fingers, and have with you prepared pieces of sponge or many dry cloths to wipe away the blood.
Then insert the double-edged scalpel, whose image is this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and with it you flay all the skin between the two incisions together with the membrane on the bone. Then you do the same also from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove this scalpel. And also insert into the first incision another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth, not sharp, on the other; and this is its image:
And place its sharp side upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle incision, and cut with it all the vessels which descend from the head to the eyes, without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do this in the middle incision to the other incision.  After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, squeeze the areas of clotted blood. Then place in each incision a cotton wick, and place over it a dressing moistened with a syrup and oil, or vinegar and oil.
Lest a hot swelling occur. On the third day, untie the bandage and use much washing with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil, and whatever else you treat wounds with, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When someone experiences frequent, sharp, severe, persistent discharges, and you see his eyes weak, small, and his vision weakened, and his eyelids ulcerated, and the eyelashes falling out. And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is this: order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is this: order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or slightly higher horizontally, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple. The incision should reach the bone, and keep your hand away from the temporal muscles that move during chewing, until the bone is exposed and all the blood is dried with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or a linen thread, then bandage from above and soak the bandages with syrup and oil, lest a hot swelling occur. When you remove them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins.
Then treat it with a drying preparation that produces flesh, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, preparing an ointment from them and using it for flesh growth in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a semi-stone like iron and glass; or a vegetable seed like chickpeas and kernels and the like; or a flowing thing like water and vinegar and the like.
Or an animal. When a stone or a kind of stone falls into the ear which does not grow in the ear, then expose the ear to the sun. If you see the stone, then instill some violet oil or cerate into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with a sternutatory. And plug the nostrils when sneezing comes, after you place a ring of rags or wool around the ear and extend the ear upwards. Often it comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out otherwise, then try to remove it with a gentle probe, the image of which is this.
If it comes out with the probe, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved, blind hook. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the hole of the ear gently.
And seal what is around the tube with pitch softened with oil, lest there be a way for the wind other than the tube, then draw it with your breath strongly, and often it comes out with that. If it does not come out with what we described, then take a little of the gum of Nabataea or of the prepared gum with which birds are caught, and put it on the tip of the probe.
After wrapping tightly a piece of cotton around it, then insert it into the hole of the ear gently after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out with all that we described, then proceed to incision before hot inflammation or spasm occurs.
The description of the incision is that you first bleed the patient in the cephalic vein and remove blood from him according to his strength. Then you seat the patient before you and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its lobe in its lowest part.
And the incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Then quickly stitch the incision and treat it until it heals.
But if the thing that has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, try to remove it as mentioned. If it does not respond to removal, then take a thin, delicate scalpel of this shape.
And try with it to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear.  You only do this if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the vapor of the ear until it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a blind probe or a soft sponge or by suction.
As we mentioned, then it is easy to remove it. As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with hellebore, having filled his ear with some old cotton while lying on the ear containing the water. If it comes out with that,
otherwise, take many small, smooth pebbles the length of the fingers, warm them slightly by fire, and let the patient insert one into the hole of his ear. He should press down on his foot on that side and strike the stone in the ear with another stone.
He continues to do this with one pebble after another until all the water comes out.  The water may also come out by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and applying fire to the other end until most of it burns, then repeating with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or draw it out with a tube as was described for the pebble. As for removing an animal that has entered, see if it is small in body like a flea and so on, treat it as described in the section, and as for...
if its body is large enough to be felt, try to remove it with forceps and hooks. This is easier than anything else that gets into the ear. As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if you treat them as mentioned in the section on instillations and your treatment is not effective,
you should look into the ear in the sun. If you see anything of the worms, remove them with forceps or fine hooks. If you do not see anything, take a tube of this shape.
narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate. Then suck strongly, doing this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If they do not come out, seal around the tube with wax as I described for the pebble.
If they do not come out by what we have mentioned, use the instillations I have mentioned, which the ancients have tried in killing worms. You will find that in the section on instillations.  Pouring oils and medicines into the ear is done with this instrument, and this is its shape:
As you see, make it from silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top. If you wish, the nozzle inside the tube can be made of tightly fitting copper. If you wish, you can take a reed and wrap cotton tightly around one end.
Then pour the oil, juice, or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear. Then insert the reed with cotton from above and squeeze it gently with your hand until the oil is forced into the ear and felt.
The patient will feel it entering.  Whatever you pour into the ear should be warmed slightly over a fire. Be careful that what is dropped into it is neither too cold nor too hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of the accidental blockage of the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear openings not perforated.
Also, some people may experience a blockage in the ear canal from a wound or from newly grown flesh. This blockage may be deep within the ear opening, unseen by the eye.
It may also appear slightly outside the opening and be visible. That which appears deep within the ear and is not visible is, in most cases, difficult to cure.  That which is visible should have the patient's ear exposed to the sun. And look into it; if you see the blockage, open it with a delicate scalpel, which is of this form:
Its tip should be slightly broad at the end, while the rest of the scalpel is smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the blockage is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently but firmly until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep in the ear, take a smooth, delicate probe and warm it slightly in the fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the blockage and the patient experiences a feeling of heaviness in hearing, remove it gently.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum. Then insert into the ear a wick to fit the size of the opening, having smeared it with Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are sure the wound has healed. Or take a wick, moisten it in water, and sprinkle powdered vitriol on it.
Use it. If you see that the ear has become swollen with a hot inflammation, you should remove the wick and replace it with another wick that has been smeared with a preparation made with rose oil to calm the hot inflammation, then continue the treatment until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments mentioned in the chapter on styptics to stop the bleeding.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts appearing on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  They should be grasped with forceps or a hook and cut off at their base with a scalpel. Then apply powdered verdigris to the area.
If it bleeds, cauterize it with a mild lentil-shaped cautery. Cauterization is best after cutting.  It often returns if any of the roots remain, but if cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and it does not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of cold sores appearing on the eyelids.
Something resembling a cold sore in its intensity and hardness often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore it is called a cold sore, which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The treatment is to examine it; if the cold sore is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves to every direction, then it is easy to treat, so make a transverse incision on it.
Then gently peel it from every side until it is freed, then suspend it with a hook and cut it. If you cannot cut it except after penetrating the eyelid with the cut, this will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, then close it with sutures and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, then there is no harm from it, for the ointment will heal and join it. If the cold sore is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then evert the eyelid and suspend the cold sore with a hook without needing an incision, and excise it from every side.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the cut, that will not harm. Then wash the eye after cutting the cold sore with salt water and treat the area with something that will join it until the patient heals.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of the stye which appears in the eyelid.
A stye is a fleshy growth that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid. This mostly occurs in children, and it weighs down their eyes, causing them to experience colds at night and prevents them from looking at sunlight. Because the tears flow quickly to them.
Therefore, you see them always leaning on their faces, and on them they sleep, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, swollen, and the stye is clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the stye between them. The procedure is to have the patient place his head in your lap.
Then you take a linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick to the extent that it surrounds the stye from every side, then place it on it and press with your fingers from every side to gather the stye in the middle of the circle.
Then you make a transverse incision in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, and the incision should not be larger than the incision made in bloodletting. As for the depth, you should cut all the skin until you reach the stye.
Then, in most cases, the stye protrudes from the incision in the form of a white piece of fat, so you pull it out with a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers, while turning your hand right and left until it heals. Beware of increasing the incision lest the lancet reaches the eye and harms it.
If the stye does not appear to you at the first incision, you should gently increase the incision a little until the stye protrudes, then pull it out as we said. Then dip a cloth in vinegar and water, place it on the area, and bind it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
And they place it inside the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture. Then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with emollient compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of eyelid inflammation.
If superfluous eyelashes grow on the eyelid, outside the natural course beneath the natural eyelashes and brows, they harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, whiteness, and thickening, even to the point of causing blindness.
Eyelid inflammation is of four types: either cauterization with fire, or with a caustic medicine as previously discussed in the chapter on cauterization, or the inflammation may be treated by excision and suturing, or with a reed, as I shall describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand; if it does not turn, then insert a needle threaded with silk from beneath the eyelid, passing the needle and thread from above, close to the hair itself, and draw the eyelid upwards with the thread.
Turn it over with a probe, then incise the inner eyelid below the superfluous hairs with a sharp scalpel, from the larger canthus to the smaller, then remove the thread and place a small pledget of cotton or linen under the eyelid, and mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of a laurel leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to raise the eyelid, because people vary in this; some may need a portion of the eyelid to be excised, according to the degree of relaxation of the eyelid.
And some need less to be cut; all this is according to the relaxation of the eyelid.  Then incise with the scalpel along the two lines that you have marked, beginning from the larger canthus to the smaller, and let each incision be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a probe.
Then insert the hook into one corner of the skin and strip it all off, then gather the edges with stitches using a fine woolen needle and thread, wipe away the blood, and stick the remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
If you wish to do that, otherwise it does not matter. Then leave the stitches and threads for about three or four days, then treat it. And if you wish, leave the wound without stitching and treat it with things that dry and constrict, for the eyelid will rise when the wound heals and comes together; stitching is better.
This method of blepharoplasty was mentioned by the ancients, although it is burdensome for the patient; it is a good procedure and has no danger. Another method of blepharoplasty is also to mark on the eyelid a shape like a laurel leaf, as we described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, either separated or grouped in this manner:
Then cut off the excess eyelid with small scissors, in this manner, in moderate pieces:
If you cannot hold the hooks and they do not straighten for you, then take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger canthus and a third thread near the smaller canthus, and gather the threads between your fingers moderately, then raise your hand with them moderately.
Raise the marked skin completely as we described, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If hot swelling appears when you cut or incise, then soothe that swelling with cyrrhotum or similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also have its margins inverted; you should use in this case what we mentioned of cutting, suturing, cauterization with fire, and with a caustic medicine; cauterization with fire or with a caustic medicine is more effective and gentler for the patient than cutting and suturing, and I make use of both in these cases.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this way: you invert the eyelid and make an incision on the inside as described, then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you cut at their ends on both sides where the threads are attached, then gently bring them together on what remains of the eyelid and tie the two reeds tightly on both sides and leave it for some days; then the tightened skin will die, turn black, and become necrotic.
Until it falls off by itself; if it is slow, cut it with scissors, then treat it until it heals. When it is healed, the eyelid will be raised and the eyelashes will not be inverted. This is an image of the two reeds: make them in this shape and this exact size in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On raising inverted eyelashes from the eye with a needle.
If there is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle and insert a smooth, thin silk thread into it, then bring the two ends together and tie them in a very delicate knot, and let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then attach another thin thread to the hook, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Then have the patient put his head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the work may be clear, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the hook into the root of the extra hair.
And quickly pass it through the eyelid until you bring it out above the natural hair, then pull the thread upwards towards the hook and insert into the hook that hair, whether it is one, two, or three, but no more.
Then pull with your hand until the hair comes out with the hook in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the hook and the hair does not come out with it, pull the hook downwards with the thread you attached to it until the hook comes out of the upper hole.
Then return the hair into it and pull them both. Do this until it rises in the upper hole and you see it clearly. Then remove the hook and the thread and take them out, and bind the eye and leave it bound for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a long hair from the natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after a while, then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the sty that occurs in the upper eyelid.
The condition to which this sty is exposed is called arnabiyya, and this sty is either natural or accidental. The accidental one is from the healing of a wound or a cut or cauterization and the like.
The method of treatment is to incise that adhesion and separate its lips, and place a linen wick between them and tie it until it heals.  It is not advisable to use things that dry and constrict in its treatment.
For if you do that, the sty will return more severely than it was. Rather, use things that relax, such as fenugreek and a poultice with water in which alkanet and flaxseed have been boiled, and a diachylon ointment melted with some fats.  Smear the wick with it and treat it with this.
The key to its treatment is to try by every means to prevent it from adhering in the way it was initially.  This treatment that we have mentioned is only some improvement of the sty, not a restoration of the area to its original state at all.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of a sty which occurs in the lower eyelid.
This sty which forms in the lower eyelid is what is truly called a sty, and it is both natural and accidental. The accidental kind results from a wound, incision, cautery, or the like.
The method of treatment is to take a needle with doubled thread, and to insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left canthus to the right canthus, so that the thread lies at both ends of the flesh. Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is proper, then that is it. Otherwise, take a probe and place it on the site of the incision, and manipulate the eyelid with it, and make two incisions in the inner aspect of the eyelid.  The ends of the two incisions should meet at the corners of the previous cut, so that they form an angle. Until, when they meet, their shape becomes similar to this shape:
This is the Greek letter lambda. Then remove that flesh to the extent that the sharp edge is below what is next to the eyelid. Then gather the separated parts with two stitches, sewing it with a wool thread, and then treat it with the previously mentioned emollient and astringent medications until it heals.
And if the fistula resulted from an incision, suture, or cauterization, then it is necessary to make a simple incision without the hairs of the eyelids, as mentioned before, then separate the lips with a tent, as we have mentioned.
In short, in the treatment of a fistula, whether it is above or below, you should proceed according to the form of the fistula, for it may vary greatly in appearance, and the skilled surgeon devises a method in whatever way is possible.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it.  The disease itself, in most cases, indicates the appropriate procedure and instrument.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur to many people, such that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing easy movement and hindering its natural functions.  It is then necessary to insert the point of a probe under the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not sharply like cutting scalpels, but rather somewhat dull, lest the patient be disturbed during the operation and the scalpel slip, injuring the eye with its sharpness.  Your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed. Then pour into the eye salt water to wash it, or instill a solution of vitriol in water, then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen thread.
And place on top of the eye wool soaked in egg white, and after the third day use emollient eye-salves until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the excision of pterygium and the fleshy excrescences of the conjunctiva.
Pterygium is of two kinds: either nervous, resembling a thin, hard membrane; or non-nervous, resembling a firm, white moisture; if touched with an iron instrument or seized with forceps, it is torn.
And a forceps has not been fixed in it. Both types begin from the larger caruncle, extending little by little until they cover the pupil and obstruct light and the movement of the eye.  The procedure for its excision is that the patient places his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, and you lift the eyelid with your hand; then you seize the pterygium with a slightly curved forceps and stretch it upwards; then you take a needle and insert into it a horsehair or oxhair and strong thread, and you bend the tip of the needle slightly and pierce it into the middle of the pterygium.
And you pass it through with the needle and tie the pterygium with the thread and stretch it upwards and dissect with the hair the side of the pterygium next to the pupil, as if you were spreading it with the hair to its end; then you cut the remainder at the root of the caruncle with a fine scalpel or small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the caruncle.
Lest there result from its excision a constant flow of tears.  The pterygium may be stretched with the forceps alone, or with the thread as we said, then the smooth scalpel dissects the end, the image of which is this:
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, lest it cause a wound and the pupil is injured. Then after the excision, throw into the eye some powdered salt or some vitriol and leave it until the next day; then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the pterygium is not fibrous and you are unable to insert a needle into it or fix it with forceps, this type of pterygium is often treated with medications, and it heals as described in the classification. If you wish to excise it, open the patient's eye.
And take a fine, smooth scalpel with a weak edge, of this shape: And dissect the pterygium from above gently with it. If you see that it is loosening and disintegrating and the dissection has affected it, then instill into the eye from your instrument vitriol or red vitriol or powdered salt and bind the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until all of it is gone, unless a hot inflammation interferes in the eye; then leave it and treat the hot inflammation until it heals, then repeat the procedure of dissection until it heals. As for the fleshy excrescence of the canthusâ€¦
If the excrescence injures the eye severely, then hook that excrescence with a forceps and cut some of it, but do not overdo the cutting lest it cause tearing, then instill into the canthus red vitriol or white vitriol until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the excision of the fleshy excrescence and what grows of excess flesh in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, a red fleshy growth may sprout, accumulating until it covers the pupil or approaches it or overflows onto the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids turn outwards and it resembles a rose flower.
The treatment is that the patient should place his head in your lap, then open his eyes, and you should pick up all that red flesh with suitable forceps.
Or grasp it with a hook or forceps, then cut the flesh, piece by piece, until you have removed all of it, being careful not to injure the eye during the procedure. Your cutting should be either with the scalpel we described for cutting the pterygium, or with small scissors. Like those used to pluck hairs, as their image will be shown in the chapter following this one.
When you have finished cutting and removed all the growth, fill the eye with crushed salt or instill some Armenian bole or similar caustic medicine, and apply a cotton compress soaked in egg white externally to prevent inflammation.
If any of the growth remains and bleeding is excessive, and you fear inflammation, leave the eye and treat it with remedies that reduce swelling, then resume treatment until it heals.  Do the same for excessive flesh of this type that appears in the eye.
However, you should avoid treating any eye weak by nature or circumstance with instruments, and do not undertake any of these procedures unless you are sure that the organ can tolerate the treatment.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the removal of foreign bodies from the eye.
Pterygia are red vessels that grow over the eye, preventing vision and weakening the eye over time. First, you should examine: if the eye with pterygia is strong and has no other disease besides the pterygia,
then the removal is performed as follows: you should have the patient place his head in your lap, then hook the vessels with one or two hooks, according to your skill. The hooks should be delicately curved, in this manner:.
Or there may be two double hooks in one body, in this manner:
Then, gently grasp those vessels with a delicate pair of scissors, and wipe away the blood from time to time until you see that the vessels have been removed from the eye and the bleeding has stopped.  Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors.  Let your work be done during midday, facing the sun.
And be very careful in your work so that you do not cut anything other than those vessels. When you have finished, instill green or red vitriol into the eye; its sharpness will destroy what remains of the pterygium. If you are unable to remove it all at that time, apply a bandage to the eye to soothe the pain.
Leave it for some days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is gone, then repeat the operation in exactly the same way until it is healed. This is an image of the appropriate scissors.
Leave it [and the patient's head should be in the student's lap, while another wipes away the tears and checks the front and back of the eye, and you]. The principle of the operation is to open the eye with fixed or triangular specula held by a student.
Sharp; and the patient's head should be in the student's lap, while another wipes away the tears and checks the front and back of the eye, and you have the choice of beginning to grasp from the top or the bottom of the eye. It is preferable to remove two strands in the upper part, one at the end of the iris and the other at its beginning.
Grasp it between the middle and index fingers, then hook the forceps underneath opposite them, then grasp it between the little and ring fingers, and lift your hand gently until you see that all the pterygium has been raised. Cut it from the smaller side if you wish with scissors, or if you wish with a delicate scalpel.
And insert the thread on a needle and raise all the pterygium until it is elevated on the conjunctiva, and do not cut the forceps, for the tears and blood will obscure your vision.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the cornea from above and below, so that nothing remains attached except to the cornea. If you can elevate it from the cornea by scraping with the thread, it is better, and this may be possible in some people.
And when you have cut it, let its site be cauterized for several days, until the pain subsides and the inflammation is healed, then resume treatment on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
On restoring the nose.
Physicians call a fistula a polypus. If you treat it with cautery or with the sharp, burning medicine previously described, and it does not heal, then there is no remedy except to incise the swelling when it is ripe and to extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed.  And when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument of this shape:.
The rasp is called a head; it is made of Indian iron, and its head is round like a button, finely engraved with the pattern of a file or rasp. You place it on the corrupted part of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers, pressing gently with your hand, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away.
Do this repeatedly, then dress the wound with drying, astringent medicines. If the wound closes and the flesh grows over it, and the discharge ceases, and forty days pass without recurrence, inflammation, or other complication,
know that it has healed. Otherwise, there is no remedy except to return the fistula to the nasal opening in this way:  You expose the bone again with an iron instrument or with a sharp medicine. When the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The tip of the instrument is triangular, and its shaft is conical, tapering towards the tip, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the ethmoid and slightly away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
He will feel air escaping from it when he holds his hand to his nose. Then dress the wound with the drying astringent medicines as I have instructed you. When the flesh grows and the place is firm, the matter that was discharging externally will return to the nose, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In returning the protrusion of the eye.
If the eye protrudes in its entirety, and no damage or deficiency occurs in the vision, then you should ease the patient, and then bleed him in the cephalic vein, then apply a cupping glass to the nape of the neck without restriction, and gently suck.
Then apply to the eye pledgets made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and gum tragacanth. Then bandage the eye from above with many soft bandages. The preparation of the bandages is to take many soft, thin pieces of linen, and fold each piece into four layers, making many of them.
Then place them one on top of the other as much as the eye needs, then bind them tightly with a firm cloth, and repeat cupping without restriction. Then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, then it should be tightened gently. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise, repeat the application of the pledgets, bandages, tightening, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the excision of the uvula.
If a hernia occurs in the uvular layer, then it protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is disfigured because of it, and you wish to excise it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It is that you insert a needle into the root of the uvula from below upwards, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the uvula and pass it through, and leave the first needle as it is.
Then you cut the place where the doubled thread bends and tie some of the uvula around the needle with it and pull it gently, then you remove the needle and place on the eye wool moistened with egg white and leave it until the threads fall off with the uvula, then you treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  And you may pull the uvula from every direction with a circle of cloth until the eye is further protruded gently, then you incise with a thin scalpel reaching to the depth of the eye so that the white moisture flows and the eye descends and withers in place, then you pull it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-two.
In the treatment of the uvula.
This ailment which is called kimana is a fluid which gathers in the eye resembling running water, but it is not. The method of treatment is that you seat the patient on a chair upright, then you take his head in your hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the fluid go to the lower part with your eye, then it is fixed and does not move, and the light departs and the patient sees things as he used to see them. And if it does not descend to the lower part, we know that it is water; if the descent of the fluid is not achieved by what we have mentioned.
Otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision with it above the cornea at the point of junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the ciliary region until the substance comes out.
When it comes out, instil into the eye hot water in which honey has been mixed, or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled, then treat with other treatments until he recovers.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already mentioned the types of water in the classification and which is suitable for cupping, in a detailed and explained statement, so take that from there correctly. Then you should seat the patient before you, sitting squarely facing the light near the sun.
And you bind his healthy eye and tie it tightly, then you lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye in which the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the cupping instrument near the cornea, with the thickness of an awl, in the same white of the eye on the side of the smaller canthus, then you push the cupping instrument forcefully while turning it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel with the cupping instrument that it has reached something empty.
And it should be that the extent to which the cupping instrument goes into depth is the extent of the distance which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, which is the cornea of the eye, for the copper is seen by the same observer with the naked eye due to the clarity of the corneal membrane, then you move the cupping instrument upwards to the place in which the water is.
Then you press it downwards time after time. If the water descends immediately, then the patient will see what his sight has been opened to immediately, with the cupping instrument in his eye, then it rests a little. If the water rises, then lower it again without removing the cupping instrument.
Then if it settles and does not rise, remove the cupping instrument gently while twisting it with your hand little by little, then dissolve in the water some clean Andarani salt and wash the eye with it from the inside, then apply to the outside of the whole eye a compress or wet wool.
With rose oil and egg white, and you bandage it with the healthy eye. As for us and the people of our time, we place crushed cumin with egg white on it. If the cupping instrument does not respond to entering the eye due to its hardness, because some people have very hard eyes, Then you should take the scalpel which is called the *barÄ«d*, whose image is this:
Then pierce with it only the adherent part and do not exaggerate in the piercing; rather, it is that you make a delicate opening for the cupping instrument, then insert the cupping instrument as we have mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm recumbent position in which he sleeps on his back in a dark room.
And he abstains from all movements and from coughing, and you make his food that which softens his constitution, and he does not move his head to the right or to the left at all, and the bandage remains in place until the third day, then you untie it in that dark room and test his sight.
And you show him things, then you return the bandage until the seventh day. And it is not appropriate to do this during treatment or after cupping at your hour, but rather you should avoid that before the water rises quickly with intense vision.
If a hot swelling appears, then it is necessary to untie the eye before the seventh [day] and treat that with what calms the swelling, until when it is calmed, then at that time you release his sight and place on his face a veil which trains his sight from under it for days while he is in that dark room, then...
he leaves the house gradually and manages his affairs. And know that in cupping, the learner cannot dispense with observation many times, then afterwards he proceeds to the work. And it has reached me from some Iraqis that he mentioned that he makes in Iraq a cupping instrument that is penetrating, with which he sucks out the water.
And I have not seen anyone in our country make that, nor have I read it in any book of the ancients.  And it is possible that this is recent. And this is an image of types of cupping instruments for those ignorant of them:
It is made especially of copper, and its tip is of this very thinness, triangular-tipped, sharp.
Chapter twenty-four.
In the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Various superfluous flesh may grow in the nose; some resembling a scorpion with many legs, some cancerous flesh, hardened and dark in color, and some soft flesh, not dark in color.
If any of this flesh is soft, not malignant nor cancerous, then you should have the patient sit before you facing the sun, open his nostril, and insert the snare into that flesh, then draw it outwards, then cut off what you have grasped with a delicate, sharp scalpel from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone; if any remains which you could not cut, then remove it gently with one of the delicate instruments, so that nothing remains.  If the bleeding overcomes you or a hot inflammation occurs,
then treat it as is appropriate. If it is one of the malignant tumors, then act quickly to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone. Then put vinegar and water or a potion in the nose after the cutting. If the nose opens and the moisture passes through to the throat,
know that he is healed. If the moisture does not pass through as it should, then know that inside there is flesh growing on the top of the porous bones which the instrument did not reach by cutting. Then you should take a linen thread of some thickness.
and tie many knots in it, making the space between each knot the width of a finger or less.  The patient should skillfully insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or whatever is possible after he has made it like a button, and draw his breath until it reaches the pharynx.
and it comes out at his throat. Children in school often do this, and it is easy for those who want to. Then bring together the two ends of the thread, the one which came out of the mouth and the other which remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to saw the flesh, doing this until you know that the flesh has been cut by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread and put in the nose, after wiping away the blood, a wick that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more.
until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh. Then, finally, put a lead tube in the nose for some days until it heals. If it needs drying treatment, use that.
This is an image of the instrument with which ointments and medicines are dropped into the nose:
It is made of silver or copper, similar to a small, open oil lamp, and its spout is likewise.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the spout of the instrument flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when you heat the ointment or whatever you wish of juices or flowing things in it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
Concerning warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Frequently a wart grows on the tip of the nose, and it increases and grows larger with the days until its appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and to excise it completely, then apply to the place either cautery or a burning medicine which serves as a substitute for cautery.  If you miss cutting it off until it grows large, then look: if it is hardened, solid, dark in color, and insensitive,
do not apply the iron to it, for it is a cancerous tumor, and I have often seen those who cut this tumor suffer a great calamity from it.  But if the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that it can be completely cut, then let the patient vomit and cut it off without hesitation or delay, and treat the place with that which dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is separated by a wound or something similar, know that when a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, the treatment rarely works except in some people.
So when something like that happens to someone, see if the wound is fresh with its blood; gather the edges of the wound with sutures, then treat it until it heals. And if the separation of the connection has separated its edges and each part is sound, then you should scrape each part to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then gather the edges with sutures and tighten them, and sprinkle on them mastic and frankincense, powdered.  Then place over the sutures a plaster of palm-tree ointment or another adhesive ointment, and leave it bound for two or three days, then unbind it.
And change the dressing and leave it until the threads separate by themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The method of suturing is to gather the separated connection either with needles, as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread, as I explained there.
Chapter twenty-seven.
On removing the knots which appear in the lips.
Many people develop small, hard tumors inside their lips; some resemble barleycorns, others are smaller or larger.  You should evert the lip and incise over each nodule and then remove it with a hook.
And cut it from each side, then pack the wound after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the area with something astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter twenty-eight.
On cutting away excess gum.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called epulis. You should hook it with a hook or grasp it with forceps and cut it at its base, and let the blood flow, then apply powdered alum to the area.
Or one of the astringent, drying powders. If that flesh returns after treatmentâ€”for it often doesâ€”cut it again and cauterize it; for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On extracting teeth with an instrument.
Rough, unsightly crusts may gather on the surfaces of the teeth, inside and out, and between the gums.  These may become black, yellow, or green, even causing damage to the gums and disfiguring the teeth.
You should seat the patient before you, his head in your lap, and clean the tooth or teeth showing crusts or anything resembling sand, until nothing remains.  Do the same with black, green, yellow, or anything else, until you have thoroughly cleaned them.
If this removes the material on the first cleaning, well and good. If not, repeat the cleaning on another day, and then again, until you achieve your goal. Know that a tooth may require different shaped and many kinds of instruments, depending on what is needed.
For the instrument used to clean a tooth from the inside is not the same as that used to clean it from the outside, nor that used to clean between the teeth, which is of yet another form.  These are several forms of instruments that you should have ready.
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat a toothache by every means, and delay extraction, for there is no replacement for a tooth once it is extracted, as it is a noble substance, except when extraction is unavoidable.
If the patient is determined to have it extracted, you should ascertain that the tooth is indeed the source of the pain. For often the patient is deceived by the pain and thinks it is in a sound tooth, and has it extracted, yet the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted; we have seen this repeatedly done by barbers.
If you are certain that the pain is in that particular tooth, then you should incise around the tooth with a strong scalpel, to loosen the gum on all sides, then move it gently with your fingers or with delicate forceps, little by little, until you loosen it.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps, holding the patient's head between your knees, having secured him so that he does not move, then pull the tooth straight out, so as not to break it; and if it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it beneath it from each side gently, and move it as you did before.
And if the tooth is decayed or carious, you should fill the cavity with a piece of cloth and pack it firmly with the end of a thin probe, lest it crumble when you pull it with the forceps; and you should carefully incise around the gum on all sides.
And take care not to break it, for if some of it remains, it will cause the patient a greater affliction than his first pain; and beware of doing what ignorant barbers do in their haste and recklessness in extracting it without using what we have described.
For they often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or that it pulls out with part of the jawbone, as we have witnessed many times; then, after extraction, let him rinse his mouth with a drink or with vinegar and salt; and if bleeding occurs from the placeâ€”which often happensâ€”then crush some alum and plug the place with it.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the alum does not help you. The image of the delicate forceps with which you first move the tooth: they should have long ends, short handles and be thick, lest they bend when you grasp the tooth with them. And this is the image of the large forceps:
They should be, as you see, thick in the handles so that when you grasp them, they do not yield or bend; short in the ends; and let them be of Indian iron or of well-tempered steel, with sharpened ends, and at their tips, teeth that interlock, so that they grasp firmly and tightly.
And the ends may be made like the shape of a file, and they will also be strong in their grip.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On extracting the roots of molars and removing broken jawbones.
If, when extracting a molar, a root remains broken, it is necessary to place a pledget of cotton soaked in butter on the area for a day or two until the area relaxes, then insert the forceps or hooks. whose ends resemble the beak of the bird called the "bulbul", and this is an image of the hooks:
They are made like a file from the inside or like a rasp. If you cannot remove it with these hooks, you should excavate the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert the instrument that resembles a small lever, which is depicted here:
short and slightly thick at the tip, and not too pointed, lest it break. If the root is extracted thus, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments, which are depicted here: the first has a triangular tip and is somewhat thick.
Another fine triangular image:
And we may also use this instrument with two branches, which is also depicted here:
and other instruments and tools previously mentioned in the extraction of molars.  We may also use this instrument which resembles a large hook, which is depicted here:
triangular, curved at the tip, slightly thick so that it does not break, and not too pointed. Know that the instruments for molars are numerous, as are all other instruments; they are almost innumerable, and the skillful and expert craftsman may invent instruments for himself, according to what the work and the diseases themselves indicate.
Because there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention instruments, due to the variety of their types.  If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth is broken or decayed, then examine it in its place with one of these instruments and hooks that were mentioned in the extraction of roots.
And we use a curette; this is its image:
It should be somewhat thick to grasp the bone so that it does not slip until the bone is removed. We treat the area with appropriate medication. If the bone has slight decay, scrape off its decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two
On the extraction of molars growing on top of others.
If molars grow outside their natural course, they are unsightly, especially in women and slaves.  Therefore, one should look; if the molar has grown behind another molarâ€¦
â€¦and its extraction or pushing back is impossible, extract it.  If it is attached to another molar, cut it with this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron.  The cutting should be done over many days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so as not to shake other teeth. But if it is projecting and firmly rooted, file it with a file made of Indian steel, whose image is this:
It should be entirely of Indian steel, its cutting edge very fine, like the file used to make needles. File the tooth little by little over many days gently, lest you shake the tooth and cause it to fall. Then file it again and remove it with some forceps.
If a tooth is broken and hurts the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed until the roughness of the fracture is removed, it becomes smooth, and it no longer hurts the tongue or impairs speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On binding loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything to eat lest they fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medications but the treatment has not worked,
the remedy is to bind them with a gold or silver thread, gold being preferable because silver tarnishes and decays after a few days, while gold remains unchanged forever; and the thread should be of medium fineness, according to what fits between the teeth.
The method of binding is to take the thread and insert its loop between the sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach a sound tooth on the other side by weaving.
Then you return the weaving to the side where you began and pull gently and skillfully so that they do not move at all, and tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth lest it loosen; then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them, twist them with a small drill, and hide them between the sound and loose teeth.
lest it injure the tongue; then leave it thus bound for as long as it remains; if it loosens or breaks, bind it with another thread, and he will enjoy it thus his whole life.  And this is an image of the teeth and the manner of binding in two sound teeth and two loose teeth as you see:
And one or two teeth may be returned to their place after falling out and bound as described and remain; this is done by a skilled craftsman, and he may carve bone from a cow and make it in the shape of a tooth and place it in the place from which the tooth fell. And it is tightened as we said, and it remains and he enjoys that.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Concerning the cutting of the ligament which appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound which has healed.
The procedure is that you open the mouth of the patient and place his head in your lap and raise his tongue, then cut that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its restraint. If there is some rigidity and complexity, and that is from the healing of a wound, then insert a hook into it and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knots are loosened.
Beware that the incision is not deep into the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and bleeding results. Then, after the cutting, let him gargle with rose water or with vinegar and cold water, then place under the tongue a pledget of linen which the patient holds in place each night.
Lest it reunite. If bleeding occurs, place powdered alum on the place. If the bleeding overcomes you, cauterize the area with a suitable lentil-shaped cautery. Then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the removal of a frog formed under the tongue.
A swelling resembling a small frog may occur under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and perhaps growing until it fills the mouth.  The procedure is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun and look at the swelling.
If you see it dark in color or black, hard and insensitive to the patient, do not interfere with it, for it is cancer. But if it is inclined to whiteness and moist, then pierce it with a probe and incise it with a fine scalpel, freeing it from every side.
If blood overwhelms you during your work, apply powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then return to your work until you remove it completely. Then have him gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And what grows in the throat from other tumors, glands may appear inside the throat similar to those that appear outside, called tonsils. If you treat them with what I mentioned in the section and they do not heal,
look, and if the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not approach it with an instrument. And if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not approach it with an instrument either, for fear of bleeding, but leave it until it matures, then either you may lance it or it may burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut.  The procedure is that you should look before operating whether its hot inflammation has completely subsided or diminished somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, his head in your lap, and open his mouth.  Have an assistant hold his tongue down with an instrument of this shape:
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue down with it and the tumor becomes visible, take a hook and insert it into the tonsil and pull it out as much as possible without pulling any of the membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument of this shape: it resembles scissors, except that its two ends are curved, and the edge of each is very sharp.  It is made of Indian iron or Damascus steel:
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel of this shape:
sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same way. Then, after cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peel or myrtle leaves or similar astringents have been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then treat him until he recovers. Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils; cut them as described for the tonsils.
I treated a woman with a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a polyp, somewhat insensitive, which nearly blocked the throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and was prevented from eating and drinking water.
She was on the verge of death if she had remained for a day or two.  Two branches extended from the tumor until they emerged from her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserted a hook into one of them, and pulled; a suitable piece was extracted.
Then I cut it where I reached it through the nostril. I then did the same with what protruded from the other nostril.  Then I opened her mouth, depressed her tongue, and inserted the hook into the tumor itself, cutting some of it. Only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and eat.  We repeatedly cut away parts of the tumor for a long time, the tumor replacing what was cut, until it lasted a long time for both of us.
Then I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat with a cautery iron. It stopped growing. Then she traveled away, and I do not know what God did with her afterwards.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cutting a uvular tumor.
That which is called a uvular tumor: if it descends as a swelling to the uvula and is white and oblong, it is called a column; and if it is thick below and round, it is called a grape. If it is treated with what we have mentioned in the classification and the treatment is not effective,
and I saw that the hot tumor had subsided from it and it was thin, then it should be cut, and whatever was gathered together, round, and did not have length, and was bloody or the color of pus, or black, or insensitive, then it should be avoided cutting it.
For there is danger to the patient. Therefore, if you see it in the manner which I mentioned, of its whiteness and length, then let the patient sit facing the sun, and compress his tongue with the instrument which I previously described, then insert the hook into the grape.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in cutting the tonsils. And it is necessary not to cut from it except that which exceeds the natural state without excess, for if you cut more from it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the cutting, use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of cutting it, then it is necessary to use a method in cauterizing it without fear or caution, and the way of cauterization is with a sharp medicine, and that is to place the patient's head in your lap.
Then compress his tongue with the instrument which we mentioned, then take from the sharp water which I mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unquenched lime, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument with it, and this is its image:
Its end, in which you place the medicine, has a concavity like the concavity of a spoon, and you place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lying on his side so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat, lest anything descend from it into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine while you squeeze it on the uvula for about half an hour until you see that it has blackened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe and moisten the cotton in the medicine.
And insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula, then leave it; it will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter and wipe away the medicine around it. Then gargle with cold water and treat externally with lotions and internally with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cautery and cutting; it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take fumitory, hyssop, thyme, rue, wormwood, chamomile, and betony, and similar herbs. Gather them all or some of them in a pot and submerge them in vinegar.
Boil it, the pot being coated with philosopher's clay, and there should be a hole in the middle of the lid into which a hollow instrument is fitted, made in this manner, of silver or copper:
Insert the end containing the pomegranate into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the uvula through the tube until the uvula is sufficiently steamed. Then repeat this several times until it withers. And beware of performing this treatment at the first onset of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling. It should only be done when its hot swelling subsides. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit an eggshell to its end, lest the patient's mouth be burned.
For the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is a good treatment and safe.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other things that lodge in the throat.
Often a bone or fishbone or something else lodges in the throat.  What is visible should be removed with the eye, after you have pressed down the tongue with an instrument towards the uvula.
So that you may see what is in the throat.  And if it does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, then you should make the patient vomit before his food is digested in his stomach, for perhaps the lodged thing will come out with the vomit, or the patient may swallow a piece of beet or a head of lettuce.
Or he may swallow a piece of dry bread, or you may take a piece of soft sea sponge and tie it to a string, then he swallows it; and when it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly.  Do this repeatedly.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out. If it does not come out by the means mentioned, then use a lead instrument of this shape:
It should be slightly thicker than a probe, and at its tip a hook. The patient inserts it into his throat gently, raising his head upwards, and taking care not to touch his windpipe, lest he cause coughing and push the bone or thorn further in; or the physician inserts it with his hand.
And the patient's insertion of it is better, for he knows the location of the lodged object, and he pushes downwards or pulls upwards with the instrument, all this as much as he is able, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On the removal of leeches lodged in the throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the section on medical treatment, and it is ineffective, then look into the patient's throat in the sunlight, after you have pressed down his tongue with the instrument I described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or with a soft, strong forceps; but if you cannot manage it otherwise, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert into the tube an iron heated in the fire.
Do this several times, and the patient should abstain from water the whole day; then he should take a jug full of cold water, open his mouth into it, and rinse his mouth with it, without swallowing a drop, moving the water from time to time with his hand.
The leech will fall out immediately when it feels the water; but if it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and with galbanum with the instrument described in the fumigation of the tonsils; do this several times; it will fall out.
The method of fumigation is this: take a pot containing hot coals heated in the fire, the pot being covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole; into this hole you fit the end of the instrument, then throw in the incense; the patient puts his mouth to the end of the tube, and closes his mouth so that the incense does not escape, until he knows that the incense has reached his throat.
Then the leech will fall out immediately; but if it does not, repeat the fumigation several times, and let the patient endure thirst, and eat salt and garlic, but drink no water; it must come out with this treatment.  And this is a picture of an instrument by which the leech is drawn from the throat when it is seen.
It resembles forceps, as you see, except that it has this curve which enters the throat, and its two ends resemble the beak of a bird; they are as rough as a file, so that when they grasp something, they do not let go.
And another instrument, by which the tongue is compressed; it is a wide iron ring, large enough to encompass the mouth above the tongue; it is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so that the mouth remains open without the will of the patient, nor can he remove it at all until it is taken away.
The description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened in this very way.
Chapter Forty.
It will deal with the cauterization of tumors and their incision.
Tumors are numerous and varied in kind, as will be described one by one in this book, and they differ in their cauterization and incision in two respects: one, from the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
And the second type is due to the locations in which they occur in the body, for a tumor occurring in the head is not the same as a tumor occurring in the rectum, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy location is not the same as a tumor occurring in a joint; each has its own ruling in treatment.
And some tumors should not be cauterized except after the pus in them has matured and become complete, while others should be cauterized while they are raw and have not fully matured, such as tumors that are near the joints.
For if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition prolongs until it corrupts what is around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, and thus be the cause of the paralysis of that member; or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
For if you delay its cauterization until it matures, you will harm that vital organ; or it may be near the rectum, so cauterize it raw.  It is necessary to cauterize a raw tumor, not fully mature, which is near the rectum, lest it become putrid and penetrate into the rectum and become a fistula.
And it will reach a point where it does not heal. And you should know the time for cauterizing tumors that have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor softens, and all the other signs.
And you should apply the cauterization in the lowest possible part of the tumor if that is possible, so that the flow of the matter will be easier downwards, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor. And let the cauterization extend along the length of the body if the tumors are in the hands or feet.
And the places of the muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and in short, in all flat places which do not bend.  But those which bend, let the cauterization be applied according to that place.  And if the tumor is in fleshy places, it is best to leave it alone.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said, for if you cauterize it before that, the pus will flow from it for a long time, and it will be very harmful and dirty, and perhaps its lips and base will harden. And some tumors may be cauterized across the body when necessary or according to what the member requires.
And you should use a single cauterization for small tumors and a wide cauterization or many incisions for large tumors, according to the size of the tumor. And there may be tumors which it is necessary to scrape the skin and cut if it has become like a rag.
And it has reached the point where it has died, like what happens in many abscesses and boils. And some of them are incised with a three-sided incision, and some of them are cut in the shape of an ivy leaf, like the inguinal tumor.
