And some of them use a circular incision and a crescent-shaped incision and similar incisions. And what does not have a head, like smooth, flat tumors, it should only be cauterized simply.  And if the tumor is large and has gathered for a long time and you cauterize it, you should not rush to remove all the pus at that time.
Rather, remove some of it, then dress the tumor until another day, then remove some of the pus as well. Do this repeatedly gradually until you remove all of it.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden removal of pus, so the patient may die and you do not realize it. Therefore, beware of this matter greatly.
After cauterizing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and look. If the wound of the tumor is small, or the incision is single and simple, then use a thread of linen or old cotton.  And if the tumor is large and the incisions of the cautery are many,
then you should insert a tent into each incision until some of them reach each other. And if some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or excised, then you should pack it with old cotton or linen lint without moisture and dress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after having soaked a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the measures, treatment, and styptics that we have described in many places in this book and in the division. If you are in winter and the location of the tumor is very nervous, then you should moisten the bandages with a warm drink and oil.
And place them on the location. And if you are in summer and the locations are fleshy, then you should apply the bandages soaked in water and oil or in a drink and oil, all of that cold, until the third day, as we said. Then you should unwrap the tumor and wipe it.
And use similar things in its treatment until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, I have mentioned for each tumor how to treat it, categorized and summarized.
Chapter forty-one.
On the incision of tumors that appear in the scalp.
Small tumors appear in the scalp; they are types of boils contained within sacs, which have conditions like a chickenâ€™s crop, and their types are many.  Some are fatty, and some contain a moisture resembling mud.
And some contain a moisture resembling pus and broth and the like, and some are hardened and solid.  There is no danger in incising and removing all of them, unless you encounter an artery when incising them.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument whose image comes after this, which is called the *madass*, until you know what it contains. If what it contains is moisture, then incise it lengthwise with a simple incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from point B to point C. When the moisture is evacuated, remove the sac that contained that moisture and cut it all away, leaving nothing of it at all, for it often returns if any of it remains.
Then dip cotton in yellow ointment, if available, otherwise in salt water, and fill the wound with it, and leave it until the next day, for it will eat away what remains of the sac. Then apply the cotton with the yellow ointment again, a second and third time, if needed.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. And if the tumor contains a fatty substance, incise it in this manner:
Make a cruciform incision and hook the hooks into the lips of the wound and strip it from every side and do your best to remove the peritoneum that contains it. If an artery obstructs you, do what I described to you afterwards.  And do likewise in the tumor if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as I mentioned. The incision on the hardened tumor is easier because it has little blood and moisture.  Some of these tumors which are in the head in some people may have no moisture at all.  For I incised a tumor inâ€¦
the head of an old woman and I found the tumor like hard, rough, white stone. It was impossible to break it, and if someone were to throw it, it would cut him. And it was not like other tumors occurring in the head, other than these, like the tumors that appear in the heads of children.
And at the roots of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision and make their bottoms from their lowers so that the flow of the matter to the bottom is easy, then treat them with what suits them of treatment.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning the incision on the swellings which frequently appear in the neck. These tumors frequently appear in the neck and under the armpits and in the groins, and may appear in the rest of the body. That which appears in the neck may be one or many, and some may develop from others.
And every swelling of them is within a special sac, as is the case in the abscesses and tumors of the head as we have described, and the types of these swellings are many; some are hardened, and some contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
So I saw that which was good in condition to the touch, and its exterior was near to the color of the skin, it moved to every direction, and it was not attached to a nerve of the neck, nor to a vein, nor to an artery, nor was it sunken. Then it should be incised with a simple incision from above to below the body in this manner, from line J to line B.
And you should dissect it from every side and stretch the lips of the skin with a hook or two hooks or many hooks if you need that, as we said in the tumors of the head, and you remove it little by little and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and let the scalpel not be very sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cutting or the patient be troubled, and you cut what you do not need to cut.  If you cut a vein or an artery and it hinders you from working, then place in the wound powdered verdigris or some of the things that stop blood.
And tighten the wound and leave it until the intensity of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to fester, for the blood will then stop. Then return to your work until you finish, then examine with your index finger if there remains anything, then small other growths, and cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the growth, then it should not be cut from its root, but it should be tied with a doubled tight thread and left until it falls off by itself without harm, then you stuff the wound with old cotton that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment, then you treat it.
And if you cut the growth entirely, then you should bring together the lips of the wound and stitch it immediately after you know that no residue remains. If you see that some residue of the bone of the growth remains,
then you should aim to cut to its root and base and use sutures and what we have mentioned. And as for any of the growths that contain fluids, lance them slightly where their ripening appears, and make the lance from the lower side as we said.
Then after lancing, use a dressing with Egyptian ointment or the like to eat away what remains of the corruption until, when you see the wound is clean, treat it with ointments that grow flesh until it heals.
Chapter forty-three.
On the incision of the throat for a tumor that occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the account of their words. As for those with angina, they should avoid an incision in the throat, for they will not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and the lung will be diseased. But those who have a hot tumor in the mouth or throat or tonsils, if there is no disease in the trachea, then it is necessary to use an incision of the throat to escape the harm which results from suffocation.
Then, if we want to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the trachea, a small transverse incision between two rings, to the extent of the incision in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This place is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it. If the physician is cowardly, he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until he reaches the trachea, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should incise the peritoneum which we have described. He will know the incision of the trachea from the phlegm which comes out of it, along with whatever is punctured, and from the interruption of the voice.  The wound is left open for a time.  When the time in which suffocation was feared has passed, the edges of the wound are joined together with skin.
And its outline is separate from the cartilage. Then apply medicines which cause the growth of flesh until it heals.  The author of this book said: The explanation of this statement which we have narrated is only if they see that the patient's throat is obstructed by one of these tumors which they mentioned.
And the patient is close to death and his breath is about to cease, they proceed to incise the throat so that the patient breathes through the wound, breathes somewhat, and is saved from death.  They are ordered to leave the wound open until the intensity of the illness subsides, its intensity being three days or thereabouts.
Then they are ordered to suture the wound and treat it until it heals. What I have witnessed myself is that a maidservant took a knife and plunged it into her throat, cutting some of the trachea. I was called to treat her, and I found her collapsing like one who is slaughtered. So I uncovered the wound.
I found that the blood which came out of the wound was slight, so I was certain that she had not cut a vein nor an artery, and air was coming out of the wound.  So I proceeded, I sutured the wound, and treated it until it healed.  Nothing happened to the maidservant except for hoarseness in her voice, and nothing more.
And after days she returned to her best condition. From this, we say that incision of the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-four.
Concerning the incision on the tumor which appears in the throat from the outside and is called the throat elephant.
This tumor which is called the throat elephant is a large tumor, the color of the body. It is frequent in women and is of two types.
Either it is natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it; as for the accidental, it is of two kinds: one resembling fatty swellings, and the other resembling the tumor which results from the complication of the artery, and its incision is dangerous. Therefore, one should not at all use the iron on them except what is small of them, if you probe and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles a fatty swelling and is not attached to anything of the veins; then incise it as you incise on swellings. And remove it with its containing sac if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove all of it, then treat the place with what is appropriate of treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
Concerning the incision of various swellings.
Swellings are numerous in types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a swelling and an abscess, since it is difficult.  I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and frightening pains until the boiling of the matter subsides. And the putrefaction is completed, then the fever and the intensity subside.  A swelling is not accompanied by heat, fever or pain, and it is contained in a serous sac, which is its special container. It is the color of the body, and its beginning is like a lentil, and it becomes like a blister, larger or smaller.
And it is of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are many, as I mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when you come to treat a swelling, you should probe and examine it first with the instrument called the probe, whose image comes in the chapter after this.
Assess the size of the swelling, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If flowing moisture of any color comes out, then incise it with a simple incision as I mentioned in the case of other swellings. If no moisture comes out after the probe, then know that it is fatty.
Then make a firm incision on it in this manner as I have taught you, and hang it with hooks.  Peel the skin from every side gently, and preserve the sac if you can so that you remove it whole with the swelling.
If the sac is perforated during the procedure and you cannot remove it wholeâ€”and this often happensâ€”then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part remains, however small or large, the swelling will return in most cases.
If it overcomes you and a little remains, cauterize the wound when you have finished with some sharp cauterizing points, and tighten the wound.  Place on top of it something that soothes hot swelling and treat it with other treatments until it heals. If the swelling is large, suture its lips and treat it with what unites [the wound].
If you encounter a bleeding or non-bleeding vein and bleeding occurs, quickly cauterize the area with powdered vitriol and leave it constricted for two or three days until the wound becomes infected and the flow of blood stops.  Then return to removing what remains of the swelling.

Chapter forty-seven.
On the treatment of men's breasts.
The breasts of some people may swell at the age of puberty until they resemble the breasts of women, remaining swollen and unsightly.  He who dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this manner.
From line B to line C. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with cauterizing medicine, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the breast leans downward and becomes flaccid due to its size, as happens to women,
it should be incised on its upper sides with two crescent-shaped incisions, each connected to the other at their ends, so that the larger line encircles the smaller one, in this manner: From line B to line C, then remove the skin between the incisions and remove the fat, and use what we have mentioned of sutures and cauterizations and whatever treatment is needed until it heals.
If you cannot remove what should be removed because of the patient's anxiety or because of bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in caustic ointment and leave it until it consumes the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the type of hard warts contained in a sac, and some contain fluids.  As for those containing fluids, an incision should be made in a crescent shape in this manner.
from line B to line C.  Then, if all the fluid in the tumor is evacuated, pack it with old cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the type of knotted warts, make an incision on it as we mentioned in the incision on warts. If the wound persists to the point that pus spreads, use cauterization as we mentioned previously.
Chapter forty-nine.
On incising the tumor that appears near the artery or vein, called aneurysma.
If the artery is injured and the skin over it heals, a tumor often results from this; similarly, a vein may also develop a swelling and tumor.
The signs by which one knows whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or a vein: if the tumor is from an artery, it is oblong, situated deep in the body, and if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel as if it has a murmur.
That which is from a vein, the tumor is round, on the surface of the body, and incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other places of the body, and when they are very large; therefore, one should avoid treating them with iron.
And those also in the extremities or in the head should be avoided. As for those that are a swelling of the mouth of the artery, incise it in the skin lengthwise, then open the incision with hooks, then strip the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I have shown you in ligating the two arteries in the temples, then incise with a scalpel the area between the ligatures until all the blood in it comes out.
And the tumor will resolve, then you use treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off, then you treat it with suitable ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a vein incision, then you should grasp with your hand as much of the tumor as possible together with the skin, then insert a needle below the place you grasped with your hand and pass it, with a doubled thread in it, until you bring it out from the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly with it, according to what I mentioned to you in tying the uvula from both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads will slip, then insert another needle with another thread under the whole tumor at the intersection of the first needle.
And tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its middle until what is in it comes out. Then cut away the excess skin and leave what was tied. Then apply a pledget that you have soaked in a drink and oil. Then use treatment with cauterization and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor which occurs from the twisting of a nerve.
Just as a tumor occurs in an artery and a vein, so a tumor occurs in a nerve if a blow occurs in it or it occurs from excessive fatigue or the like. And this is most common in the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a firm tumor resembling in color the rest of the body. It is, in most cases, painless.  If pressed firmly, the patient feels a kind of numbness.  The tumor is not located deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, but does not extend forward or backward. As for those in the joints, they should not be treated with iron, for it may cause paralysis. And as for those in the head or foreheadâ€¦
Incise the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its base. If it is large, hook it with a hook and strip it off, then remove it and close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-One.
Concerning the excision of warts which appear in the abdomen.
Often, some people develop warts on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies, called fungal warts because of their resemblance to mushrooms. Their base is thin and their head is thick; their lips may be transformed, and some are small while others become very large.
I once saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen that most resembled mushrooms; they were white, thin at the base, their lips had been transformed and cracked, and moisture constantly flowed from them.  I excised them; one weighed about eighteen ounces, and the other about six ounces.
In excising them, observe: if the patient is moist and the wart is white, moist, and thin at the base, excise it with a wide scalpel, and have cauteries ready in the fire, for often a great deal of blood gushes forth when they are cut.
If the bleeding overwhelms you, cauterize it immediately. If you see that the patient is cowardly and frightened by cutting with iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the wart of this description, and leave it for two days, then increase the tightness of the lead, continuing to tighten it.
Keep tightening it until it affects the wart so that it is severed and falls off by itself without effort. If the wart is thick at the base, there is some fear and risk in cutting it, especially if it is in the abdomen; however, it is possible to cut half of it or part of it.
Then cauterize it lest it return, and then treat it until the wound heals. Beware of attempting to cut a wart that is dark in color, insensitive, and foul-looking, for it is a cancerous tumor, and we shall describe cancer later.
Chapter fifty-two.
In the treatment of an umbilical polyp.
An umbilical polyp results from many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum of the abdomen, from which fat or intestine protrudes, as occurs in other hernias; or from blood emitted from a vein or artery, as previously described; or from gas that collects there.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of fat, then the tumor will be similar in color to the body, soft, painless, and of varying appearance. If it is due to the protrusion of intestine, its appearance will be more varied than we have described. If you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns; sometimes it is accompanied by gurgling and increases greatly with the use of the bath and strenuous exertion. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
It does not disappear when pressed with your hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, in addition to these signs, the tumor appears dark. If it is due to newly grown flesh, the tumor will be firm, hard, and remain at a constant size.
If it is due to gas, it will be soft to the touch.  The treatment is as follows: If the umbilical polyp is due to arterial or venous blood or gas, you should refrain from treating it, as this is dangerous and risky, as I explained in the chapter where I discussed tumors arising from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical protrusion is due to intestine or fat, you should instruct the patient to hold his breath and stand erect, then apply a ligature around the entire umbilicus. Then instruct him to lie on his back before you. Then incise with a broad scalpel around the umbilicus at the place you marked with the ligature. Then pull the center of the tumor upwards with a large forceps and tie the incised area with a strong thread or silk tendon tightly.
The knot of the ligature should be a slip knot. Then open the center of the tightened tumor above the ligature and insert your index finger. Seek the intestine; if you find that the ligature has grasped it, loosen the slip knot and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find fat, pull it out with forceps.
And cut off its excess. If you encounter an artery or vein, ligate it carefully and return to your work. Take two needles and thread them with strong threads. Insert the needles into the incision you made around the tumor, crossing them, having passed them through. Then tie the tumor in four places over the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the constricted flesh mortifies and falls off by itself, or cut it off when it becomes soft and mortified. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical protrusion is from flesh that has grown there,
or from moisture, it is necessary to constrict the tumor as we said and evacuate the blood or moisture that you find in it, then treat it with what will close the wound.
Chapter fifty-three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and the way to treat it with medication and the warning against treating it with iron, lest it ulcerate, and we have mentioned the cancer that develops in the womb and the warning against treating it.
And the ancients mentioned that if the cancer is in a place where it can all be excised, like the cancer that is in the breast or in the thigh and similar accessible organs, to remove it entirely, especially if it is small and beginning; but if it is old and large, it should not be approached.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve that, and the procedure, if it is accessible as we said, is to first purge the patient with black bile several times, then to bleed him if there is evident fullness in the veins.
Then you position the patient in a position that allows for the procedure, then you apply to the cancer hooks suitable for it, then you scrape it from every side together with the skin completely, until nothing of its roots remains, and let the blood flow and do not stop it quickly.
Rather, squeeze the areas and drain all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you can, and if during the procedure you encounter profuse bleeding from a severed artery or vein, cauterize the vessel until the bleeding stops, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter fifty-four.
On the treatment of fistula.
We have already described in the classification the types of dropsy, how the accumulation of water occurs, and the signs of each type and its treatment with medication.  That which is treated with cautery is only the ascitic type, and the tympanitic type and the fleshy type are not at all approached with cautery.
For that is deadly, if you have treated this type of ascitic fistula with medication and your treatment has not been effective, then look, for if the patient is extremely weak or has another illness besides the fistula, such asâ€¦
a cough or diarrhea or the like, then beware of treating him with cautery, for that is dangerous.  If you see that the patient is strong, without any illness other than the fistula alone, and is neither a child nor an old man, then the procedure is that you should make the patient stand before you. and an attendant behind him should press his abdomen with his hands and push the water downwards towards the pubic region, then take a pointed scalpel of this form:
pointed at both ends, long, and with a sharp point like a scalpel, except that it has a slight curve so that it does not penetrate the intestine during the procedure and injure it. Then look, if the fistula originates from the intestineâ€¦
then it is necessary that you make the incision three fingerbreadths below the navel in line with it above the pubis. If the fistula originated from liver disease, let your incision be three fingerbreadths to the left of the navel; if it originated from spleen disease, let the incision be on the right side by three fingerbreadths.
Also, the incision should not be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie down, lest the fluids flow to that weak area. Then pierce the whole skin with the instrument, and then insert the instrument into the incision and raise your hand.
with the scalpel between the skin and the peritoneum, as if you are flaying it, and the amount you flay should be about the size of a fingernail or so. Then pierce the peritoneum until the scalpel reaches an empty space, which is the location of the water, then remove it and insert into the opening the instrument of this form:
It is like a tube made of a thin reed, made of silver or copper or smooth, polished lead. It has a small opening at its lower end, and three openings at its sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And it may have its end sharpened in the shape of a pen nib, in this form:
At its upper end is a ring; if the instrument reaches the water, it will immediately flow down onto the instrument, and you will evacuate a moderate amount of water at a time. For if you evacuate more than is necessary at one time, the patient may die from the dissolution of his animal spirit.
Or he may experience a faintness approaching death. But evacuate according to his strength and what the patient's condition indicates to you from the strength of his pulse and the good color of his complexion. Then remove the instrument and the water will be retained, for it will be retained immediately.
Because of the skin which covers the opening in the peritoneum which I have told you to prepare in that manner. Then you may reinsert the instrument another day if you see the patient is able to tolerate it.
And you will also evacuate a small amount of water, doing this for several days while you remain vigilant and guard against error, until only a little water remains. If you fear for the patient and leave a large amount of water, treat him by burying him in hot sand.
And profuse sweating in the bath and sun, and making him endure thirst, and treating him with drying medicines until he is cured.  And cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after evacuating the water as I have described above.
Chapter Fifty-Five.
In the treatment of children who are born with their urinary passages imperforate, or with a narrow or misplaced opening.
Some boys may be born with their urethra imperforate, and it is necessary to immediately puncture it at birth.

As for one whose opening is narrow, it is treated with lead as we said for many days until it widens.  Those whose opening is misplacedâ€”for some are born with the opening at the end of the penis and cannot urinate forwardâ€”
