Chapter Fifty-Six.
Concerning the ulcer that appears in the foreskin and penis, and blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the foreskin to the penis.
This ulcer often appears in the urethra; it is a fleshy, rough growth, and there are benign and malignant types.  The benign type should be tied off with a fine ligature.
And cut it until you remove it entirely, then apply a cotton swab soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it afterwards with palm ointment until it heals. But if the ulcer is malignant and dark in color, you should cauterize it after cutting and excising it.
If the ulcer is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and part of the ulcer is inside the foreskin and part outside, you should remove the inner part first, until it heals; then treat the outer part.
Because if you treat both at the same time, you risk perforation of the foreskin.  Blackness and corruption may also appear in the labia and foreskin; you should excise everything that is black and tending towards, or already corrupted.
Then apply honey mixed with crushed and sifted pomegranate peel and acacia, then treat it with other remedies until it heals. If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a crescent-shaped cautery iron of this shape:
Cauterization is beneficial for both conditions, namely bleeding and a corrupted wound. If the glans is eroded and completely destroyed at some point, you should insert a lead tube into the urethra so that the patient can urinate through it. As for the adhesion of the foreskin to the glans...
This adhesion occurs in those whose foreskin is intact and who have not been circumcised.  It may also result from a wound or swelling. You should separate it with a blunt scalpel until the bond is broken and the glans is freed on all sides.
If it is difficult to distinguish them upon close examination, you should remove some of the glans and some of the foreskin.  The foreskin is thin, so it may quickly perforate due to its thinness. Then separate the foreskin from the glans with a thin linen cloth soaked in cold water, lest they adhere again.
Then treat with an astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter fifty-seven.
On the cleansing of boys and the treatment of what may befall them.
Circumcision is nothing other than the separation of a connection like other wounds, except that since it is done by our will and is especially used in boys, it is necessary that we describe the best procedure for it.
And the easiest path leading to safety.  I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is something we have acquired.
by experience.  And that is because I found the majority of surgeons and barbers use purification with the scalpel and with scissors, and they use the cautery and ligature with thread and cutting with the fingernail. I have tried all of these methods.
So I found nothing better than purification with scissors and ligature with thread, because purification with the scalpel often adheres to the skin, because the skin of the foreskin is in two layers, so perhaps the upper layer of skin is cut and the lower layer remains.
so it is necessary to cut again and renewed pain.  And purification with the cautery does not prevent cutting the end of the urethra, because it may enter its hole. And as for purification with the fingernail, perhaps the skin slips and your work is spoiled or the boy's skin is naturally short...
so they are often born like that and do not need purification, and I have seen that. As for purification with scissors and ligature with thread, experience has revealed its advantage to me because the scissors are proportionate in cutting, because the blade above is like the blade below.
So when you press your hand with the proportion of the two blades, you cut with one measurement and in one time, so the rein of the thread becomes like a wall for the skin of the urethra from all sides, with no mistake at all. And the way of working first is to reassure the boy.
Especially if he is one who understands a little that you are only tying the thread in his urethra and leaving him until another day, then rejoice him and please him in every way you can, and what he accepts with his mind, then make him stand upright before you.
And let him not be sitting, and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the boy's eye does not fall upon them at all, nor on any of the instruments, then you insert your hand into his urethra and inflate the skin and lift it upwards until the head of the urethra comes out.
Then you cleanse it from what gathers in it of filth, then tie the marked place with a doubled thread, then tie a second ligature a little below it, then grasp with your thumb and forefinger the place of the lower ligature firmly, and cut between the two ligatures. Then raise the skin upwards quickly and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and lessens the swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some ash of burnt dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or fine wheat flour, for it is also excellent.
Then raise the skin upwards quickly and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and lessens the swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some ash of burnt dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or fine wheat flour, for it is also excellent. Then apply on the testicles from above a compress of a cooked white egg in rose water, beaten with fresh, good rose oil, and leave it on until the next day, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And this is the image of the scissors which are suitable for circumcision:
They should be sharp, cutting, not crooked, with smooth nails, and the length of the two blades should be equal to the length of the two handles.  As for the mistake that occurs in circumcision, it may be that the inner skin is entirely or partially inverted during the cutting; therefore you should extend it at once with your fingernail before the place swells.
And cut it evenly; if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with a thread and cut it. If three days have passed and what is under the head of the urethra remains swollen and inflamed, then leave it until the hot swelling subsides and then remove it gently.
And cut it as seems best to you, and be careful of the head of the urethra, for if something of the head of the urethra is cut, it does not harm, so treat it with what heals the wound from the [medicinal] plants which we described in the chapter on plants.
And if some of the skin is cut beyond the proper amount and it retracts upwards, that also does not harm much, so treat it as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter fifty-eight.
In the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Urine retained in the bladder is due to its obstruction by a stone, or clotted blood, or pus, or a growing flesh, and the like.  If you treat that with what we have mentioned of the types of treatment described in the classification.
And if the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is because of a stone which has become lodged in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that the patient should sit on his knees, then let someone mount his back and press his abdomen towards his back, and let the patient hold himself as much as he can.
Then the neck of the bladder will be forced to push the stone backwards, and the urine will flow. If it does not flow by what we have mentioned, and the matter becomes severe for the patient, then it is necessary that you use its extraction with the instrument called a catheter, whose image is this:
It is made of silver and is thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's feather in thinness and length, about a span and a half, with a gentle funnel at its head. The method of drawing off the urine with it is that you take a doubled thread and tie to its end wool or cotton firmly.
And insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the catheter, and cut with scissors if any of the wool remains so that it enters the tube and stops it like a stopper. Then anoint the catheter with oil or butter or egg white, and seat the patient on a chair.
And cleanse his bladder and penis with moist ointments or oil and lukewarm water. Then gently insert the catheter into the urethra until it reaches the root of the urethra, then bend the urethra upwards towards the navel, and then push the catheter inside.
Until the urethra is completely bent, then push the catheter inside until, when it reaches near the rectum, incline the penis downwards with the catheter inside it, and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels that it has reached something hollow.
This is done in this manner because the passage through which the urine flows is tortuous. Then gently pull the thread with the wool, and the urine will follow the wool, then remove it and the urine will flow out, and replace the catheter.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
And the instruments suitable for this are made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long, thin tube like a pus-cannula; and if you wish to instill liquids and medicines, this is done with an instrument called a syringe, and this is its image:.
It is made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as thin as a millet stalk, hollow throughout except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And the hollow part in which the propellant is, should be only large enough to contain it, without excess, so that when you draw something up with it, it will be drawn up, and when you push it, it will be pushed out, in the same way as the squirt which is used to throw oil in naval battles.
And if you wish to introduce liquids into the bladder, you insert the tip of the syringe into the liquid and draw it upwards with the piston; then the liquid will be drawn up into the body of the syringe. Then insert its tip into the urethra, as we described in the catheters, then push the liquid with the piston.
Then that liquid will reach the bladder immediately, so that the patient feels it. And this is the image of a fine clyster also, with which the bladder is injected:
It is made of silver or lead, its upper end resembling a small funnel, and below it a groove in which the ligature lies. Then you take a camel's bladder and put into it the liquid you wish to inject into the bladder, then tie it tightly between the two parts with a doubled thread.
And warm that liquid a little over the fire, then insert the tip of the clyster into the urethra, then press your hand firmly on the bladder containing the liquid until the patient feels that the liquid has reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder, take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around. Then insert a strong thread into these holes as they go around, then gather the circle as you gather a cloth, after putting in it whatever you want of liquids, ointments and water.
Then tie it to the instrument and do with it as you did with the bladder, squeezing with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the Extraction of Stones.
We have already mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and described the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and the signs of all that, and mentioned the stones in which action by hand and incision is necessary.
These are the stones generated specifically in the bladder, and those which become lodged in the urethra; and I am describing this with clear explanation and brevity.
I say that stones generated in the bladder most often affect boys, and among their signs is that urine passes from the bladder resembling water in its thinness.
And sand appears in it, and he scratches his penis and plays with it, and it often hangs down, then spreads, and the anus protrudes with it in many of them.  It is easy to treat in boys until they reach fourteen years of age, and difficult in old men; as for young men, it is intermediate between these.
And the one whose stone is larger, his treatment is easier, and the small ones are the opposite.  If we proceed to treatment, then it is necessary first that the patient be given an enema to remove all the excrement in his intestines, for it may prevent the finding of the stone upon examination.
Then the patient is taken by his feet, and he is shaken and jolted downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place several times. Then seat him between your hands upright, with his hands under his thighs, so that the entire bladder is tilted downwards, then examine him.
And feel it from the outside. If you feel the stone in the space, then immediately proceed to incise it. If it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary to lubricate the index finger with oil from the left hand if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If he is a fully developed boy, then insert it into his anus and search for the stone until, when it falls under your finger, you move it little by little to the neck of the bladder. Then press on it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place you want to incise.
And you order a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and you order another servant to stretch the labia upwards with his right hand, and with his other hand to stretch the skin under the labia towards the place where the incision will be. Then take the sharp scalpel, whose image is this:
And incise between the anus and the labia, not in the middle, but towards the left side of the anus.  The incision should be upon the stone itself, with your finger in the rectum, and you pressing it outwards obliquely, so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, to the extent that allows the stone to exit.
Not larger, for perhaps the finger in the rectum will press the stone during the incision, and it will exit without difficulty. And know that there may be stones which have angles and edges, making their exit difficult because of that.
And some are smooth, resembling an acorn and round, making their exit easy.  As for those that have angles and edges, enlarge the incision a little. If it does not exit like this, then you should try to grasp it with a firm forceps, the end of which is like a file.
To grasp the stone firmly so that it does not escape, or else insert a delicate instrument with a hooked end underneath it.  If you cannot manage it, widen the incision slightly.  If you are overcome by blood, staunch it with alum.
If there is more than one, push the largest first towards the opening of the bladder, then incise upon it, then push the smaller ones afterwards.  Do likewise if there are more than two. If it is very large, it is foolish to make a large incision upon it, for it exposes the patient to one of two things.
Either death, or a constant dribbling of urine because the wound will not heal at all. But try to push it out, or try to break it with forceps until you remove it in pieces.
When you have finished your work, dress the wound with costus, aloes, and myrrh, and bind it. Place over it rags moistened with oil and wine, or with rose ointment and cold water, to calm the hot inflammation. Then let the patient lie on his back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day.
When it is loosened, wash the area with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with palm ointment and basil ointment until it heals. If excessive hot inflammation, gangrene, or the like, appears in the wound, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing urinationâ€”which is known by the passage of blood with urineâ€”
insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains, it will lead to corruption and decay of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of problem with a suitable remedy until it heals.
Throughout the treatment, it is necessary to bind and draw together the thighs to secure the medications applied to the area. If the stone is small and has lodged in the urinary canal, preventing the passage of urine, treat it as I have described before resorting to incision.
I have often dispensed with incision by means of this treatment, which I have tested.  It consists of taking a steel probe of this shape:
The tip is triangular, sharp, and fixed in a shaft; then take a thread and tie the penis below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder, then gently insert the probe into the urethra until the probe reaches the stone itself.
And turn the probe in the stone little by little, aiming to pierce it until it passes through to the other side, whereupon the urine will immediately flow. Then press your hand on what remains of the stone from outside the penis, for it will break up and exit with the urine, and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not feasible due to some impediment, tie a thread below the stone and another above it; then incise over the stone in the penis itself between the two threads and remove it.
Then untie the ligature and clean the clotted blood from the wound.  The reason for tying the thread below the stone is to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other above, so that when the thread is untied after the stone's removal, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should pull the skin upwards so that it returns and covers the wound as we have said.
Chapter sixty-one.
On the removal of stones from women.
Stones rarely develop in women.  If a woman does develop a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One is that the woman may be a virgin, and another is that you will rarely find a woman who allows herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or one of his close relatives, and thirdly, you will not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially in manual work, and fourthly, the incision site for stones in women is far from the stone itself, requiring a deep incision.
And in that there is danger. If necessity demands it, then a skilled female physician should be sought, but they are rarely found. If you cannot find one, seek a chaste and gentle male physician, or find a skilled midwife experienced in women's matters, or a woman who has some knowledge of this craft.
Then have her present and instruct her to do everything you tell her to do, first examining for the stone.  This is done by seeing if the woman is a virgin; if so, insert a finger into her rectum and search for the stone. If you find it and press it with your finger,
then instruct her to incise it. If she is not a virgin but a woman who has had intercourse, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If she finds it, she should move it from the opening of the bladder downwards as far as she can reach, until it reaches the groin. Then she should incise it opposite the middle of the vulva at the groin, whichever side allows it and where the stone is felt in that area.
And her finger should not leave the stone, pressing on it. Let the incision be small at first, then insert a probe into that small incision. If she feels the stone, enlarge the incision as much as is necessary for the stone to come out. And know that the types of stones are many.
Some are small and some are large, smooth and rough, long and round, and some have branches.  Know their types so that you may know what to do. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered verdigris on the area and hold it there for a while until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
until the stone comes out. And be sure to have with you the instruments which were mentioned for removing stones from men, to help you in your work. If the bleeding is excessive, and you know from the pulse of the blood that it is from a severed artery, place a styptic on the area.
And bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it; do not touch it again, and leave the stone; do not remove it, for the patient might die. Then treat the wound. When the severity of the bleeding subsides after a few days, and the area becomes infected, then return to your work until the stone comes out.
Chapter sixty-two.
On the incision for the watery dropsy.
Watery dropsy is the collection of moisture in the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle, and is called the scrotum. It may be in a special membrane prepared for it by nature in a part of the testicle.
So that one thinks it is another testicle. It is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum that we mentioned. This occurs rarely. This dropsy is produced by a weakness affecting the testicles, so that this matter gathers there. It may also result from a blow to the testicles.
This moisture is of many colours; it may be yellowish, or bloody red, or black and purulent, or white and watery, which is most common. The signs by which it is known are:
Where the water collects, if it is in the white peritoneum that we mentioned, the swelling is round, slightly elongated like the shape of an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a special membrane, then the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, one imagines it to be another testicle. If the moisture is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum, it is palpable. But if you wish to know the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe, the picture of which has preceded. Whatever comes out after the probe, you judge it according to what is inside.
If we proceed to treatment with cautery, you should order the patient to be bled if he can and you see his body is full. Then he should lie on his back on something slightly elevated, and you place many cloths under him, then you sit on his left side.
And you order a servant to sit on his right side, stretching his penis to one side of the scrotum and towards the groin. Then you take a broad scalpel and incise the skin of the scrotum lengthwise in the middle to near the pubis.
Make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum in half, until it reaches the white peritoneum containing it. Then peel it back and be careful not to tear it.  Your peeling should be done from the side attached to the testicle more, and you should complete the peeling as much as you can.
Then widely evacuate the water-filled peritoneum and remove all the water. Then separate the edges of the incision with hooks and stretch the peritoneum upwards, without touching the containing scrotum skin, and cut the peritoneum however you can cut it, either in its entirety or piece by piece.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely cut it, you cannot be sure the water will not return. If the testicle protrudes outside its skin during your work, then, once you have finished cutting the peritoneum, return it, then close the edges of the scrotum skin with sutures.
Then treat it like other wounds until it heals. If the testicle is damaged by another disease, then it is necessary to tie off the vessels in the spermatic cord for fear of bleeding, then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle, then treat it as we have mentioned.
If the fluid is collected in both sides, know that there are two hydroceles. Incise the other side as you did the first, or if it is easier to do both at once, do so. Then wipe away the blood.
And insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose ointment, and outside place other wool soaked in a medicament and oil, and spread it over the testicles and the flanks of the abdomen, and place folded cloths on top, which are the pads. And bind them from above with a bandage that has six ends, in this manner:
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together, and stuffed with fluffy wool in this way, and the ends that are used to bind it are made of cotton or damp wool, and gathered tightly in the waistband of the trousers, and one end is taken over the buttocks to the back.
The second is taken towards the pubis, and the two ends are placed on the thighs from below, and the two remaining ends on the groins, and all the binding is gathered in the waistband of the trousers. This dressing may also be made with cautery instead of incision with an iron.
It is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and with it incise the skin of the scrotum while it is hot, as we described, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed. Then take another cautery of this form:
It resembles the Greek eye, then you cauterize that peritoneum with it while it is hot until all the moisture comes out, then you stretch the peritoneum with hooks and peel off with the cautery the containing layer and cut it as much as you can until you excise it all, and be careful that the testicle is not touched by the fire.
Know that this procedure is safer and prevents more blood loss, and is better than the procedure with incision.  If during the incision or during cauterization you encounter a hot swelling, bleeding, or something else, you should treat whatever you encounter with the appropriate treatment, as previously explained.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure because of its unpleasantness, then use cauterization as I describe, if it is proven to you by what we have presented as evidence.
If the hydrocele is watery, then the patient should sit on a high chair, then cauterize the swelling at its base with a wide scalpel, and make the cauterization along the length of the body, and make the opening wide so that all the water flows out. Then place cotton on the area and bind it.
And leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out during cauterization, but rather because the white membrane obstructs the opening of the wound and prevents the water from coming out,
then you should insert a goose feather or the instrument with which the water of hydroceles is extracted, the image of which has been previously shown, or enlarge the opening of the wound slightly.
And know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so. If it accumulates, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should protect himself during those days.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
Concerning the incision on the fleshy scrotum and its treatment.
Know that an incision on this scrotum is one of the dangerous things leading to death in most cases; therefore, we see that it should be left alone and kept safe.  I will, however, mention the treatment and types of swelling.
I say that many swellings may occur in the bodies which constitute the testes, and this is due to many causes, either from a sharp humour which descends to the testes, or from a blow. The colour of the swelling is the same as the colour of the body, and it does not contain water.
And the swelling is hard, and perhaps the swelling is hardened, its colour is dark, and it is insensitive. It may be from the entanglement and swelling of the arteries, as has been mentioned before, or from the swelling of the veins. As for what is from the swelling of the arteries, this is known by the dispersion of the swelling.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be exposed at all. As for that which is from the swelling of the veins, nothing disperses from it when pressed with the fingers.  This type may possibly be incised, as well as other flesh.
And that is to incise the skin of the testicle, then to extend the testicle upwards and to bring it out from the white peritoneum, and to free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and to ligate the vessels and to cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from every side of the testicle. If the testicle has adhered to those growing tissues.
Then it is necessary to bring out the testicle and cut it. And if the adhesion is between some of the peritonea or between the vessels, then it is necessary to free all that adhesion and cut it with a circular cut.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is from behind, then it is necessary to cut it all and bring out the testicle as we said. When your work is finished, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter sixty-four.
In the treatment of hydrocele with a hernia.
A hernia is a twisted tumor, somewhat twisted, similar to a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the testicles and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking.  The treatment of this ailment is very similar to what has been mentioned previously.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that it is necessary to seat the patient on a high chair, then push the scrotum downwards, then grasp the skin of the testicle with your fingers along with the vessels which are near the penis.
And let a servant hold it for you and stretch it tightly. Then incise with a wide, sharp scalpel an oblique incision along the vessels until the vessels are exposed. Then dissect from each side as I mentioned to you in the dissection of the arteries in the armpits. Then insert a needle with a doubled thread.
And tie it at the first place where the hernia appeared and tie it also at the end. Then incise it in the middle with a vertical incision along the body and remove what has gathered within of foul, putrid fluids.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds that you want to heal, so that the vessels which nourish one of the testicles fall away; there is no harm in that.  But if the hernia affects all the vessels, then it is necessary to remove one of the testicles with the vessels, lest the testicle be deprived of nourishment. before cutting the vessels, so that they wither and are of no use.
Chapter sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal hernia.
This hernia occurs from a tear which appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen towards the groins from the flanks of the abdomen, so that the intestine descends from that rupture into one of the testicles.
And this rupture is either from a tear in the peritoneum or from its extension.  These two types occur from many causes, either a blow, a jump, a shout, lifting something heavy, and the like.  Its sign, if it is from an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs little by little over a long time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is even towards the depth because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.  Its sign, if it is from a tear in the peritoneum, is that from its beginning it causes a great pain suddenly, and the swelling is different, apparent under the skin nearby.
That is by the exit of the intestine and its passage outside of the peritoneum.  And with the intestine, the epiploon may exit; this hernia is called intestinal and epiploic.  It may be with wind.  And the stool may flow into the intestine and be retained there, so that the death of the patient results.
Because it causes severe pain and gurgling, especially if it is constricted.  The treatment of the types of this illness with iron is dangerous, so it should be avoided. The method of treatment is that you order the patient to return the intestine with his hand into his abdomen if it is possible to return it.
Then he lies on his back between your hands and raises his legs. Then you stretch the skin next to the groin upward and incise the skin of the entire scrotum lengthwise. Then you insert hooks into the edges of the incision to the extent needed to open the incision with them.
And the incision should be of a size that allows the testicle to come out. Then you strip away the membranes which are under the skin of the scrotum until the white, hard peritoneum is revealed on every side. Then you introduce your index finger next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the testicle and the second peritoneum, and you loosen the adhesion which is behind the testicle. Then you draw it inwards with the right hand into the skin of the scrotum, and with this you stretch the white peritoneum upwards with the left hand and raise the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
And you order the assistant to pull the testicle upwards, and you yourself completely loosen the adhesion from behind, and you search with your finger lest there be something of the twisted intestine in the white, hard peritoneum. If you find anything, then push it down into the abdomen.
Then you take a needle with thick thread twisted from ten threads and insert it at the end of the peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum next to the incision. Then you cut the ends of the twisted thread until there are four threads. Then you place some of them on top of each other in a firm way and tie them tightly to the peritoneum, which we said is under the skin of the scrotum, in two places. Then you also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing of the vessels which nourish it can reach it. Lest a hot swelling result from that, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, you leave of the peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum the amount of a finger bone.
Lest a hot swelling result from that, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, you leave of the peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum the amount of a finger bone. And you cut all the rest in a circle and remove the testicle with it. Then you make an incision at the bottom of the scrotum's skin so that blood and pus flow from it as we described before. Then oiled wool is used and placed in the wound, and the ligature which we described is used.
And the white peritoneum which we mentioned may be cauterized after it is cut, as a precaution against bleeding, for that often happens.  And the ligature is left until it falls off by itself. If it is slow to fall off, then let it be loosened with warm water and apply something to it that will make it sweat until it falls off.
Then you treat the wound with the rest of the treatment for wounds until it heals.
Chapter sixty-six.
Concerning the windy hernia.  I have not seen anyone dare to treat this windy hernia with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as we described in the hernia with a dÄlÄ«yah, that is, that the vessels be gently ligated below after an incision is made on them.
Then it is incised in the middle and the tumor is treated with something that opens it until the vessels fall away, and the wound is treated as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter sixty-seven.
Concerning the hernia which is in the groin.
A hernia may appear in the groin, as we said, so the place swells and nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum; and if it descends, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if time passes, the fissure in the peritoneum increases until the intestine and epiploon descend into the scrotum, and this occurs from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the place swells and protrudes, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I have previously described.
And it may be treated with an iron instrument in this manner: the patient lies on his back in your hands, then you incise the swollen, protruding hernia horizontally to the extent of three fingers, then you loosen the peritoneum which is under the skin.
Until, when the white peritoneum which follows is uncovered, you take a probe and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum and press it into the depth of the abdomen, then you sew the two protruding parts of the peritoneum to the end of the probe, and adhere one to the other with the suture.
Then you remove the probe, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle nor anything else, as I instructed you in the treatment of intestinal hernia, then you treat it with the treatment of wounds.
If the threads are cut, I clean them and dress the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the protrusion of the hernia does not increase.
Cauterization is better in this place because it is closer to safety.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the skin of the testicle.
The skin of the testicle often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly.  Whoever wishes to treat it should place the patient on his back.
And he cuts all the relaxed skin on the thick skin, then brings the edges together. And if you wish, first sew the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, and make sure of the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our law.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book. However, I mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that it may be in the knowledge of the physician if he is asked about it, and so that he may know the treatment of one who is afflicted with it; and the other, that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, goats, cats, and the like of animals.  So I say that castration is of two types: either by crushing or by incision and cutting. As for that which is done by crushing, its method is that we seat the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften, and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are not perceptible to the touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, it is necessary to hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with the left hand, then tie the cords and make one incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles emerge, then cut them after you have skinned them, and do not leave any of the membranes on them except the thin membrane that is on the vessels.  This type of castration is better than that which is done by crushing.
Because crushing may leave something of the testicles, so the animal desires mating. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types. One is that which appears in the area behind the scrotum or in the skin of the scrotum between the testicles, in a shape like a woman's vulva, with hair; and urine may flow from that which is in the scrotum's skin.
As for women, there is one type, and it is above the vulva on the pubis like small male genitalia, protruding outward, one of them like a man's penis, and the two like testicles. And the treatment of the three types.
The two types in men and the one type in women: it is necessary to cut away that excess flesh until its trace is removed, then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals. As for the second type in men, from which urine flows, which is in the scrotum's skin, there is no action in it, nor is there any benefit from its removal.
Chapter seventy-one.
On the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris may exceed the natural amount, until it becomes large and unsightly, and it may enlarge in some women until it becomes prominent like in men and becomes fit for intercourse.
Then you should grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or with forceps and cut it off, and do not go too far in cutting, especially in the depth of the root, lest bleeding occurs.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And it may come out to the outside like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the "tail disease."  Then you should cut it off as you cut off the clitoris, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
In the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vulva is not perforated, or the perforation is small. This may be congenital or acquired.  The acquired kind results from a previously existing condition, and this may be from an excess of growing flesh.
Or a thin or thick membrane, and it may be in the depth of the uterus, or in its sides, or at its top or bottom. It prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and may also prevent menstruation. This is recognized by examination by the midwife.
If the obstruction is apparent and near, but if it is not apparent, then she should examine it with her fingers or with a probe. If the obstruction is from a thin membrane and is near the labia, then hasten to rupture it.  This is done by placing something like pledgets on the labia, then placing both thumbs together.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread apart. Then, she forcefully stretches the labia until the thin membrane is ruptured and the obstruction is opened. Then, she takes wool, soaks it in oil, and applies it to the area. The woman should have intercourse daily, lest the area close again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, then it should be incised with a broad scalpel resembling an aspen leaf. If the obstruction is from excess flesh, then suspend it with hooks and cut it. You should have styptics at hand that do not sting, such as acacia, mastic, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then, use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not close quickly. Keep it in place for several days and use a wick of dry linen, then treat it with other methods of healing until it is healed.  Other fleshy growths may occur in the uterus; these should be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor. A cancerous tumor in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red sores that appear in the vulva of women.
As for hemorrhoids, they are a swelling of the mouths of the veins, so that much blood constantly flows from them.  If hemorrhoids persist, they become warts.  Their types and signs have been mentioned in the classification, and we will mention them here.
What accepts treatment and what does not accept treatment. I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and do not appear to the touch, have no treatment with iron. Those that are at the mouth of the uterus and can be felt are the ones that are treated.
Then the woman should enter a cool room, and then the warts should be stretched with a hook or a rough cloth and cut from their roots. Then, if bleeding occurs, apply one of the styptics that stops bleeding without cauterizing.
Such as acacia, catechu, frankincense, and the like. Then she should raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding continues, seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, gallnuts, and the like. Then take barley flour, and knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it as a compress to her back. If the bleeding stops, otherwise, apply cupping to the loins and breasts without restriction. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of the mandrake, or the juice of Plantago, or the juice of the branches of the Verbascum, with a gallnut drink, and apply it to the area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pustule, it resembles the heads of the intestines, rough in appearance.  It should be cut as described for warts, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the suppuration which occurs in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may occur in the uterus, such as cancer, hard tumors, ulcers, fistulas, phagedena,  abscesses, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors.  I have described all these diseases, their types, and their symptoms in the classification.
It is necessary in this chapter to mention the hot tumor which occurs in the uterus, if it is among the tumors which collect over time, how its suppuration is done with iron.  It should be examined; if the pain of the tumor is initially sharp, with throbbing, inflammation, and fever,
and you see it red when touched, do not hasten to its suppuration, and treat it with what helps in ripening, until these symptoms subside. Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean on its back.
and bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, and her thighs should be apart, and her arms should be under her knees, and bind them with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the uterus is opened, the image of which comes later. If you want to use this instrument, you should measure with a probe the depth of the woman's uterus, lest what enters from the instrument be more than the depth of the uterus.
Then it harms the patient. If the instrument is longer than the depth, it is necessary to place pledgets on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus.  The screw which runs in the instrument should be on the upper side.
And you hold the instrument, and the attendant turns the screw until the cervix is opened. When the abscess appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and thin, it should be incised at its head with a wide scalpel. When all the pus is evacuated,
a soft pledget soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent should be placed in the wound. The pledget should be outside the incision in the cervix, and clean wool soaked in water in which alchemilla has been boiled should be placed externally on the uterus and pubis.
Then, after two days, treat with the ointment which is suitable for that until it heals.
And the uterus and wound may be washed by injecting with water and honey, then with water in which licorice root or long rhubarb has been boiled. Then return to the ointment, if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
Then you should refrain from treating it with iron, but treat it with what we mentioned in the section.
Chapter seventy-five.
On instructing midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural manner.
The midwife should first know the form of a natural birth.  Among its signs, if you see the woman straining downwards and yearning to breathe air and what she is experiencing from childbirth becomes easier for her.
And the fetus's emergence is quickened. Know that this birth is in the natural manner, and its emergence is head first with the afterbirth with it or hanging from its navel. If you see these signs, then you should press her abdomen so that the fetus emerges quickly.
For if it descends head first, the afterbirth descends with it and is cleansed of those impurities well. Whatever is besides this birth is unnatural and blameworthy, for the fetus may emerge on its feet, and its hands may emerge before its head and feet.
And one hand or one foot, or its head and hand may emerge, or it may emerge folded, and perhaps it turns onto its back and similar blameworthy forms.  It is necessary for the midwife to be skillful and gentle with all these things and to beware of mistakes and errors.
And I am explaining each form and how the means in it is so that you may be guided by that and stand upon it. If the fetus emerges head firstâ€”the natural emergenceâ€”and that is severe for the woman and labor is difficult for her, and you see that her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and order women to restrain her.
And pack her womb with cooked fenugreek and with moist ointments, then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers and cuts the afterbirth with it, or cuts it with her fingernail until the moisture within flows out, and she presses the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then it is necessary to inject the woman with fenugreek saliva with shirÄj ointment, then after the injection, order her to cough and make her sneeze with a sternutatory and hold her mouth and nose, for the fetus will emerge immediately. If the hands of the fetus emerge first, then it is necessary to return them gently little by little.
If they do not readily allow entry, place the woman on a stool and raise her legs upwards, then shake the stool on the ground, and the woman holds on so that she does not fall when shaken. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately, then he will emerge.  The emergence of the fetus on its feet: If the fetus emerges on its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards, then turn the fetus little by little until you straighten it, then take one of its feet and extend it little by little.
If they emerge to the thighs, order her to cough and make her sneeze with a sternutatory, then it will emerge. If it does not emerge as we described, otherwise return the fetus little by little until you put it into its natural form, then it will emerge smoothly. If it is prevented by all that we have described.
Take some saliva of the plant *khatmi*, and saliva of fenugreek, and shirÄj ointment, and dissolved gum, and pound everything together in a mortar well, then apply it to the vulva of the woman and the lower part of her abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the pubic bone.
If you see that her lower parts have softened, make a pessary for her of myrrh and she will tolerate it.  If the pessary remains in place for an hour, then seat her on a stool, then make her sneeze, and close her mouth and nose, and gently squeeze her lower abdomen, then the fetus will emerge immediately.  The emergence of the fetus on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, gently insert its hands little by little, then the woman lies on her back and hangs her hips downwards while lying on a bed and clasps her hands, then press gently on her abdomen little by little. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her lower parts are raised. If the fetus does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously, then press gently above the pubic bone little by little until the fetus ascends upwards, then the midwife inserts her hand and adjusts the fetus little by little.
And instruct the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The emergence of the fetus sideways, hanging by one of its hands; gently try to replace its hand. If you are unable to replace it, make the woman stand and have her walk. If she is unable to walk, let her lie down.
on her bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then use the poultice which I described from the saliva. If labor pains seize her, seat her on a stool, then work to replace the hand and adjust the fetus to its natural position, and make the woman sneeze and instruct her to strain until it emerges.
The emergence of the fetus on its back, extending its hands, and its face towards its mother's back. The midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then turn it little by little and gently adjust it. Once it is adjusted, apply to its head what we described of the ointment and poultices.
Then tell her to move little by little. If she does this, seat her on the stool and tell her to lean to the right side, then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutatory, then it will emerge.  The emergence of the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the placenta, tear the placenta with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture. If it adheres to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the navel lest it be strangled and die, then push the fetus inwards until it is in the proper position.
And tell the woman to strain repeatedly, then it will emerge easily. The emergence of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are frequently born, and three or four may be born and live, although this is rare. As for five, it is something outside of nature; they will not live at all.
And they are surrounded, even if numerous, by a single placenta, and they are separated by a membrane, a barrier between them, attached to the navel of each one. And they may emerge like a single fetus, naturally and easily, and they may emerge in a blameworthy manner as we described.
If one of them is difficult at birth and becomes as we said, in unnatural forms, then treat it as we described. And let the midwife be gentle and do what she does with gentleness and slowness, and she should contrive for every form with whatever means is available to her leading to safety.
Mention of what is conceived in the womb of fetuses that are aborted. One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived in the womb.  It has been confirmed to me that a woman aborted seven, and another fifteen, all conceived.
By the estimation of the All-Powerful, the All-Knowing. All of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing will happen to her someday, so she will meet it with what is appropriate.
Chapter seventy-six.
In the extraction of the dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus with what I mentioned in the section on treatment, and it does not come out, and you are compelled to work with your hand, then you should look. If the woman is healthy and strong, and has nothing of the symptoms from which harm is feared, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be tilted downwards and her legs raised, and a servant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, lest her body be pulled when the fetus is drawn out.
Then moisten her womb with moist ointments, along with the saliva of the dog and fenugreek and flaxseed. Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these ointments and saliva, then gently insert it into the womb, then search with it for a place to hook the fetus, and the appropriate places for that.
Look; if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, then hook into its eyes, or its nape, or its mouth, or its palate, or under its chin or collarbone, or in the areas near the ribs, or under the cartilages.
But if its descent is feet first, then [hook into] the pubis and the middle ribs.  It is necessary to hold the hook with the right hand, and to bend it between the fingers of the left hand, and to gently insert the hand and hook into some of those places that we have mentioned.
Until you reach something firm, then insert another hook opposite it, or a third if needed, so that the traction is even and does not tilt to one side. Then pull with a steady pull; and the pull should not be straight only, but should be moved in every direction.
So that its exit is made easier, as is done with a tooth when it is extracted.  Between these actions, the pull should be relaxed. If it becomes stuck on one side, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to rotate what is stuck. If some of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other places.
[The midwife] raises it slightly, doing this until the entire fetus emerges. If a hand emerges before anything else and cannot be returned because of its compression, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it and then pull it until, when it has entirely emerged, it is cut.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.  Thus it is necessary to act in the other hand and in the legs. If the head of the fetus is large and experiences difficulty in exiting, or if there is collected water in its head, then it is necessary to insert a hooked scalpel between the fingers.
And split the head with it so that the water exits, or extract it with the instrument called the *mishdakh*, whose image comes with the images of the other instruments in the chapter which follows this one.
Similarly, if the fetus is naturally large-headed, it is necessary to split the skull or crush it with the *mishdakh*, as we have said, then remove the bones with the hooks. If the head emerges and is compressed at the clavicle, then let it be split so that the moisture in the chest flows out.
For the chest then becomes constricted.  If the chest does not become constricted and egress is not achieved, then cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or has a swelling, then it is necessary to puncture it so that the moisture flows from it.
If the fetus is in a feet-first position, then pulling it is easy and straightening it towards the mouth of the uterus is simple. If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, then it is necessary to pull it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and split the abdomen or chest until what is in them flows out.
If all the limbs are extracted and the head is retracted and retained, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the uterus is open, let her insert her hand into the neck of the uterus and seek the head with it and pull it with her fingers to the mouth of the uterus, then insert a hook or two hooks into it and pull it with them.
If the mouth of the uterus has become constricted due to a hot swelling affecting it, then it is not necessary to be forceful on it, but it is necessary then to use the pouring of moist, fatty substances and to use poultices and sit in waters which relax and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, if it is possible to straighten it, then let what we mentioned for the live fetus be used. If that is not possible, then let it be cut and removed.  It is necessary not to leave any of the afterbirth inside at all.  When you have finished your treatment, use what we have mentioned for the treatment of the hot swellings which appear in the uteruses.
If bleeding occurs, then let her descend into astringent waters and use all other treatments. I have witnessed a woman who had conceived, and the fetus died in her womb, and then she conceived again upon it.
Then the other fetus also died. After a long time, a swelling appeared in her womb and it swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close, so I put on it some strong, drawing ointments.
Then a bone came out of the place. Then days passed and another bone came out. I was amazed by that, since the abdomen is a place where there are no bones. I judged that they were from the bones of the dead fetus, so I examined the wound and removed from it many bones from the bones of the fetus's head.
I continued doing this until I removed many bones from her, and the woman was in the best of health.  She lived like that for a time, a little pus being discharged from the place. And I have only mentioned this rare case here.
because in it there is knowledge and aid for what the skillful physician attempts with his hand in treatment.
Chapter seventy-seven.
In it are images of the instruments which are needed in removing the fetus.
Image of a screw to open the mouth of the womb.
This is the binder with which books are bound; it has two screws at the ends of the two pieces of wood, except that this screw should be finer than the binder and should be either of ebony or of boxwood, and the width of each piece of wood should be the thickness of two fingers.
And their width is about a finger and their length is a span and a half, and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two guides of the same material as the wood itself, which are fastened in them; their length is half a span or a little more, and their width is about two fingers or a little more, and these two guidesâ€¦
are those which enter the mouth of the womb to open it when you turn the screws; an image of another instrument for the same purpose, finer and lighter.

then seat the woman on a bed with her legs hanging down, spread apart between her thighs; then insert these two guides together into the mouth of the womb, while you hold the end of the instrument below between her thighs; then open your hand with the instrument as you do with the hooks, to whatever extent you want to open the womb. until the midwife does what she wants.
