All these instruments, the more numerous their types and the better prepared they are by the craftsman, the quicker his work and the higher his standing among people. Therefore, do not disdain any instrument that you have prepared, for there will surely be a need for it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
In removing the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, then you should instruct the patient to hold herself, then make her sneeze with a sternutatory and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, well and good; otherwise, use a forceps.
And pierce a hole in the lid and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as vervain, rue, dill, chamomile, wormwood, fumitory, and centaury; all or some of these medications.  Submerge them in water and place them over a fire. Then place a silver tube on the hole in the lid of the pot and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb, then make her sneeze as we said. Then the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to immerse her left hand in goose grease or mallow juice.
Then she should insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta. If she finds it, she should grasp it and pull it out little by little. If it is attached deep in the womb, then insert the hand as we described, until you find the placenta, then pull it out little by little, gently.
Lest the womb fall out with a strong pull, but it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then the amount of pulling should be increased. Then it will respond and be freed from its attachment. If the mouth of the womb is constricted, then we have described the treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, then avoid violence in pulling again.  But it should be bound from where it has come out to the mouth of the womb to the woman's thigh, then inject it with the tetrapharmacon, for it will cause it to rot after a few days.
And it will dissolve and come out. However, if it becomes putrid, a bad smell will rise from it to the stomach and head, and this will harm the patient. Therefore, suitable fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients have tried the fumigation of rue and dried figs.  The image of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated when menstruation and the placenta are retained, and so on:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper. The thin end is placed in the vagina, and the wide end is placed on the fire, and the incense is placed on the coals. It is held until that incense is gone, and then more is added.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the treatment of the non-perforated fistula.
Many boys are born with their anus imperforate; a thin membrane may have closed it.  It is necessary for the midwife to perforate that membrane with her finger; otherwise, let her incise it with a sharp scalpel, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then she should place wool soaked in syrup and oil on it, and then treat it with ointments until it heals. If she fears that it will close up, let her place a lead tube in the opening for many days and remove it whenever the child wants to defecate.
The anus may also become obstructed by the healing of a wound or a tumor.  This adhesion should be incised, and then treated as described above with medication and the lead tube.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower region.
Fistulas which occur in the lower region are a complication and thickening which occurs near the anus externally or internally from one side.  The fistula may be single or multiple.
If this complication becomes chronic, it opens and a watery white fluid or thin pus discharges from it. Some of these fistulas may penetrate to the bladder and the intestine, and some may not.  Those which penetrate may be recognized by the discharge of feces and flatus from them when the patient defecates.
Worms may also emerge from them.  There may be fistulas, if they are internal, which penetrate to the bladder and to the urethra. Some may penetrate to the hip joint and to the sacrum. A fistula penetrating to the rectum can also be distinguished from one which does not.
You should insert your index finger into the rectum and insert a thin probe into the fistula, made of copper or iron, if the fistula is not tortuous. If it is tortuous, then insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair into it.
Until you feel the probe or hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all and nothing emerges from the opening, neither feces, nor flatus, nor worms, as we have said, then know that it is not penetrating; proceed to treatment.
If the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urethra, the sign is the passage of urine from it and the inability of the area to heal with medication.  If it penetrates to the hip joint or to the sacrum, the sign is the probe reaching there.
If there is no tortuosity to the bone, and pain is present in that area, and pus constantly emerges from it, and treatment is ineffective, and it does not heal with ointmentâ€”all these penetrating fistulasâ€”then there is absolutely no cure for them.
And their treatment is a futile effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt it. As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, recovery is hoped for with cautery, as I describe and as I have tested.  This is to place the patient on his back between your hands.
Raise his legs and tilt his thighs toward his abdomen. Then insert a copper or lead probe, if there is a tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it toward the rectum, then you should insert your index finger into the rectum.
If you feel the probe in your finger, having penetrated openly without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is penetrating, so do not tire yourself with it, for there is no cure for it, as we said.
And they have said that it heals in some people rarely.  And of the treatments for which benefit is hoped for is to heat a thin cautery iron according to the size of the fistula, as has been previously mentioned, and insert it hot into the fistula until it reaches toward the rectum, then repeat it two or three times.
Until you know that all that excess, matted flesh resembling a bird's feather tube has been burned. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter until the burned flesh comes out, then treat it with healing ointments, if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment. But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger which is in the rectum, and there was a thick barrier of flesh or membrane between it and the probe, and you see the fistula near the surface of the skin, then incise the skin from the beginning of the fistula. And as you pass with the incision along with the probe, while it is in the fistula, until you reach with the incision where the tip of the probe ends, and the probe becomes free and falls out. Then cleanse that matted flesh resembling a bird feather tube, and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If blood overcomes you and prevents you from cutting that flesh, then the best and quickest remedy is cauterization with fire or a caustic medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning that excess flesh.
And cutting the blood and drying the moisture. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter or sulfur powdered with oil, until the area suppurates and that burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that grow firm flesh.
These are medicines that constrict and dry, for when it is closed, it has healed and you do not fear a return. If the fistula has reached the depth of the rectum and is far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a membrane of peritoneum or flesh, and it is near to penetration, then the work in it is only by way of hope and expectation, that is, you use one of three methods: either cauterize it as we said.
Or incise it until you reach its bottom so that you can insert the lint and treat it from up close. Do not extend the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the rectum and cause the patient to involuntarily pass stool. Then treat it as we mentioned; perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or penetrate that membrane and pierce it after incising it near the rectum as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then clean all the thickened flesh in the incision as far as you can, thoroughly.
Then suture the entire wound, including the incision and the upper opening, until it is closed and the fistula remains open inside the rectum, which is easier for the patient. And the fistula may be perforated in this manner.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is in a side of the rectum toward the surface of the body with the skin and the circumference of the rectum, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a shoemaker's needle, in this image:.
And insert a twisted thread of five or so threads into it, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom.  If it penetrates the margin of the rectum from the inside nearby.
Then bring the thread out through that hole by inserting your finger into the rectum and bringing out the end of the thread, and bring both ends together and tighten them and leave it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads is severed and falls away. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, penetrate it however you can, except that it should not be very deep, for you should not do that because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then do with it as we have mentioned until it heals.
The image of the hooked scalpel with which fistulas are incised: its hook is very sharp, and the other side is not sharp, lest you cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
Concerning the opening of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red bladders or like grapes, and there are small ones and large ones, and blood always flows from them.
And there may be one or many, and they may be outside the rectum and at its edges, except that those which are outside the rectum are, for the most part, less moist, with a yellowish fluid or a little blood flowing from them constantly, and they are the color of the skin.
The treatment of those which are inside the rectum is that you should order the patient to strain and bear down until the rectum protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then quickly seize them with hooks or grasp them with your nail, and then cut them at their base.  If the hooks do not hold because of their moistness and laxity,
then rub them with a rough cloth or a piece of a hard sponge, or pull them with your fingers, then cut them. Then, after cutting, sprinkle on them some sharp powders, so that they may act in place of cautery, or cauterize them as explained in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the rectum does not protrude, then inject the patient with an enema containing a little irritant, to wash what is in the rectum and cause it to protrude quickly when the patient strains. As for the hemorrhoids which are outside the rectum, their treatment is easy.
That is, you should seize them with your nail or hook them with a hook and cut them, then treat them as we have mentioned until they heal. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron, it is necessary that he use a ligature in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the hemorrhoid upwards and pass the needle through its base from the other side, and twist the ends of the thread below the needle while it is crosswise, and pull the hemorrhoid tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this to all the hemorrhoids, and leave one which you do not ligate.
so that the remaining blood may flow from it. Then place on the rectum a cloth soaked in rose oil or cyperus oil, and place it on the rectum and order the patient to be still, then leave him until they fall. When the hemorrhoids have fallen, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often arise from dryness of the feces and retention of the humors. If they become chronic and medicine does not work, it is necessary to scrape it with the blade of a scalpel or with your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper layer which prevents it from uniting is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more severely than the first time until it bleeds well and is excoriated, then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts, and corns.
A corn is a round nodule, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail. It occurs throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and the toes, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to incise around the corn, grasp it with a hook or forceps, and remove it from its root, then treat it. If you wish, cauterize it as previously described in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, nodular outgrowth on the surface of the body.
They frequently appear on the hands of children.  Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it off, or pierce it with silk thread or a hair until it falls off. If you wish, cauterize it with fire or a caustic drug, as previously described.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument I have described in the treatment of corns. As for corns, they are also a small, nodular, thick outgrowth on the surface of the body, extending deeply. If they are exposed to cold, a sensation similar to the sting of ants is felt in them.
They occur throughout the body, most often on the hands.  Its treatment is to take a tube of strong goose or eagle feather, and place this tube over the corn so that the tube's circumference surrounds it on all sides.  Then rotate the tube with your hand until you incise around the corn.
This will cause it to go deep into the flesh, and the corn will be removed from its root.  If you wish, you can do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be solid and twisted to facilitate its grip and rotation by the fingers. If you wish, cauterize the corn as previously described in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter eighty-three.
On the shapes of instruments used for injections in rectal ailments, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, china, or cast or hammered copper.  Small and large versions of this instrument may be made according to the users. Those used to treat young children should be small, and those for patients with narrow or painful rectums should be very gentle. Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, to which the bag is tied, is wide in this manner; it has a partition where the bag is tied, as you see, and its lower end, which enters the rectum, is solid, very smooth, and slender; on one side it has two holes, and on the other side one hole.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a gimlet or slightly larger, and the bag in which the medicine is placed should be made of an animal bladder or sheepskin, shaped like a small bowl, its diameter being a span.
It is pierced with many holes, arranged in a circle, the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger. Then a strong thread is inserted into those holes, spun from ten or so strands, and the bag is gathered with it like a bowl. Then, when the medicine is placed in it, it is tightly bound at the head of the syringe between two stones, and then it is injected. This is an image of the circular bag and the holes:
The method of injection is to fill the skin or bladder with warmed medicine, to a degree the patient can tolerate; beware of it being too hot or too cold. Then the patient lies on his back and raises his legs.
A sheet or blanket is placed beneath him, then the tip of the syringe is lubricated with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva or something similar, and it is gently inserted into the rectum. Then the attendant takes the bag in both hands and squeezes it firmly until all the contents of the syringe are emptied into the bowel.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient raises his legs to the wall. If the medicine stimulates evacuation, let him restrain himself as much as possible; and if he sleeps all night with it, it will be more beneficial.  If injected for diarrhea and evacuation is hurried, let the injection be repeated until he recovers.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful swellings in the rectum, let the syringe be very soft and smooth, smoother than those used to inject children. This is an image of a soft syringe with its skin stretched over it:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of wounds.
I have mentioned in the classification the treatment of what resembles cauterizations, and I will now describe their treatment by hand and by medicine completely.  I say that wounds differ according to the instrument with which the wound is made and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things that cause wounds are many, such as a blow from a stone, or a cut from a sword or knife, or a stab from a spear, stick, or arrow, and so on from many things.
As for wounds according to their location on the body, there is the wound that occurs on the head, or neck, or chest, or abdomen, or liver, and so on from the organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds, especially because the compound ones will be mentioned in the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone, I look; and if it is from a blow from a stone or the like, and only the skin is lacerated, and the wound is large, and I fear the occurrence of hot inflammation in the patient, then I hasten and incise the cephalic vein in that place. And do not delay that, and let blood be drawn from him according to his strength, if nothing prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is little, and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the injured part.
And warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and thick foods; if something prevents him from bloodletting, let him reduce his food, and not approach meats or drinks. And apply to the wound, if hot inflammation occurs in it, cotton soaked in rose oil alone, or with a styptic drink.
And if you are safe from hot inflammation, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh and the air has not altered it, the powder which I shall describe to you shortly; but if the air has altered it, then apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large, and is from a sword cut or the like, and its edges do not come together by themselves, then join it by suturing, as I describe in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs down, and its attachment is only by a small piece, cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines which produce firm flesh in place of the skin; and order the patient to nourish himself with foods which possess firmness, such as heads and broths, and the like.
If gangrene occurs in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, cut away all that is gangrenous and throw it away, then treat it.  If in these wounds there is an artery or vein which bleeds and the bleeding is not stopped by medicines, then search for the artery.
If you find it and it is not severed, sever it with the scalpel and tie it.  And if necessity demands, if what we have mentioned is not effective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, then their treatment is easy and simple; it suffices to treat them by sprinkling this powder on them while they are still bleeding.
before the air alters them. The preparation of the powder is: take one part frankincense, two parts acacia, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime; grind all together, sift it, and fill the wound with it, and bind it tightly so that it adheres well.
It will coagulate with the blood; then bind it from above with bandages and leave it; do not unbind it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not separate from it until it heals.  And the lime alone may do this if you do not have frankincense and acacia.
This action may also be done with alum in many large wounds if its application and binding are skillful, and the wounds are fresh and bleeding, provided the air has not altered them.  But if the air has altered them somewhat, especially in summer,
then the application of this powder is pointless; treat it as we have mentioned, by applying some ripening ointments or a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until the pus is discharged, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
But if a fracture occurs in the bone along with the wound, and it is slight, pull it with a forceps. I have described the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in the surgery of the neck. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a head wound if it is slight.
But if a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with things that strongly constrict, like verdigris and vitriol and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
And nothing cold should be placed on the wound at all, because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection is to the brain, which is the noblest of organs. What you treat it with should be soft medicines such as washed natron, moistened with fresh water many times, moistened with oil or rose ointment and tutty.
If it is also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and moist ointments. But if the wound is large, use sutures or bind its edges with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound is deep and a cavity forms at its bottom in which pus collects, lance it at its lowest point. If an artery is severed in the wound and you see its blood flowing, ligate it or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx and the windpipe is unharmed, join the edges of the skin by stitching over the windpipe.  Do not touch the windpipe itself, but straighten it and restore it to its natural shape, then tightly bind it and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, explore it; if there are bone splinters, extract them all, or as many as possible.  Leave those you cannot reach until the wound suppurates, heals, and their removal becomes easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we have described. If a bone in the wound suppurates and becomes necrotic, treat it as described in its chapter, in the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife and you see that it is deep, observe whether air escapes from it when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a mortal wound. If it is not deep and is fresh with blood, do not initially apply powder to it nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depths.
This will cause it to spread to the heart and kill the patient.  However, apply an absorbent ointment and dress it twice a day. If you do not have ointment, place an old cotton ball in the wound opening to absorb the secretions, and have the patient sleep on the wound.
so that whatever collects there may flow out. If three days or more have passed since the injury, and the patient has not experienced convulsions, a bad pulse, or shortness of breath, and you see that his other conditions are good, know that the wound is safe and treat it with cauterization and other treatments until it heals.
If it is difficult to see and the suppuration is continuous, know that it has become necrotic, and treat it according to its chapter.  If the wound is in the chest or back, treat it with the previously described stitching.
If it is large, use powders; if it is small, do not. If it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, explore the wound and promptly remove those fragments if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them until...
the wound becomes septic; then it will be easy to remove them. As for other wounds occurring in other members, their treatment is governed by what we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-five.
On abdominal wounds, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound which affects the abdomen may be large, or it may be small or medium.  A large wound may allow the intestine or several intestines to protrude; returning them and suturing is more difficult and arduous.
A small wound may also be difficult in another way, making the return more arduous.  If the intestine is not immediately returned to its place, it swells and thickens, making its return difficult. For this reason, a medium-sized wound is preferable, because returning the intestine is not as difficult with it as with the other two types.
Know that suturing the abdomen is done in four ways. Two sutures are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing all other wounds of the body.  The other two sutures are specific, used for all wounds, but they are most appropriate for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and some of the intestine protrudes and its return is difficult, that is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestine has been inflated by exposure to cold air.
If this is so, then it should be warmed by dipping a sponge or a damp cloth in lukewarm water alone, or in water in which dill, sweet basil, and spikenard have been boiled, and applying it to the intestine until the inflation subsides.
A styptic drink may also do this, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.  If you see that the inflation has subsided, wet the intestine with water in which comfrey or mallow has been boiled; for this will make its entry easier.
If its return is still difficult after this treatment, make a small incision in the wound with the instrument with which you incise fistulas. The first treatment, if successful, is better than incision. Incision is only resorted to after necessity and the failure of other methods. This is an image of the instrument:
One side is curved and limited, the other side is unlimited.  The thin end is not as fine as a lancet, but rather somewhat blunt.  It is an instrument resembling a small spatula, as you see. If the wound is wide and the intestine has entered, then it should be returned to its natural shape.
And its proper place, if you can manage it, is best. But if the wound is wide and is in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper abdomen, his head and chest should be higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always keep the side with the injury higher than the other side.  This should be done in large and medium-sized wounds.
As for small wounds, position it as best you can. Then have a skilled assistant ready to hold the entire wound with both hands, bringing its edges together, then revealing it bit by bit to the person performing the stitching.
This is the description of a common suture: take one needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then leave a space from the edge of the wound equal to the thickness of the little finger, and insert a needle without thread into the edges of the skin. along with the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin from the inside, until you pass it through from that side.  Having brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers, then tie a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly joined.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger again, and insert another needle, then tie it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as necessary until you have completed the entire circumference of the wound. Let the needles be of a moderate thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
Because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin, therefore they should be of moderate thickness.  And the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too close to the wound itself.
lest the flesh quickly tear and the wound open before it heals.  Do not space the sutures too widely apart either, lest the wound fail to unite.  Then cut off the ends of the needles lest they injure the patient in his sleep, and make for him pads of linen from each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of suture with needles is most suitable for small wounds, because one or two needles, or so, may suffice for suturing them.
As for the method of suturing the second type, the common one, it is that you gather with the suture the four edges, namely the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the peritoneum, at once with a needle carrying a twisted thread of moderate thickness, then when you have passed the needle through these four edges, return the needle to the side from which you began.
So that the thread falls interwoven from the top of the wound, so that the suture is according to the stitching of a pouch with which goods are tied.  And make the space between each stitch the width of a little finger. This suture is known to everyone.
With this suture, a wound was stitched in a man's abdomen; he had been wounded by a knife and the wound was more than an inch wide, and about two inches of the middle intestine had protruded. The wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
I replaced it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he recovered. He lived many years afterwards, managing all his affairs, while the physicians had judged that he would never recover at all.
And it is remarkable that I did not treat him with an ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines. I used to apply worn cotton to the wound twice a day and wash it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two kinds of special sutures, I mention it according to the words of Galen. It is that you begin the suture from the skin, and insert the needle from outside to inside. When the needle has passed through the skin and the muscle, going straight along the whole length of the abdomen.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side into which I inserted the needle, and passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, into the other edge of the peritoneum. Then, when I had passed it through, I passed it again through this same edge of the peritoneum, from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on that side, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through, pass it into the edge of the peritoneum which is on its side, until it passes through it entirely.
Then begin again from this same side and sew it together with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out through the skin near it. Then return it into that skin, and sew the edge of the peritoneum on the other side together with this edge of the peritoneum.
And bring it out through the skin on its side, and do that again and again, until you have sewn up the whole wound in that way. The general description of this suture is that you sew it like the sewing of furs together.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and leave it once from the other side until you finish. As for the second type of special suture which Galen also mentioned, this is his exact statement.
It is that you sew it in the manner that some physicians sew it, by joining each part to its counterpart which is naturally similar to it, so that they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge. This is done as I describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the edge of the peritoneum nearest to you from the outside, and pass it inwards into it alone, and leave the edges of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and pass it from the outside inwards into both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And pass it from inside to outside into the other edge of the peritoneum which is on the opposite side. This type of suture is better than the easy common suture, which is the suture mentioned earlier. This is Galen's exact statement. Some have said that the suture of the abdomen may be done with two needles. That is to say, you insert one thread into them and begin by inserting the needle from your side and pass it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side and pass it to your side, according to the suture of the basket-weavers.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then its suturing is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its rule in its treatment is the rule of all other wounds. That is, if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it, and you return the intestine and sew it up and secure it, then apply to it the medicated pledgets.
If the wound has been changed by the air, then apply to it some of the ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the threads fall out and the peritoneum and the intestines unite, then treat it as you treat other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, then apply to it, from when it begins to suppurate, old cotton and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals. For you do not need other treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound will affect the principal organs with pain,
then it is necessary that you dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or in rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and the armpit. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestinesâ€”and this often happensâ€”then inject him with a lukewarm astringent black drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound into its cavity. And know that what is thick of the intestines is easier to heal, and what is thin of them is more difficult to heal.
And as for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not readily heal from a wound that occurs in it at all. This is because of the abundance and size of its veins, and the thinness of its substance, and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. But if what emerges from the wound is omentum and you find it moist, then return it in the same way as you return the intestine.
If a period of time passes and it has turned green or black, then it is necessary that you tie it with a thread above the place which has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for in the omentum are veins and arteries, then cut below that ligature.
And leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to pull it and remove it when the omentum sloughs and the wound suppurates.  Mentioning the wound which occurs in the intestine. But if a perforation occurs in the intestine and it is small,
it may heal in some people, for I have seen a man who was wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach, and the wound became chronic and fistulous, discharging feces and flatus.  So I treated him, although I did not have hope for his recovery. And I continued to treat him gently until he healed and the place united. When I saw the place had united, I feared for the patient that a bad incident might occur within his abdomen, but no bad incident at all occurred to him, and he remained in the best of conditions. He correctly eats, drinks, copulates, enters the bath, and exerts himself in his service. And some experienced people have mentioned that if a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sutured in this manner.
And it is that large-headed ants are taken, then the lips of the wound are brought together, then one of these ants, with its mouth open, is placed on the lips of the wound. When it closes and tightens its mouth, its head is cut off, for it adheres and does not come loose. Then another ant is placed near the first.
And you continue to do that with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you return it and suture the wound. For those heads remain adhering in the intestine until the intestine is healed and recovers, and no harm at all occurs to the patient. And it may also be possible to suture the intestine with fine thread.
That which is drawn from the intestine of the animal adhering to it after it is inserted into a needle is that the end of this thread from the intestine is taken and gently drawn, then a fine twisted linen thread is tied to its end, then that thread is inserted into the needle. And in it is the thread of the intestine, and with it the intestine is sutured, then it is returned to the abdomen. And this type of suturing with ants and intestine is only by way of hope and expectation.
But if the rupture is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, then there is no remedy in it, nor recovery at all.
Chapter eighty-six.
In the treatment of catarrh and fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and longstanding and becomes an ulcer and does not heal, and continuously discharges pus that does not stop, is called, in general, in whatever member it may be, a fistula; and we call it catarrh, and fistula.
It is, in reality, a complicated, matted, hard, white thing, with no pain accompanying it, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather; and it is sometimes moist, continuously discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture may cease; and this moisture may be abundant or scant, thick or thin.
And I have stated in the classification, when mentioning wounds, that every ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow, is so because of one of nine reasons; one of them is either because of a deficiency of blood in the body, or because of its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is full of pus and filth; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter it discharges is poor in quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a corruption that has occurred in the region, of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a specific characteristic of the region.
Or because it is full of pus and filth; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter it discharges is poor in quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a corruption that has occurred in the region, of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a specific characteristic of the region. As occurred in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is naturally slow; or because it contains one or several bones.  I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the classification.
It is necessary here that I inform you of the treatment of fistulas and abscesses which is done by hand. Know that fistulas may occur in all parts of the body; some of them end in large veins and arteries, or in a nerve.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the composite joints, such as the hand and foot; and some lead to the vicinity of a principal organ, and so on.
Now, those fistulas which are of this kind are among the diseases difficult to cure, which do not accept treatment, and to occupy oneself with them is weariness and ignorance. But if there are fistulas which have not reached these places which I have mentioned, and I hope and trust in their treatment,
And you wished to arrive at the knowledge of that, then take a probe of copper or iron; if the fistula runs straight, examine it with this.  If there is a curve in the fistula, examine it with a probe of thin lead, for lead is soft in its substance.
It will become smooth upon entering and will bend towards the curve. If the fistula has many openings which you cannot discover with the probe, inject one of its openings; then the moisture which you inject will pass towards the other openings and flow out.
Then complete the examination in whatever way you can, to discover if there is bone or nerve, or if the bottom of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a single fistula with many openings. Ascertain all this to the extent of your ability by questioning the patient and by observing the pain when you touch the area.
And similarly from the signs and causes which occur in the swelling.  If you have ascertained all this with certainty, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  This is that you should look, for if it is...
the fistula is apparent, near, or in a healthy place far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the places I mentioned to you, then incise the fistula as I described before, and remove from it any adherent matter, corrupt flesh, and excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its opening of flesh or wart and the like, and treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep-seated and straight, then you should incise it deeply as much as you can, and then cleanse it of all its corrupt flesh.
Then use a pledget of lint soaked in strong medicine and insert it into the bottom of the fistula which you can reach with an instrument; do this repeatedly until the strong medicine destroys all that remains of corruption in the bottom of the fistula.
Then treat it with ointments which cause healthy flesh to grow until it heals. If it does not heal by this, cauterize it as described before. If the cause of the fistula is bone, and this is clear to you, then probe it and incise it as I told you, if nothing prevents you fromâ€¦
a nerve, or a major organ, as we said. If the bone is exposed and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until its corruption is gone, and then treat it with what will heal it until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues as beforeâ€¦
Know that you have not completely removed the corruption; expose everything again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with the utmost effort, then treat it. If it heals, well and good; otherwise, know that the corruption is in a deep recess. Expose everything again.
and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with the utmost effort. If the decayed bone is small and you can pull it out, then pull it out with fine forceps suitable for this.
If there are many bones, thoroughly extract them all and do not leave any behind, to the best of your ability. If something of the ligaments obstructs you, and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and flesh if it is there, and free the bones.
If you cannot extract the bones at the time, apply something to the wound that will cause it to fester, and leave it for days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones fester. Keep the wound from closing, the incision that you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place on it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For when those ligaments have festered and the bones have become loose, it will be easy to extract and pull them out.
If it is a single large bone, such as a bone of the leg or thigh, and so on, and only its surface is decayed, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and decay disappears, then dress the wound.  If a large part of it is decayed and the decay has reached the marrow of the bone, then it must be opened up.
And cut it all away to where the decay ends; then treat it until it heals. And I will tell you about gangrene that appeared in a man's leg, to make it an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause moving within his body until the materials reached the leg and swelled with a large swelling, and there was no external cause. Then time passed with the mistakes of the doctors until the swelling opened.
And much material flowed from it, and he was treated badly until the leg became gangrenous and many openings appeared in it, all of them discharging pus and bodily fluids. A number of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no one among them skilled in surgery.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg and the materials flowing from those openings with a great flow, and the man's body was emaciated and his color yellow.  Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
And I found that some of them led to others from all sides of the leg.  So I began and incised one of those openings until I uncovered some of the bone.  And I found it decayed, eroded, blackened, putrid, and perforated until the perforation reached the marrow. Then I spread what was revealed to me.
And I grasped the decayed bone. And I think that there is no decay in the bone other than that which I cut and spread, and that I have excised it. Then I proceeded to dress the wound with healing medicines for a longer period, but it did not heal. Then I returned and uncovered the bone again beyond the first uncovering.
And I found the decay connected to the bone.  Then I spread what appeared to me also of that decay. Then I attempted to dress it, but it did not heal nor unite. Then I uncovered it again, and I continued to cut the bone piece by piece and attempt to heal it, but it would not heal, until I cut about a span of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow. Then I dressed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and recovered.  And this repetition in its treatment and incision was necessary because of the weakness of the patient, his poor tolerance, and my fear for his death, because he frequently suffered from excessive vomiting and severe fainting. Then he recovered completely.
And in the place of the bone, firm flesh grew, and his condition in his body improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs, and he suffered no harm whatsoever in walking. For if a prominent bone in a part of the body is broken,
it should be spread in this manner: you take a ligature and tighten it around the end of the prominent bone, and you order someone to pull it upwards, and you make another ligature of thicker wool than the first ligature.
Then you tie it to the flesh under the bone, and you pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, and you uncover the flesh from the place which you want to spread, lest the saw injure the flesh, and you place a piece of wood or a board firmly under the bone from below.
Because if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupt bone. And the sawing should be a little above the place of corruption, lest there be corruption in the cavity of the bone which does not appear on its surface to the senses, so that you are compelled to saw it again.
If the bone is corrupt and is not prominent, but its parts are connected to one another, and the corruption is in its middle or in some part of it, then uncover the flesh from all its sides, then place the piece of wood from below, then saw it from the first side where the corruption is.
When you have finished sawing it, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the corruption, as we have said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupt joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy except excision. If the corruption is in the metacarpus of the hand or the metacarpus of the foot, the matter of its treatment is very difficult, but you should peel off the corruption however it appears to you, and excise it and cleanse it in whatever way you can.
And by whatever method it is straightened for you, provided that a vein or nerve does not obstruct you. And know that the instruments and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, their setting, their thickness and thinness, their size and smallness, their hardness and porosity. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare for each type of work an instrument similar to that work.
And know that the works themselves may indicate to you the type of instrument that you need, if you have long practice and knowledge of the arts of this craft, for [it is] from the mastery of the craft and witnessing various types of diseases. He may therefore devise for himself instruments similar to [those] for each disease, and I have depicted for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.

Chapter eighty-seven.
In the amputation of limbs and the sawing of bones.
Limbs may become putrefied, either from an external cause or from an internal cause.  If you treat this corruption with medicinal treatment and the treatment is not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing restraining it,
then it is necessary to amputate that member to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved thereby from death or from a great affliction which is greater than the loss of the member. And a sign of this happening to someone is that the member turns black until you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may become putrid after the blackness, until that putrefaction spreads to what is next to that member and attacks the whole body.  Therefore hasten to cut it off.  Likewise, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some insects, like a sea scorpion, or a snake, or a tarantula, and the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.  Likewise, if it has affected the hand, cut it off from the base of the forearm and do not allow the corruption to spread until it affects the bones of the arm.
Likewise, if it has affected the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow in the joint itself. If the corruption spreads and you see it reaching toward the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient.  And use other treatments as much as possible.
And likewise you do with the leg: if the corruption affects the toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints; and if it affects the metatarsus, cut off the whole foot; and if it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no recourse but to leave it and abandon the patient to death.  The method of cutting off or excising a member is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut off, and tie another ligature above the place.
And one assistant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another assistant pulls the upper ligature upwards, and you yourself dissect the flesh between the ligatures with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then it is cut or excised. And it is necessary that you place linen cloths on all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy tissue, causing the patient additional pain and hot inflammation. If bleeding occurs during your work, cauterize the area quickly or apply some styptic powder, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured member with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. And I will tell you of an example which occurred in a man's leg, this very condition which I describe to you: that is to say, there appeared in his leg blackness with burning like fire.
And this corruption first appeared in his toe, until it took the whole leg.  So the man, of his own accord, when he saw the corruption spreading in the limb, with the intensity of the pain and burning he felt, cut it off at the joint, and he recovered.
Then, after a long time, the same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand. He came to me, and I sought to restrain that corruption with the medicaments I applied to the hand after cleansing his body.  But the corruption did not abate and proceeded to the other finger.
Until the corruption seized the hand, and he urged me to amputate his hand. But I refused, hoping to restrain the corruption, and fearing also for his life if I amputated his hand, because the man's strength was failing.  When he despaired of me, he departed to his own country.
Then news came to me that he had, of his own accord, amputated his whole hand and recovered. And I have recounted this story to be of help in cases of this kind of disease, and to be a guide to follow and act upon.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On the treatment of fistulas and how to inject them with medicaments.
If a swelling occurs in one of the fleshy members, and the duration of the swelling is prolonged until it gathers pus, then bursts or is lanced, and all the pus within it comes out, and the place remains empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a rag, thinned, and its corruption has not affected the bone, nor the nerve, nor the ligament.  Therefore it deserves to be called a fistula, and not a sinus, unless its duration is prolonged until the corruption affects something of these members.
Then it is called a sinus or a fistula, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin as it goes around, especially if it has thinned and become like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere, and its corruption has not reached that extent, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. This is to look at the fistula; if it is large and the pus that flows from it is foul-smelling, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris in equal parts, gathered in a vessel and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens and reaches the consistency of honey. Then take what you need of it, thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the fistula, and bind its mouth, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then remove it by pressing; do this for several days until the fistula is cleansed and the stench is gone.  It may also be injected with ash water if this medicine is not available to you.  This is to take the ash of grapevine wood or the ash of oak wood and pour water over it.
Then filter it and inject it until you are certain that the fistula has been washed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then inject it with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and syrup mixed, for honey cleanses and washes, and syrup adheres to the fistula, especially if the syrup has astringent and drying properties.  When you have done this several times and are certain that the corruption of the fistula has gone, then inject it with something that will grow flesh in it.
Such as taking some palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and an astringent syrup, or inject it with some of the other medicines and ointments whose properties we have described in the chapter on ointments. If the mouth of the fistula is narrow and does not admit the injection tube,
widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place in it a pledget soaked in Egyptian ointment or theriac ointment until it widens.  Similarly, if the opening is also wide, bring its edges together with sutures, leaving only enough space for the injection tube to enter, no more.
Similarly, if the opening from which the pus flows is high up, make an incision in the lowest part of it so that the pus flows downwards, for if pus is retained in the depths of the fistula, it prevents the growth of flesh there.
If you cannot incise the fistula downwards as you wish, then position the limb so that the pus flows easily from it, as seems best to you, so that no pus at all is retained in it.
As for the agglutinative medicines which are applied externally to the fistula, and the manner of applying them, take one of the agglutinative ointments similar to what you want, and spread it on a linen cloth; let the cloth be large enough to cover the entire fistula. Then cut with scissors opposite the opening of the fistula a hole slightly larger than the fistula's opening.  Then spread some soft ointment on another cloth, to the size of the hole, and place it there, keeping it away from the fistula's opening so that the pus may easily escape.  Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
several days; as for that on the opening of the fistula, remove it at all times so that what has collected there of pus may come out, and so that you may know what flows from it of pusâ€”whether it is much or little, mature or immature.
Along with this also, examine the fistula itself: does its owner feel pain in it, or is there no pain?  Is there swelling, or not? If your treatment continues thus, and you see the material that comes out is slight at the opening of the fistula,
know that the fistula has adhered or is close to adhering. Then, resolve it on the morrow or on the third day. And when you have opened the wound, change the cloths to others, and renew the ointment.  If after a long time thin pus comes out of the fistula, do not despair of its closing.
Its healing is near, for some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others the opposite. If after many days there comes out from the fistula immature pus, know that the fistula has not closed. If the fistula is slow to close,
and its matter is prolonged, increase the drying medications, and let them be medications that are moist in their consistency and dry in their strength, like palm ointment if an excess of  colocynth has been added to it.  Among the most effective things to treat it with is to take palm ointment.
and mix it with rose oil, and sprinkle on it aged wine of moderate consistency, then knead it with it and use it.  He may do this same action if palm ointment is not available to you: if you cook honey until it boils and use it, or take myrrh, aloe, and costus, crush them all together, and sprinkle them on the honey while it is on the fire. Then spread it on a cloth and bind it to the fistula, or it is spread with the honey that you cooked until it thickens, and sprinkle on it the drugs and place on it the cloths and bind it.
Long rhubarb, the root of the asphodel, pounded  cress, and centaury may be used in such a case, singly or together, crushed and sifted, and sprinkled on the honey described above and used. If the matter of the fistula is prolonged and it does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And if it has affected the bones or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it as a fistula, as described above.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
On the treatment of the felon, the injured nail, the amputation of the superfluous finger, and the separation of adherent fingers.
A felon is an excessive growth of flesh under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and sometimes it grows in the other fingers. If its matter is prolonged and its treatment neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it becomes corrupt and purulent until it eats away the root of the nail, and perhaps all of it, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul smell and the end of the finger becomes broad and its color livid.  If you treat it with what we mentioned in the section and your treatment is not effective,
it is necessary to cut off with an iron instrument all the excess that grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterwards; for cauterization is very beneficial in this case, but if the bone is sound and the nail is also sound,
and the corner containing the nail has constricted the flesh inwards, causing it to pinch and injure it, it is necessary to place a thin probe under the corner of the nail that pinches the flesh and lift it upwards and cut that flesh gently.
And you should apply to what remains of the flesh of the cauterizing corrosives until it is all gone, then treat it with ointments until it heals. But if the corruption has affected the bone, it is necessary to cut that bone and remove it, for it will not heal as long as there is corrupt bone at all.
If you see the erosion and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the joints as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail is struck or bruised and severe pain occurs in it, it is necessary to bleed the patient first.
Then you should incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, an oblique incision from above downwards, and be careful not to reach with the incision to the flesh under the nail, for you will thereby cause the patient severe pain and it will be a cause of excess flesh growing in the place, then treat the place with what alleviates pain.
As for the extra finger that grows in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bones in it, or it may have a nail, and the growth of some of them is at the root of the joint of some fingers.
Or its growth may be in some of the finger joints.  Those that grow in some of the finger joints do not move, and those that grow at the finger joint may move. As for that which is only fleshy, its cutting is easy, and that is to cut it at its base with a wide scalpel.
As for that whose growth is at the root of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting it. As for that which grows in the finger at one of the joints, it is necessary to first cut its flesh in a circular cut to the bone, then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion that occurs between the fingers, it often occurs.  This may be from birth, or from the healing of a wound, a burn, or something similar. It is necessary to incise that adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural state. Then place between them a pledget or strip soaked in rose oil, lest it quickly adhere. Separate them, or place a thin lead plate between them until the area heals properly.
Similarly, if adhesion occurs between some of the fingers and the palm, incise that adhesion according to what is appropriate and improves the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On the excision of polyps.
Polyps are thick, twisted veins filled with dark, stagnant blood. They occur in most parts of the body, most commonly in the legs, especially in the legs of those who carry burdens, such as porters and carriers.  First, strong rubbing of the body with black bile should be used repeatedly.
Then, bleed the patient from the basilic vein. As for treating them with iron instruments, there are two methods: one is to incise and remove the black blood; the other is to extract the vein and remove it entirely. As for incision, it is done in this manner: first, bathe the leg with warm water thoroughly.
Until the thick, turbid blood is softened. Then, tie the patient's leg from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage. Then, incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then, draw out the black blood with your hand, from the lower leg upwards and from the upper leg downwards, until you have removed the amount of blood you deem sufficient, or as much as the patient's strength can bear. Then, bandage it and instruct him to avoid foods that produce black bile.  The purging and bloodletting should be repeated.
When the veins are filled and this harms the patient. As for extraction, it is done in this manner: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair. Then, take him to the bathhouse and bathe his leg with hot water until it reddens and sweats, or he performs vigorous exercise.
If a bathhouse is not available, heat the limb. Then, incise the skin along the vein, either at its end near the knee or at its lower end near the heel. Then, open the skin with hooks and dissect the vein from all sides until it is perceptible to the touch.
When it appears, you will see it as dark red. Once it is freed from the skin, it will appear like a tendon. Then, insert a probe underneath it until, when it is raised and emerges from the skin, you hang it on a smooth, blunt hook. Then, make another incision near that incision, about three fingers wide.
Then, dissect the skin from over the vein until it appears. Then, lift it with the probe as you did before and hang it on another hook as you did initially. Then, make another incision, or many incisions if necessary.
Then, extract it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel. Then, pull and extract it until it emerges from the second incision. Then, pull it to the incision above it and do this until you pull it from the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, until, when it is all out, you cut it.
If it does not yield to pulling and extraction, insert a needle with strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull. Insert the probe underneath and twist it with your hand in every direction until it comes out. Be careful that it does not break, for if it breaks, its extraction will become very difficult for you.
And harm will befall the patient from it. When you have extracted it all, place wool soaked in a potion and rose oil or olive oil on the site of the incisions, and treat it until it heals. If the varix is constricted with convolutions and a twisting towards different directions,
and is not straight as we said, then it is necessary that you incise it at each part of its convolutions and places of twisting, then hang it with hooks until you extract it all. And be careful when incising it that you do not cut or injure the vein, for this will make its extraction difficult for you, so exert yourself carefully.
Image of the incision with which the varix is incised.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not be hooked like other hooks, nor should it be sharp-pointed, lest it injure the vein. It should be thick, smoothly curved, for if it is thin, it will cut the vein with its thinness; rather, it should have a considerable thickness, as we said.
Chapter ninety-one.
On the removal of the varicose vein.
This vein originates in the legs in hot countries like the Hejaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot, arid countries with little fertility.  It may also originate in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its generation is due to an infection that occurs under the skin.
As happens in the interior of bodies with snakes and worms, and the gourdâ€™s [i.e., pumpkinâ€™s] ulcer and the worms generated between the skin and the flesh.  The sign of the beginning of this veinâ€™s occurrence is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the place suppurates, then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that suppuration.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal; so when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams, and tied, leaving the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the remainder; do not cut it from its root before it has all emerged, for if you cut it, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing a swelling and corruption in the place and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be treated carefully and pulled out little by little until it is completely removed and nothing of it remains in the body.  From this vein, in some people, something may emerge that is five or ten cubits in length.
And it has reached me that it emerged from a man at twenty cubits.  If it breaks for you while you are treating it, insert a probe into its hole and probe it thoroughly along with the body until all that is in it of matter is emptied, and try purifying the place with medicines for days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself, producing many openings, and from each opening a branch emerges. Treat it as we mentioned in the classification and as previously described.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
Concerning the incision for the worm that is generated under the ear and is called the cow's disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries the cow's disease, because it often affects cows. It is a single, small worm that is generated between the skin and the flesh, and moves throughout the body, ascending and descending.
It is perceived by touch as it moves from one member to another until it breaks through the skin at some point and emerges.  Its formation is from the corruption of some humors, as happens with worms, snakes, and the gourd-like growth in the abdomen.
The harm to be expected from it is that if it moves in the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, it may break into it and emerge, destroying the eye. This often occurs. If you want to treat it and remove it, this is done when it is moving and is perceptible to the touch.
Then it is necessary to tie what is above and below it tightly with a bandage, then incise over it and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply cautery with fire to the area until you burn it. The greatest harm to be expected from it is its damage to the eye, as we have said.
If you see that it has reached the head near the eye, then tie tightly below it on the forehead, then incise over it and remove it.  The patient should be treated with purgative medicines to cleanse the body of bad, corrupt humors, and should be careful to avoid foods that produce corruption.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
Concerning the incision for the disease known as the ulcer.
This disease, which in our country is called *nafir*, is a pain that appears in some organs and then moves from one organ to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in the desert. She uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm. After a short time, I saw that swelling moving along the ulna like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
As it moved from place to place, the pain disappeared from that spot and settled in the shoulder. Then, after a while, it ran throughout the body until it reached the other arm. Then she told me that it circulated throughout her entire body, as I had witnessed.
I was amazed at the speed of its movement from one organ to another. I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this way, except that I had seen people whose pain moved from one organ to another, but I had not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I could only explain it by the fact that the woman was a desert dweller, with a dry body and exposed veins; therefore, that moving wind was perceptible.  It would not appear in the same way in people of comfort, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you want to treat it, and the patient feels pain, and if it appears to you visibly as we have mentioned, then bind above and below it with a bandage and incise it until that congested wind escapes, and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by means of things that dispel and disperse the wind, such as the seeds of rue and the seeds of  *skombion* and similar medicines.
Chapter ninety-four.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places in the body where they lodge. As for their differences according to their types, there are large and small ones, hollow glass ones and solid glass ones, and some have three points and some have four.
Some have barbs and some have splinters. As for those according to the members in which they lodge, they are of two kinds: either they are in principal hollow members such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like; if an arrow lodges in one of these members and you see the signs of death which I shall describe to you later, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for in most instances death will overtake the patient. But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not hidden deep within the member, then extract it and treat the wound.  And of the signs [of injury to] the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane covering the brain, then there results from that a severe headache, shivering, and dizziness.
But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not hidden deep within the member, then extract it and treat the wound.  And of the signs [of injury to] the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane covering the brain, then there results from that a severe headache, shivering, and dizziness. and redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, convulsions, delirium, vomiting, and perhaps blood comes from the nostrils or ears, and perhaps speech is lost and the voice is gone, and a white moisture resembling gruel comes from the place of the wound, and a watery substance like the water of flesh flows from it.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the patient and from extracting the arrow if it has not been extracted. As for the signs [of an arrow] striking the heart, if it is near the left breast and you feel it as if it were driven into something solid and not into something empty;
and perhaps the arrow has a movement resembling the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and this is followed by coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting; then know that death is inevitable. And a sign of the arrow if it wounds the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels next to the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily and desires to inhale cool air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm which is in the chest, then it will be near the small ribs, and the breathing will be labored with severe pain.
And he sighs and all the members of the shoulders move. And if the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain follows and blood resembling the liver in its redness comes from the wound. And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from the food comes from the wound.
And its condition is evident. And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and becomes embedded in it, and something of the excrement, or pus, or intestine which has been ruptured comes from the wound, then there is no hope in treating it or in extracting the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine comes out;
and something protrudes from it to the outside and the pain intensifies for the patient, then know that he is dying. As for the other members, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, vertebrae of the back, clavicle, thigh, leg, and similar members, they are mostly unharmed.
When the arrow does not encounter an artery or nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned. And I tell you some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may thereby be guided in your treatment. That is, an arrow once struck a man in the corner of his eye at the root of the nose.
So I extracted it for him from the other side under the lobe of the ear, and he recovered and no harm befell his eye. And I extracted another arrow for a Jew which had struck him in the lobe of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had hidden itself, and I grasped only its small point which is attached to the wood.
And it was a large arrow from the composite arrows, square-headed iron, smooth, without barbs.  The Jew recovered and no ill befell his eye. And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arabic.
and it is the one with barbs, so I made an incision for him between the two jugulars, and it had sunk into his throat. So I gently worked at it until I extracted it, and the Christian was safe and recovered. And I extracted another arrow for a man which had struck him in the abdomen, and we judged that he would die from it.
But when thirty days or so had passed, and nothing in his condition had changed, I made an incision over the arrow and worked at it and extracted it, and he recovered and no ill befell him. And I saw a man struck by an arrow in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
Then after seven years the arrow emerged at the root of her thigh. And I saw a woman who had been struck in her abdomen by an arrow, and the wound had closed, and the arrow remained, and nothing in her condition changed, nor did she experience any harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man struck by an arrow in his face, and the wound closed and he felt little pain from it. And there are many similar cases. And I extracted an arrow for a man, one of the Sultan's guards, which had struck him in the middle of his nose, inclining slightly to the right side.
And the whole arrow was hidden. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I explored it with a thin probe, but I did not feel it. He felt a stinging pain under his ear on the right side of the incision.
I hoped that this stinging pain was from the arrowhead, so I dressed the wound with a dressing possessing drawing and ripening power, hoping that the area would swell and the arrowhead would appear, so that I could incise it. But nothing happened in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached that place.
I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened. Then the wound closed, and the patient despaired of its removal for a time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me.  So I applied a strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it, and I felt the thin point of the arrow that adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medicine until the arrowhead appeared to us clearly.  It took about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not yield to removal.  I continued to gently manipulate it and devise ways with various instruments until one day I grasped it firmly with forceps, as its image appears at the end of the chapter.
And the physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound united, and the patient recovered completely, without any harm whatsoever.  And I tell you the method of removing some arrows so that you may use this as a measure and guide for what I have not mentioned.
