Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description nor encompassed by a book.  Rather, the skilled craftsman judges by the little from the much, and by what is present from what is absent, and he deduces a new action and a new instrument for strange emergencies.
If we descend to this craft, then I say that arrows are removed from the members in which they are lodged in two ways: either by pulling from the place where they entered, or from the opposite direction, and those that are removed from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, and it is pulled and removed. If it does not yield to removal from the place where it lodged, then you should leave it for several days until the flesh around it putrefies, so that it is easy to pull and remove.  Likewise if it is lodged in a bone.
And if it does not yield to removal, leave it also for days and return to pulling and moving it every day, for it will come out.  If it does not yield to removal after days, then you should bore around the arrow in the bone itself from every direction with a fine drill until you widen [the hole] for the arrow, then pull and remove it.
If the arrow lodged in the bone of the head has penetrated deeply into one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of those symptoms I mentioned to you appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after several days.
then persevere in pulling out the arrow. If it is very firmly lodged and does not yield to pulling, then use the drills around the arrow as I described to you, then treat the place until it heals.  And if the arrow has disappeared in a place of the body, and is hidden from sensation, then probe for it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it with some instrument suitable for pulling it. If you cannot reach it because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is neither bone, nor nerve, nor vein,
then incise it until the wound widens and you can reach the arrow so that you extract it. If it has barbs, grasp them, and free the flesh entangled in them from every side by any means you can. Persevere, and if you cannot free the flesh, break the barbs and twist them until you free it.
And when you attempt to extract the arrow, wherever it may be, use the twisting of your hand with forceps in all directions until you free it. Be extremely gentle, lest the arrow break and it becomes difficult for you to pull it out. If you cannot manage it at the time,
leave it for some days until the flesh around it becomes putrid, then try again; for it will then be easier. If bleeding occurs, use what we mentioned of treatment in its chapter, and take care to avoid cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon; use skill.
By any means you can, free the arrow; let this be done gently and carefully and firmly, as I described to you. It is necessary that when you pull out the arrow, you position the patient in the same manner he was in when the arrow entered, for this is most appropriate.
If you cannot do this, use whatever positions you can. As for an arrow which emerges from the opposite side, either a part of it will have emerged externally, or you will feel the tip of the arrow just beneath the skin, near the surface, and see it protruding.
Make an incision over it; let the incision be sufficient for the forceps to grasp it, then pull it out, for it will easily come out. If it is stuck in a bone, twist your hand circularly until the arrow affects the bone and makes space for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for some days, then try again until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still there, push it with it. If the shaft has fallen off and you wish to use pushing, then insert the hollow instrument so that its cavity enters the end of the arrow, then push it with that.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters that cavity, for the arrow will easily come out by this means. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize all the flesh into which the poison has entered, if you can, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the abdomen, or in the bladder, or in the flank, and it is near enough to be felt with a probe, and you can incise over it, then incise and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it needs stitching, then treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps with which arrows are extracted:
Their ends are like a bird's beak; they are made like a file, so that when they grasp the arrow or anything, they do not let go.  And various sizes, large, small and medium, may be made of them, each according to the size of the arrow, and the width and narrowness of the wound.
Image of the hollow pusher:
Image of the solid pusher:
This has a solid end like an awl, to make it easier to insert into the hollow arrow and push it.
Chapter ninety-five.
On the incision of veins.
The veins which it is customary to bleed in the body are thirty veins; of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the "whispering" veins, and the two arteries in the temples which are visible, and the two veins in the corners of the eyes known as the "seeing" veins.
And the vein rising in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip â€“ these veins are known as the "jeharak" â€“ and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins which are bled in the arm and hand, they are five veins: one of them is the cephalic, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the "head vein," and the median vein which is the middle vein and is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the "body vein.
And the basilic, which is situated on the inner side and is also called the axillary, and the common people call it the "belly vein," and the vein of the forearm, which is situated on the ulna, and which adheres to it, and which appears clearly above the thumb, and the salem.
And this is the vein which is between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one of them is that which is below the hollow of the knee on the outer side, and the second is the saphenous, and its place is at the heel on the inner side, and the vein of the nerves, and its place is at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to them. As for the two veins behind the ears, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic colds, migraine, ringworm, and chronic, bad sores of the head, and their method of bleeding is as I describe.
And that is to shave the head of the patient and rub his head well in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the two veins and their location appear behind the ears in the two low places of the head, then you should feel them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, there you should mark with ink.
Then you take a lancet-like scalpel, which is known as the *nashal*, then you insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone, then you lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin a cleanly severed cut, and the length of the cut should be the amount of two joined fingers.
Or something similar, and you let out the amount of blood that you want, then you bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal.  And the cutting of them and their method has been mentioned in the beginning of the book. As for the two arteries which are in the temples, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and sharp, excessive flow of humors into the eyes.
And their method of bleeding is as I describe to you: the patientâ€™s neck is constricted with a turban until the veins appear clearly to the touch and their pulse is apparent under your finger; then you mark with ink. Then you lift the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
And you insert the *nashal* scalpel from below and lift the vein upwards and sever it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out blood according to your need, then you loosen the constriction of the patient's neck and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it and bind it tightly from above and leave it until it heals.  Their mention, cutting, and cauterization have been detailed at the beginning of the book. As for bleeding the vein of the forehead, its benefit, after bleeding the temporal veins, is for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, sores, and erysipelas.
And its method of bleeding, as I describe to you: you constrict the patient's neck with a turban until the vein appears; then you take the instrument called the axe, and this is its image:
You place the projecting spike which is in the head of the axe on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or something similar, and you let the blood flow to the extent you desire; then you loosen the constriction of the patientâ€™s neck and bandage him until he heals.
And it may be opened with a wide scalpel, except that the scalpel should not be sharp-pointed like other scalpels, but rather slightly wide-ended, and you incise it obliquely, because the bone is near, and the scalpel may break in it if it is thin.
As for the two veins in the temples, their benefit is for eye ailments such as ringworm, inflammation, and cataracts, and facial diseases. As for their method of bleeding, it is that the patient's neck is constricted with a turban, then you bleed them while you are standing over his head; and let the incision be slightly oblique lengthwise with a small, slightly wide scalpel.
For there is no flesh in that area; if the scalpel is thin-pointed, it may break. Then you let out the amount of blood you need and place cotton on them and bind them for one night, then unbind them.  As for bleeding the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headache and facial diseases such as the red erysipelas which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic.  The method of bleeding it is that the patient constricts his neck, then you hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long scalpel.
And you pierce it in the middle of the nasal tip itself, between the nasal septum, straightly, because the vein does not appear to the touch there, for the blood will emerge immediately.  You should push your hand with the scalpel slightly and let out the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night, for it clots quickly.
As for the jugular veins, the benefit of bleeding them is for shortness of breath, the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, ringworm, bad ulcers and sores.  The method of bleeding them is that the patient is constricted beneath them in his neck with a ligature, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you bleed the vein lengthwise with a somewhat wide incision, then you let out a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see as necessary. Then you do likewise with the other vein, then you untie the ligature.
And you constrict the two veins moderately, lest the patient suffocate, and you leave him until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the veins of the temples, the benefit of bleeding them, after bleeding the head, is that it benefits from canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, cracking of the lips and bad ulcers.
which are in and around the nose.  The method of bleeding them is that you seat the patient before you and constrict his neck with a turban, then you turn back his lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see to the right of the lip and the other to the left of it, and you ascertain them by their blackness.
And that is also because around them are small, black veins; you cut them with a severed cut. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, then aim to cut the largest and most obvious of them.  Likewise, do you act in the veins which are in the lower lip.
As for the two veins which are under the tongue, the benefit of bleeding them after bleeding the head is for the quinsy which occur in the throat from disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth. The method of bleeding them is that you seat the patient between your hands facing the sun and raise his tongue and look under the tongue, on one side a vein and on the other side a vein, and their color is towards blackness.
Then you bleed them, and be careful not to exaggerate in cutting them, for beneath them are arteries, and perhaps there will result a bleeding of blood from those arteries. As for the three veins which are bled in the elbow, they are those in which the custom has been to bleed them in all people, and their bleeding is done in two ways.
Either by insertion with a wide,  lanceolate or olive-shaped scalpel to the depth, or by incision with a knife-shaped scalpel, which is the lancet [and this is its picture], a picture of the wide lanceolate scalpel:
It is wide as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins which contain thick, turbid blood. And this is a picture of the olive-shaped scalpel:
And this scalpel is less wide and thinner at the tip, suitable for bleeding thin veins which contain thin, yellowish blood. And this is a picture of the lancet scalpel:
This lancet which is suitable for incision, and there are types of it, wide and narrow, according to the size and narrowness of the veins also. And one may infer from this to others, and it is well-known among the artisans.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from what is near the chest and abdomen. It is necessary for the phlebotomist, when bleeding it, to be cautious and be on the alert, for under it is an artery; if he makes a mistake and goes too deep with the scalpel, he will cut that artery.
Then bleeding occurs, therefore it is necessary that its bleeding should not be done with the incision scalpel, but its bleeding should be an incision with the lancet. If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to avoid it and turn to another, or seek some of its branches.
Or bleed in its place the vein of the arm, for it is clear, and incise it with the lancet scalpel as we said. If you wish to bleed it itself, then before tightening the arm, it is necessary to feel the place until you recognize the place of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then you bind the arm and incise the vein with a sharp incision with the lancet scalpel as we said, and take care that the incision is away from the place of the artery. And when you see, upon tightening the ligature, a swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, at the time of bleeding, spurt like the urine of a boy, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery. Then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the blood stops, and it often stops, then tighten the arm.
And leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be on his back and not move for several days until it heals. If the blood does not stop and overcomes you, and you do not have medicine at hand, then amputate the artery if it appears to you, for its ends contract and the blood stops.
Or take a pistachio shell and split it, and take one half and bind it tightly on the place of the vein with a ligature and bandages until the next day. If the blood stops, otherwise treat it with what was previously mentioned of placing styptics, and stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the tightness of the ligature on the arm, let him know. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the top of the head and the lower body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
And it is necessary for the one who bleeds him to be on his back when bleeding him, for there is a nerve beneath it. If he exceeds in the insertion of the lancet and hits the nerve, numbness occurs in it which is difficult to cure, and perhaps it will not heal at all.  And this nerve is often perceptible to the touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and not apparent, then you should make your incision in it obliquely and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the head.
And it benefits from diseases of the eyes, and it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish to bleed it, to insert the olive-shaped lancet or the wide, basil-shaped lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
Except that it is necessary for you, when bleeding, to avoid only the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft place.  It does not harm him if he is not hit by the first strike to be bled again several times, except that it might swell in some people if it is not bled on the first strike.
But that swelling does not harm him at all. As for the manner of bleeding and its complications and what should precede in its treatment, first of all you should know that bleeding is either used only to preserve health.
And either it is used in diseases, and that which is used to preserve health and maintain it and to guard against the occurrence of diseases is that bleeding should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, I mean the cephalic, the median, and the basilic, and that bleeding should be in early spring if signs of plethora appear.
And the bleeding should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of the day have passed.  As for the bleeding that is used in diseases, it does not have a limited time, but whenever need and necessity require it, day or night.
And at every hour and at every time.  And children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years, nor should the elderly who have passed sixty years be bled.  If someone intends to bleed, for whatever reason.
Then he should cleanse his bowels before bleeding with a gentle enema if there is much retained feces, lest putrid excess from the intestines be drawn into the veins during bleeding, harming the principal organs. And he should not bleed the feverish, nor the drunkard, nor the intoxicated until that passes from them.
And bleeding should also be avoided after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive coitus, exertion, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything that weakens the strength, whether physical or mental. Then look to the thinning of the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
With foods, drinks, and medicines if he can, then he enters the bath if nothing prevents him, or he engages in some exercise so that the blood is thinned, and he makes his bleeding in the middle of the day as we said, and he intends to free his chest that day from all bad psychological effects.
Such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical effects such as excessive exertion and fatigue, coitus, and the like.  And he prepares for his session things that people are accustomed to using, such as various perfumes, fragrant herbs, amusements, and the like, each person according to what he can.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion higher than the cushion on which the patient sits, then he extends his arm and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the ligature with the fillet and twists it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
Because if the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening, it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is relaxed, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then after tightening, it is necessary for the patient to rub both his hands together.
Until the veins swell and become perceptible to the touch, then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then he places the index finger of his left hand on the vein itself, slightly below the place he wants to bleed, lest the vein slip away and avoid the incision. For some veins are found like a tendon, slipping away during phlebotomy, and some are filled with wind, so when the lancet is placed on them, they sink under the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein, and if it does open it, it is only a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be careful and patient in all these matters, then he lowers the lancet; if the vein opens at that time, otherwise, he repeats it again slightly below or above that place quickly if the place is not swollen.
If the patient swells or is distressed, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the ligature, for it may cause a hot swelling and do not let him enter the bath, then he may repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes.  If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small and the flow of blood is thin.
And you fear that the desired amount of blood will not come out, then return the lancet into the same hole gently and straight, and increase the opening slightly, and do that quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place may swell with a small opening.
If you see that it has swollen, do not repeat it at all, for it will not help you; place something of oil dregs on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood, and it is better in this place than the oil itself and other ointments as well.
Therefore, use oil dregs in all your phlebotomies when the flow of blood is difficult; and the theriac Pharun and the shakhsanaya may do that if one of them is placed on the place, for the blood becomes thin and dissolves if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of phlebotomy â€“ and this often happens, especially to one who has been phlebotomized only that one time or if the opening of the vein was small â€“ then hasten to place on the swelling a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water and bind it for an hour, for it will resolve.
And it is necessary that you do this after the complete exit of blood from the vein itself or from another vein; if something of blackness or greenness remains in the place after days, it does not harm; if you wish, apply something of dissolved aloes and myrrh to it.
Or something of the juice of the foudanj and the like.  Often a swelling and a pustule occur when phlebotomizing the basilic vein; place your hand on it; if you find it softens when you touch it, then that is a bad pustule, so beware of putting anything on it of what we mentioned.
For perhaps arterial blood will bleed from it, but bandage it with something constricting to solidify the place, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.  And it is necessary that you extract from each person blood according to his strength and what appears of the color predominating in the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow until it reddens; and likewise if you see it thick, let it flow until it thins; and likewise if it is acrid, until its acridity goes away.  And it is necessary for one who is full-bodied and strong and needs to have blood extracted all at onceâ€¦
â€¦to widen the phlebotomy and the lancet to be wide; and for one who is weak, the opposite of that. And it is necessary that you extract it in several times, and that the hole be narrow. And the best thing to use in phlebotomy is that which is sharp, well-honed, incised, not punctured.
And this type of phlebotomy is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve; and it is better and safer than phlebotomy across and lengthwise. And whoever is accustomed to fainting during phlebotomy, it is necessary that you give him to eat before phlebotomy something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or oxymel.
If he is feverish and blood is extracted from him in three or four times, then if he is cold-natured, let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in hydromel or in honey-sweetened drink flavored with herbs or in a sweet-smelling rose-scented drink.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy and its cause is the excessive outflow of blood, then it is necessary that he be given meat broth and a light rose-scented drink; and he should use fragrant galbanum, and rub his chest with it, and use all else that we mentioned in the section on fainting.
which occurs from vomiting. And as for one who wishes to relax his arm and to draw blood a second time, it is necessary for one whose phlebotomy was for copious evacuation and whose strength is weak that the blood be drawn little by little, according to his strength, on consecutive days.
And as for one who wishes to relax his arm and draw blood a second time and his body is strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy. And as for one who wishes to draw blood from his body to the opposite side to which it has inclined, it is necessary to relax him on the second or third day.
And as for one in whose body the blood is much, having become hot and sharp and causing fever, it is necessary that blood be drawn from him at once, and that a large amount be drawn, and that the opening be widened until fainting occurs, after you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy. And that you place your hand on his pulse during the flow of blood, lest death occur instead of fainting; for this often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs. And it is not necessary, if you wish to relax the arm and draw blood a second time,
if the mouth of the vein has closed and the exit of blood is difficult, that you prick it forcefully or twist it strongly, for that is very bad; rather, either you leave it until you phlebotomize it a second time, or you remove with the blade of the lancet what has solidified of the blood in the mouth of the vein.
Or you apply to it something of salt dissolved in water, or you apply to it something of theriac or xylobalsamum, and prick it gently until the blood comes out; and if the vein has swollen, leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides; and if necessity demands drawing blood a second time, and there is no alternative, then either you phlebotomize above that place, or you phlebotomize in the other arm or in another vein.
As for phlebotomy of the basilic vein, it is phlebotomized instead of the cephalic and median basilic veins if they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them.  Its method of phlebotomy is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then it is tied above it with a ligature, slightly and moderately.
Then you phlebotomize the vein slightly obliquely, neither transversely nor longitudinally, and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be slightly above the joint of the hand. If the exit of blood is difficult, return the hand to the vessel of hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches your need. If you are in the summer months, you may dispense with returning the hand to hot water. The practice of letting the blood flow into hot water is most often done in winter.  Phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins. Because there is no underlying vein or nerve. As for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments.  Its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no underlying vein or nerve. As for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments.  Its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very perceptible to the touch. Then you phlebotomize it slightly obliquely; and even if you cut it completely, that will not harm it at all. Be careful not to push your hand deeply with the lancet, for under it are the nerves of the fingers, and the place is bare of flesh. Then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it flowing. For if you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will congeal in the opening of the vein and be prevented from flowing. When you have taken out the amount of blood needed, place on the vein fat and salt lest it close up quickly.
And similarly you should do with every narrow branch. As for the benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen. And you do in its phlebotomy as you did in the second, the same. As for phlebotomy of the saphenous vein, its benefit is for the illnesses which are in the lower part of the body, like ailments of the uterus.
And retention of menses, and kidney diseases, and chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses.  Its method of phlebotomy is that he puts his leg in hot water and rubs it until the veins become prominent. Then you tie above the ankle joint with a ligature; and the vein is situated near the heel, on the surface towards the great toe.
And many branches branch off from it on the front of the leg. Therefore phlebotomize in its widest branch, or at the heel where it joins â€“ this is best and safest. For if you phlebotomize it on the front of the leg, be careful of the nerves which are beneath it on the front of the leg.
And make your phlebotomy of it with a twisting motion, as if you wanted to cut it off, and let the lancet be sharp. If the exit of blood is difficult, let him return his leg to the hot water, and leave the blood flowing in it until it is emptied.  If the phlebotomist misses the vein in the first attempt,
let him return with the phlebotomy a little higher. For the place is safe; no harm is feared from it if you are careful of the nerve, as we said. And do the same with the saphenous vein of the other leg. As for the sciatic vein, its place is, as we said, at the heel from the outer side.
And the benefit of its phlebotomy is for hip pain, if that is from hot blood. And its method of phlebotomy is that you put the patient in the bath and hasten, and bind his leg from the hip up to above the heel by four fingers' breadth with a long, thin bandage; for it does not appear except by this means.
Then, when it appears, phlebotomize it in whichever way you prefer: either by twisting â€“ which is best â€“ or by cutting it off, or by splitting it. For its location is safe. And it is extremely hidden in most people. If you do not find it,
and it does not appear at all to the touch, phlebotomize some of its branches, which are those that appear on the dorsum of the foot towards the little finger and the ring finger. And be careful of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood you want.
Then loosen the bandage and place cotton on the site of the phlebotomy and bind the place, for it will quickly heal.
Chapter ninety-six.
Concerning cupping and its method of use.
Cupping vessels may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass, and cupping is of two types: one is cupping with incision and evacuation of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping without incision is of two types.
And the cupping of the cheeks, which are the sides of the neck on both sides, and the cupping of the chin, which is under the lower jaw, and the cupping of the shoulders, and the cupping of the coccyx at the base of the spine, and the cupping of the forearms, which are the middle of the arms, and the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and for that reason it does not weaken the body as much as phlebotomy, and cupping, whether with fire or without, should not be used in any of the diseases that result from plethora until the whole body has been purged.
If the need for cupping arises from illness or habit, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of the month, in the middle of it, and at any time.
That is because some people, when their blood increases to the point that it needs to be removed by cupping, feel heaviness and headache in their head; and some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck; and some feel itching in their face and sides, darkness and pain in their eyes; and some scratch the place of their cuppings.
And some laugh a lot, and some find the taste of blood in their mouth and their gums bleed and they spit blood, and some sleep a lot, and some see blood, redness, slaughter, and wounds in their sleep and things like that; so when we see any of that...
especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours of the day have passed. As for the benefit of cupping the temples, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water pouring into the eyes.
But it should be after the evacuation of the whole body, and this cupping may be a substitute for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.  It is warned against using it by one who has a cold brain or has a cold, for it greatly harms him.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their heads should not use it, and those who habitually use it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, you should instruct the cupper to lower his hand with the cupping glass a little lower, for fear of causing forgetfulness. As for cupping the nape of the neck, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic and cephalic veins.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory apparatus, cough, and fullness.  The cupping of the nape of the neck should be raised a little, for if it is lowered, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the mastoid processes, it benefits from pains occurring in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and pain at the roots of the teeth.  It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic vein. You should instruct the cupper not to deepen his hand with the scarifier, lest he cut a vein and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from thrush in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth, and it may take the place of phlebotomy of the veins in the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations that are due to fullness and heat.
As for cupping the forearms, it benefits from what benefits phlebotomy of the three veinsâ€”the basilic, cephalic, and median cephalicâ€”because it attracts blood from all those small veins in the flesh and attracts those small veins from other thicker veins until the attraction reaches the three thick veins.
You should instruct the cupper not to be excessive in scarification because the area is devoid of flesh and underneath it are nerves and arteries. As for the single cupping performed on the coccyx, it benefits from hemorrhoids and lower sores.
You should instruct the cupper that the cupping glass should be large and made of copper, because the area requires strong suction and a glass cupping glass might break and require much scarification. As for cupping the legs, it noticeably reduces fullness.
Because it attracts blood from the whole body, it benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; it promotes menstruation; it benefits from boils and abscesses; and it takes the place of phlebotomy of the saphenous veins and the veins of the ankles, except that it weakens the body greatly and causes fainting in most people. The benefits of cupping the ankles are similar to the benefits of cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping glasses is that the cupping glass is first placed empty and suctioned moderately. Do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but rather place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors are drawn to the area evenly. Continue to repeat this until you see the area has reddened and opened.
When the redness of blood appears, then scarify and resume suction slowly. Then look at the condition of the bodies. If a person has thin flesh with porous pores, then you should scarify only once, lest the area ulcerate.
Instruct the cupper to widen the scarification and deepen it slightly, and to moderate the suction gently and with a light movement. If the blood is thick, then it should be scarified twice; the first time to open a path for the thin blood and its watery parts, and the second time to completely extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, then repeat the scarification a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to extract a little blood, we are satisfied with one scarification; if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make more scarifications, especially if we judge the blood to be thick.
It is necessary that we make a deep scarification, and the moderate limit in scarification is only the depth of the skin. What should be used of ointments when applying cupping glasses, and of waters, and what the cupped person should avoid. As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and whose pores are tight,
then the places of the cupping glasses should be anointed with opening, softening, resolving ointments; if it is in the summer, then for example, with rose oil, or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is in the winter, then such as narcissus oil, or iris oil, or chamomile oil, or lily oil and the like. If the humor is thick and cold, then let the ointment be marjoram oil, or henbane oil, or butter, or rue oil and the like.
If the cupped person has wide pores and soft flesh, then the ointment should be avoided. These should wash their cupping places after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with gooseberry water, or with gourd water, or with chickweed water and the like. As for someone whose blood is very moist,
he should wash his cupping places with vinegar, or with water of asafoetida and sumac and the like. As for someone whose humors are thick, he should wash his cupping places with old wine, or with marjoram water, or a decoction of rue, or chamomile and the like. Cupping should be avoided in the bathhouse and after the bathhouse.
Rather, it should be used after leaving the bathhouse after an hour or two. No one should sleep after cupping.  What the cupped and bled person should manage before and after cupping.
First, it must be looked at; if the cupped or bled person is choleric and sharpness and inflammation prevail in his blood, then he should take refrigerants such as pomegranate, and dandelion with vinegar, and lettuce, and oxymel, and julap and the like, and make his foods from squabs, and lamb meats,  and sorrel and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, then he should be given honeyed drinks and quince drinks or seed oxymel, and he should consume moderately perfumed wine, which is between old and new, and he should be ordered to eat little food, and make his food from squabs, and quails, and sparrows, and young pigeons, spiced.  And the drink on the day of cupping and bleeding should be more than the food.
And it may be necessary to give some people at certain times some of the theriac al-Faruq, or musk medicine, or  silphium before cupping and before bleeding or after it, to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood, and it should not be given to those who have a fever.
As for cuppings that are without a condition, they are the cuppings that are placed on the liver, and spleen, and breasts, and on the abdomen, and navel, and the place of the kidneys, and the hip joint, because these organs do not tolerate the condition on them; rather what is intended by them is either to draw blood from an organ to another organ.
Such as our placing the cupping glass on the breasts in the illness of epistaxis, or we use it to remove from the organ a cold wind that has settled in the organ, such as our placing the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel, for it loosens the organ and warms it and removes the pain by resolving that wind.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if an obstruction or stone appears in them, then by the force of its drawing it may perhaps open the obstruction or remove the stone from its place, and it does the same if it is placed on the liver and spleen in the case of wind that is disturbed in them, and these cuppings may be used empty with only suction.
And it may be used with fire, and it may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of inflammation, and that is that the cupping glass is filled, and let it be large, with hot water alone, or with water in which some herbs that are suitable for that have been cooked.
Then it is placed filled on the place and held and removed and repeated many times according to the need, and this is the image of the cupping glass which is used with fire:
Its mouth is two fingers wide, open as we have pictured, and its depth is half a span, and there is in its side, about halfway up, a small hole about the size of a needle's insertion; it is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper, thick-rimmed.
Smooth, even, polished so that it does not harm the member when placed upon it, and in its middle is a transverse bar of copper or iron where the burning candle is placed; and this cupping glass may be made larger than what we have described, and smaller, according to the diseases.
And the way it is used, for the cupping glasses of boys and thin people are not the cupping glasses of men and those of full habit. As for the manner of placing this cupping glass with fire on the member, it is that you prepare a burning wick of tightly-woven flax or a small candle of pitch and place it on the middle of the solidified rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass.
So that the ascent of the fire is upwards towards the bottom of the cupping glass, lest the body of the patient be burned; then it is placed on the member, and the finger on the hole which we mentioned, so that when we have held the cupping glass as long as we need, we remove the finger.
And the vapor goes out through that hole and the cupping glass is loosened from its place; then you prepare the wick in the same way and replace it if you need to do so. As for the cupping glass which is used in the treatment of the disease called *shÅ«shah* with water, it has no solidified rod and no hole.
But it is used by being filled with water and placed on the member only, and this is its form:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to hold much water, the better it is.
Chapter ninety-seven.
On the suspension of the leech.
Leeches are used mostly in those members where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because of their smallness, like the lip and gum and the like, or because the member is devoid of flesh, like the finger and nose and the like.
And the method of using them is that you should choose leeches which are in fresh, pure water, free from impurities, then leave them a day and a night in fresh water until they become hungry and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body is first purged by phlebotomy or cupping, then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is placed upon it; and when it is full, it falls off. And if it is possible to cup the place, this is more effective; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and rub and squeeze it. If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and this is a trickle, then let a linen cloth be wetted in cold water.
And place it on top until the trickle stops. If the blood is profuse, sprinkle upon it powdered vitriol or gallnut or the like of astringents until the bleeding stops, or place on the place halves of peeled lupins.
And leave it until the lupin adheres to the place, for then the blood stops. And it is necessary that if it is needed to reapply leeches, then do not apply those leeches if others are available.  And if the leeches refuse to adhere, then let the place be wiped with fresh blood.
Or a needle is inserted into the place until some blood comes out, then it is applied; for when it senses some blood, it adheres in place. And if you want it to fall off, sprinkle upon it some aloe or salt or ashes; then it falls off in place.
Chapter Three.
On setting (bones).
This chapter also contains what is needed in the craft of medicine, which is the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never consulted a book of the ancients in it, nor read a letter from it.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become nonexistent in our country, and I have never encountered anyone skilled in it at all. And I only derived what I derived from it through my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them, until I extracted knowledge of it from them, then I adhered to experience and practice throughout my life.
And I have depicted for you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompassed. And my experience has followed it, after I have brought it close to you and freed it from the branches of prolixity, and shortened it to the utmost brevity, and clarified it to the utmost clarity, and depicted for you in it many images of the instruments that are used in it.
For it is from an increase in explanation, as I did in the preceding two chapters, and there is no strength except in God, the Most High, the Great.
The first chapter.
In it are summaries and compendia concerning the matter of fractured bones, and it is necessary to present them. And before we begin to mention the fractured and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary that we mention at the beginning of this chapter a summary of the discourse and sections. It compels you first to understand them and stand upon their reality, you and whoever else is eager to learn this noble craft.
Then I say that whenever a fracture or dislocation or wound or fall occurs to someone, it is necessary to hasten first to bleed him or purge him, or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or if the one to whom this has happened is a boy or an old, feeble man, or if the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then restrict his diet to cold vegetables and the meat of birds and gazelles, and forbid him drink and thick meats and overfilling with food, and every food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling is safe and the pouring of matter to the place is not expected.
Then let the patient return to his first regimen, which was his custom.  When the broken bone begins to knit, then the patient should eat foods that provide abundant, thick, strong nourishment with viscosity, such as porridges, rice, heads, necks, and cow tripe. And eggs and soft fish and thick drinks and the like, for by this method the setting of the fracture will be quicker and better. And know that broken bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot ever unite and knit together to their original nature.
Because of the dryness and hardness of their bones.  And what was of bones in the utmost softness, like the bones of small children, may unite and knit together, but nature grows on the broken bone from all its sides something resembling glue, thick, which adheres to it and strengthens it until it binds one part to another.
And it binds one part to another until it comes to the utmost strength and firmness as it was at first, so that nothing hinders him in his actions.
For this reason it is necessary to give the patient food which contains strength, viscosity, and thickness, as we said. And know that fractures differ in type according to the difference of the members, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone.
And so all the other members, each differs from another. And we shall come to mention each type of fracture explained in its chapter in detail apart from the others. And the type of bone fracture may also differ within itself.
For its fracture may be a splintering without splinters occurring in it, and its fracture may be along the length of the bone and its fracture may have splinters and projections, separated or unseparated, and the fracture may be with a wound and rupture in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each type there is a special method in its setting, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And among what identifies a bone fracture is its crookedness, swelling, and its appearance to the touch, and its roughness when you probe it with your hand.
Then if there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor roughness, nor do you feel upon touching the bone a disturbance, nor does the patient feel much pain, then there is no fracture, but it may be a sprain or a slight fracture or a slight crack.  Therefore it is not appropriate to move it by pulling and probing at all.
Rather, apply to it from the medications, the mention of which will come after a while, what is suitable for the place, then bind it gently. And know that if the bone is fractured and broken into two without splinters occurring in it, then each part has only moved away from its counterpart.
Then it is necessary for you to hasten at once to straighten and align it before a hot swelling occurs to it.  If a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it in whatever way you can, gently and skillfully.
And know that its setting and alignment is easier than a bone in which splinters have occurred. Then it is bound as will be mentioned. If the bone has splinters, then it is necessary to pull the broken limb from both sides, whether it be hand or foot.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand.  And let your placement of the limb be on a level surface in its natural form, so that when the broken part of the bone is extended, then return those fragments to their places in whatever way you can, skillfully and gently.
And strive with all your effort that you do not cause the patient pain or suffering by your actions, and strive with all your effort to join one bone to the other in the best manner. It is necessary at that time that you touch and feel them with your hand, and if you see there something amiss, correct and align it to the best of your ability.
And beware of strong pulling and forceful probing, as many ignorant people do, and they often cause by their actions a hot swelling or gangrene in the limb, as I have witnessed many times from their actions.
Then, after the alignment, perfection, and binding, compel that limb to stillness and rest, and warn the patient not to move it during his waking hours and sleep, and during his turning and restlessness, and during his bowel movements and all his movements as much as he is able, and let him strive that the position of the limb is a position which secures him from pain.
That is because, whenever he feels pain or discomfort in the position of the limb, he should move it to another position in which he does not feel pain, while striving to maintain a level and straight position.
Lest deformity occur in the limb if it heals improperly. As for the method of binding the broken limb, it is as I describe to you. Know that broken limbs differ in their smallness and largeness and forms.  Those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and the likeâ€¦
it is necessary that the bandages be soft and gentle; and those that are thick, like the thigh, back, and chest, it is necessary that the bandages be broad and firm, for the broad bandage is necessary for a large limb, and it binds it from every side with an equal binding that no defect intervenes.
Then, after you have finished the alignment, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. with a soft, pliable material over the fracture site. Then begin wrapping the bandage around the fracture itself three or four times, depending on what the limb requires.  Tighten the bandage slightly with your hand, then bring it to the upper part of the fracture site.
with less tightening than you used at the fracture site. Then, gradually move the bandage away from the fracture site, loosening the tension little by little until you have encompassed a suitable portion of the sound area. Then take another bandage and also wrap it around the fracture site.
Then continue the bandage to the lower part of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the bandage be as we mentioned in the first upper wrapping.  Then place between the bandages some of the soft material or cloths to even out the crookedness of the fracture.
If there is a deformity, otherwise do not put anything there. Then wrap another bandage around it, and then apply firmly fitting splints over these bandages, if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, then apply something to reduce the swelling and eliminate the inflation, and leave it for a few days. Then, at that time, apply splints.  Let the splints be made of broad, carved, skillfully prepared halves of reeds, or let the splints be made of sieve wood.
which are made from pine, or date palm stalks, or  reed, or  boxwood, or similar materials that are available.  Let the width of each splint be of this same size and shape.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be a little thicker and wider than the other splints. As for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb in terms of its size, Image of the splint:
Then tighten the splints with another bandage, in the same way as your first tightening. Then tie it from above with strong threads, according to the tightening we mentioned, which is that your tightening should be stronger at the site of the fracture. And the further it is from the fracture, the less the tightening should be.
The threads should be of medium thickness and fineness, and should be made of wet linen especially, because if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by the ignorant who make their threads from twisted linen strips, this is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them becomes excessive, and thin threads are also unsuitable because you will not achieve with them the tightening you want.  And there should not be less than a finger's breadth between the splints.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after tightening in the healthy areas, place soft padding or fluffy wool underneath so that nothing harms him. As for if the fracture is accompanied by a wound and a rupture in the skin occurs, we will mention that separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be bound with splints from the first day.  If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until after five or seven days, or more, depending on your certainty that hot inflammation will not occur.
This is a description of the bandages which the ancients used to bind fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  It is a description of a general, concise bandage that sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is not dominated by heat or cold.
It is that you take the dust from the mill, which is the finest flour that adheres to the walls of the mill during the grinding, and knead it as it is, without sifting it, with egg white, making the dough neither thick nor thin, then apply it.
Another bandage which sets fractures, dislocations and sprains: take of mastic, leadwort, acacia,  rasen,  magath, and sugar, ten drachms of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; of athl, twenty drachms; and of Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachms. Pound everything together and mix with the water of athl, or with egg white if the patient's temperament is hot. Then use this bandage; for it is well-composed, quickly sets broken bones, and is suitable for most people because of its moderate nature.
Another bandage also useful for fractures and sprains: take magath, mastic, and white leadwort, ten drachms of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; acacia, six drachms; Armenian clay, twenty drachms. Pound everything finely, sift it, and knead it with water or egg white, and use it.
A bandage for joints and bones that have been dislocated, and which relieves the accompanying pain, and for bone cracks and fractures: take carded wool, soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply it to the affected area. This bandage does not have the power to set [bones].
But it is excellent in relieving hot swelling and particularly in relieving pain. Another bandage which sets broken bones: take the leaves of the deaf fig and the leaves of wild poppy, pound them together, and apply them moist. Another short bandage used when a bone fracture is uniting.
And you want to resolve the remainder of the swelling, take of the root of leadwort, chamomile, violet blossoms, and crushed corn, one part of each. Pound everything, and knead it with white of egg if the limb is not hot; if it is hot, knead it with coriander water or with water, and use it.
Another bandage stronger in resolving than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs as the bone unites: take of the root of leadwort, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, balm mint, violet blossoms, and chamomile, one part of each.
Pound everything and knead it with vinegar, or with fresh water, or with white of egg, all according to the heat of the limb and the state of its inflammation.  As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb when setting it.
Except for the cerate made only of wax and oil, and he described it as being of medium consistency between thick and thin. As for Galen, he was of the opinion that things containing dryness with some heat, such as myrrh, aloes, frankincense, and the like that we have mentioned, should be placed on the broken limb when setting it.
Description of a bandage beneficial for weakness and pain: take gallnuts, chickpea flour, human hair that has been plucked or bird feathers, leadwort, and salt, equal parts. Pound, sift, knead and bandage with it. As for the amount of time the bandage should remain...
Then loosen it; that is to say, observe; if no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place occurs in the diseased area, do not loosen it for many days.  If, however, severe itching, troublesome pain, or swelling occurs in that placeâ€¦
then hasten to loosen it at once and do not delay at all, and remove the bandage from it. Then take a soft cloth or a damp sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the itching and pain subside. Then let the limb rest for an hour.
Then apply carded wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil and bandage it on overnight until you are sure the hot swelling has subsided, the swelling of the limb has ceased, and its pain has gone. Then return to gentle tightening and a light bandage, and do not bind it as tightly as before.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and inflammation, and all the symptoms have completely disappeared and you need a bandage and binding, then reapply it as you did initially. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the limbâ€¦
then do not loosen it except after three, four, five, or seven days, or even twenty days; all this depends on what appears to you concerning the condition of the limb, as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh begins to knit around it.
Then, at that time, increase the binding more than your first binding, and also increase the richness of the patient's diet beyond what was previously mentioned. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried out and become thinner than it should be, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you unbind it, and let this be every three days, and loosen the binding a little. By this action, nourishment will flow to the limb, and it will heal quickly. As for what ignorant bone-setters do, breaking the limb againâ€¦
if it was not initially set properly, and it is set crookedly, then this is an error in their action and a great deception. If it were correct, the early authorities would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it, and I have not seen any trace of it from any of them at all. And the correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On Fractures Occurring in the Head.
The types of fractures occurring in the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and this may either completely sever the bone until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. Just as an axe does to wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called an axe fracture.  Or, the sword may cut some of the bone, severing only its surface, without the cut penetrating completely; this type of fracture is called an absolute extraction. The wound in these two types of fractures may be either large or small.
Some fractures are comminuted or contused, caused by a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone or something similar. This fracture may either penetrate, nearly reaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone.
The wound in these two fractures may also be either large or small.  Some fractures are hidden in the bone, as fine as a hair; this is a slight crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called hairline.  Another fracture may result from a fall or a blow from a stone or the like, which drives a piece of the bone inwards, causing a depression at the site, similar to what happens to a copper pot when struck, causing a portion of it to be driven inwards.
This most often occurs in the soft bones of the head, such as in the heads of children. All these types of fractures may have loose and non-loose fragments. We shall describe the treatment of all of these in their proper place, and we shall identify all these types of fractures by examining them.
and examining them with probes and removing the corrupted flesh from them. As for the hairline type, it is identified by examining the bone, wiping it, and staining it with ink; the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of fractures, one must first look at the patient's symptoms.
If you see symptoms that clearly indicate danger, such as vomiting bile, prostration, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, sunken eyes, redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
But if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his recovery, then begin his treatment.  If the injured person comes to you immediately after the injury, and this is during the winter months,
you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day, in any case.  If it is during the summer months, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone spoils and those symptoms we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is crushing and contusion, you should cut away the crushed and contused part as I describe to you: shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in whatever way is possible for you, according to the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient. If, upon exposing the bone, you find bleeding or a hot swelling, treat it as necessary.
This is done by packing the area with cloths soaked in a potion and rose oil, and cleaning the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then begin to bore out and remove the bone; this can be done in one of two ways.
The first method is to cut the bone with a fine saw with a narrow blade.  Here is its image:
Then use another saw, slightly wider than the first. Here is its image:
Then also use another saw, wider still than the second.  It is necessary that you have a set of saws of varying widths and lengths, all extremely sharp. Let them be made of Indian steel or good quality iron.
And use gentleness in applying the saw, lest you shake the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and solid, it is necessary to bore around it before using the saws, with the borers called non-immersive borers.  They are called non-immersive borers.
Because they do not penetrate beyond the skull bone, for the borer has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a small ring or circle, preventing it from penetrating and going beyond the thickness of the bone.
You should have many of these borers, each suitable for a particular bone thickness, so that for each skull you have a borer whose sharp point is of appropriate length and shortness for the thickness of that skull. Here is an image of three types. of the borers: large, medium, and small.
As for the method of boring around the fractured bone, you place the borer on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then you move the borer to another location, making the distance between each hole about the thickness of an awl or similar.
Then cut between each two holes with the cutting instruments, doing so with the utmost gentleness as we have said, until you remove the bone either with your hand or with some other instrument prepared for this purpose.
such as forceps and delicate hooks. You should be very careful that the borer or cutter does not touch any of the peritoneum.  If you have removed the bone and it is clear of the peritoneum, if it was adhering to it, and you have removed it, then you should dress the wound.
And smooth the roughness of what remains of the bone with another instrument similar to the cutter, except that it should be thinner and more delicate than the other cutters. If any small bones or fragments remain, remove them gently with the instruments you have.
Then treat the wound afterwards with sutures and ointments, which will be mentioned later. As for the other method, it is very easy and free from danger; Galen mentioned it and praised it highly.  His words are: First, you should begin by uncovering the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until, when you have uncovered that part, you place the tip of this lenticular cutter underneath it; and this is its image:
Its lenticular part should be smooth so that it does not cut anything, and its sharp part should be on its sides extending lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the peritoneum and the sharp edge of the cutter is against the bone. Then strike the cutter from one side.
With a small mallet, until you cut through all the bone gently as it rotates, and you are safe from the membrane, so that nothing frightening happens to it at all, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were sleepy.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some parts of the bone, then scrape it off with the tip of the lenticular cutter itself, and gently free it from it; for it will be freed from it without harm or fear.
But if the bone fracture has not penetrated to the membrane, and what was broken off from the bone was its surface, and roughness and small fragments remain in it, then you should remove that roughness and extract those fragments with fine forceps, several of which have been made in different sizes, so that you can use the most suitable and best of them in each place, according to what the work leads you to.
And the broken bone itself and its shape, and you should use the widest of those forceps first to remove the bone, then use a finer one after it, and continue to do that successively until you come to using the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small, hair-like cracks and slight fractures, you should use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its healing; this is something which is not hidden from anyone who has the slightest experience in this craft and has understood what we have written and explained concerning large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, then you should take a linen cloth of the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. And dip it in syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do this as gently as you can so as not to weigh down the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage on top and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are safe from the hot swelling, then untie it and use afterwards those medicines which have a drying effect, such as the roots of iris, crushed costus, crushed spikenard, zedoary, and the like. This is done by making a powder from these medicines.
And sprinkle it on the wound dry. In short, use a medicine which cleanses and does not sting. In your treatment of the wound, strive to keep it clean, free from fat and dirt, and do not allow pus to collect in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and putrefies it, causing great harm to the patient.  This can occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If blackness appears on its surface, and the patient exhibits the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he will inevitably perish.  However, if the blackness resulted from a medicine applied to it, and it was in the nature of that medicine to cause such blackness,
you should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well, and apply them to a cloth, which you then place on the peritoneum. Then treat it with the various appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the reduction of a fractured nose.
Know that only the upper parts of the nose, both or one, can be fractured, because these are bones; the lower part is cartilaginous and does not fracture, but rather is subject to bruising, crookedness, and flattening.
If one of its sides is fractured, you should insert your little finger into the nostril and, using your index finger and thumb externally, straighten the fracture from the inside until you restore the nose to its natural shape.  Do this gently, being careful not to cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, it should be straightened with the end of a slightly thick probe. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same.  And let it be set on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after seven or ten days, when the acute inflammation has subsided. Then insert a pledget of linen into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two pledgets if it is on both sides.
Let the pledgets be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some early bone-setters mentioned moistening the pledgets with fat and changing them daily, but I do not consider that necessary; rather, the pledgets should be moistened with egg white mixed with finely ground flour.
Then leave the pledgets in place until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens.  Goose quills, wrapped in soft linen, may be inserted into the nose at the site of the pledgets, to provide firmer support for the fractured nose and to prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not necessary; you may do it or you may use pledgets instead. If acute inflammation occurs in the nose during treatment, apply a compress of quince or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or some diachylon ointment.
If no acute inflammation occurs, apply an external dressing of finely ground semolina and frankincense mixed with egg white, then apply a soft bandage, but do not tie the nose at all, if the nasal bones are only slightly fractured.
Or if they are shattered, incise them and remove the fragments with a suitable instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with appropriate ointments that promote healing. If a wound occurs inside the nose, treat it with pledgets and use lead tubes. until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the setting of lower jaw fractures.
If the lower jaw is fractured without an accompanying wound, and the fracture is external only, without being broken in two and depressed inwards, then its detection is easy.
If the fracture is in the right ramus, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is in the left ramus, insert the index finger of the right hand, and gently elevate the fractured portion from the inside outwards.
With your other hand outside the bone, you should manipulate it to set it. If the jaw is fractured in two, then traction should be applied from both sides until alignment is achieved. If the teeth are involved, they will be loosened.
Or if they are separated, then tie those you hope will remain with a gold, silver, or silk thread. Then apply pledgets to the fractured jaw, and over that a folded cloth, and over the cloth a firm, large splint or a piece of sole leather equal to the length of the jaw. Then bind it from above according to what seems best to you and suits the binding, so that it does not become undone. Instruct the patient to remain still and calm, and give him soft drinks as food. If you think that something has changed in its appearance in any way, then proceed to unbind it on the third day.
Then bind it from above according to what seems best to you and suits the binding, so that it does not become undone. Instruct the patient to remain still and calm, and give him soft drinks as food. If you think that something has changed in its appearance in any way, then proceed to unbind it on the third day. Then repair the bandage with mill dust mixed with egg white or semolina flour after removing the pledgets, and place a soft compress over the bandage. As long as the bandage adheres and the condition of the bone does not change, leave it.
Do not unbind it until it heals and the fracture is firm. Often this fracture becomes firm in three weeks. If a hot inflammation occurs during that time, then use what we have repeatedly mentioned to soothe it until the inflammation subsides.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, look; if a splinter or splinters have separated from the bone, gently remove those splinters with whatever instrument is suitable for their removal. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with the scalpel as much as you need.
Then, when you have removed those splinters and nothing remains, stitch the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that purpose. And suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the reduction of the clavicle if it is fractured.
The clavicle is most often fractured in front, at the end of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it is broken and split into two without splinters, which is easiest to reduce;
or splinters occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to reduce; or the fracture is accompanied by a wound.  The procedure, if the fracture is without a wound, is that you should have two assistants, one to hold the arm adjacent to the fractured clavicle, and the other to extend the neck towards the other side.
Then you should set the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is neither projection nor irregularity. If you need more extension, you should place under the armpit of the patient a ball of rags or wool.
Its size should be according to your need. You extend and raise the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set as it should be. If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has gone inwards, then the patient should lie on his back.
A moderately sized pillow is placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses his shoulder downwards until the clavicle that is deep rises upwards. Then set the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken and is moving, you should incise over it and remove that splinter gently. If the splinter is embedded in the bone, try to cut it with one of the instruments prepared for that purpose. After placing under the clavicle the instrument that protects the peritoneum, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a curve.  Its width should be according to the size of the bone, large or small, and its length also according to what you can manage for the work, and let it be double-ended as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound opening that you incised when removing the bone splinter is wide and you are safe from hot inflammation, join the edges of the wound by stitching. If the opening is small or you fear hot inflammation, cauterize the wound with a cautery and lint according to the size of the incision.
If hot inflammation appears, wet the cautery in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting a bone if it is without a wound or incision, it is that you apply to the bone a compress made of mill dust with egg white. And you place the soft splint on it, then you place the ball under his armpit if he needs it, then you take a bandage that is very long, and its width is a cubit or so, then apply a folded compress to the splint and the compress, then take a splint from a thin board. Let its width be three fingers and in length likewise, then insert it into a cloth, then insert that cloth with the splint into the place of the bandage which falls on the place of the fracture, then tighten the bandage on the fracture as it turns. And pass it over his neck and under his sound armpit and under his injured armpit and return it several times on each side, and as you see that the tightening controls the fracture firmly, which is not hidden from you.
And the aim of all this is that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should check on the patient every day; whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct that and tighten the bandage.  Then let the patient sleep on his back and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm is raised from his side, and the clavicle fracture is raised by raising the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck.  And do not untie the bandage unless something happens at the site, such as itching or swelling, until twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. Then leave it bandaged until the clavicle fracture heals and consolidates, and it strengthens and becomes firmer; this takes about twenty-eight days. And in some people it may be less than that.
Chapter Six.
On the setting of a fractured humerus.  The humerus rarely fractures in its wide part; rather, it fractures at its extremities.  When a part of it is fractured, or it is fractured in the middle, this is known by touch.  Then, according to the nature of the fracture, treat it.
