Straighten it and restore it to its natural shape by any means you can, then apply to the area mill dust with egg white and soft lint; place on top a pad of folded cloth, and then apply a broad splint of wood.
Thin enough to cover the whole shoulder or slightly wider. If, under the splint, there is any unevenness in any part of the shoulder, smooth out that unevenness with soft lint until the splint lies evenly, then bind tightly from above with a long bandage.
Bind it firmly and make sure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it moves from its place. Let the patient lie on his sound side. The shoulder heals in twenty [days].
days or twenty-five days. When this period is complete, remove the bandage, and you are safe, for it is among the bones which are not to be feared for shattering or breaking. If a fragment of bone protrudes and is pressing under the skin, incise over it and remove it. And do what was mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle to alleviate the hot swelling if anything of that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On the setting of a fracture of the chest.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, though this rarely happens; as for its extremities, they are more frequently fractured and shattered. Among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and there is intense pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
And perhaps blood is expelled, and the depression in the fractured bone becomes apparent and is not hidden from the touch.  Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back, a pillow is placed between his shoulders, then his shoulders are compressed, and the ribs are brought together with the hands from both sides, and gentleness is used in straightening it in every way possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be, then apply a bandage and a splint, and place on top of it a splint of thin wood from willow or elm or something similar that is light, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then gently tie it to the fractured bone lest it shift, and pass the bandage around to the back several times, and tighten it firmly, then check the bandage frequently, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity dictates its removal due to suppuration occurring at the site, or pain, or swelling, then hasten to remove it, and take off the bandage, and treat whatever has occurred in accordance with its treatment, then reapply the bandage if you see fit. And treat it in this manner, and keep it firmly bound until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the setting of broken ribs. Know that fractures of the ribs occur in the thick parts adjacent to the back, and their extremities in front are only subject to contusion, because they are cartilaginous, and the knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense upon examination with the fingers.
And its treatment is to set the fracture with the fingers in the accessible way, until the shape is made even as it should be, then it is bandaged, and the broken bone is bound with a splint if it needs it.  If the fracture of the ribs is inclined inwards, then severe pain affects the patient.
And it penetrates like the penetration which occurs to one who has a chest wound, because the bone penetrates the diaphragm, and there also occurs for him difficulty of breathing, coughing, and the expectoration of much blood. This is difficult to treat, and the ancients devised many stratagems for it.
And some of them said that it is necessary to give the patient foods that produce flatulence and wind, so that the abdomen swells and expands and the fracture is pushed outwards. And we dislike this, lest it should confirm the occurrence of hot inflammation if it has not occurred; and if it has occurred, then it increases it and confirms it.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on the place, then sucked strongly; and this is more like the proper method, except that it is feared that the cupping glass will draw humors to the place on account of its weakness. And some of them said that it is necessary to cover the place with wool which has been dipped in hot oil.
And a bandage is made between the ribs until it is full, so that the binding is even when it is wound around circularly. Then the patient is treated with the treatment of a chest wound, in terms of food and medicine; if the patient is afflicted by a severe matter which he cannot bear. And the bone penetrated the diaphragm in a harmful way, and we feared for the patient; therefore it is necessary to incise the place and uncover the broken rib, then place under it the instrument which preserves the peritoneum, which has been described previously, and gently break the bone and extract it.
Then we bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot inflammation occurs during this time, then act quickly, wet bandages in rose oil, place them on the place, and treat the patient with what calms the inflammation from within also.
And he lies on the side on which sleep is easiest for him until he heals.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture affects themâ€”and this rarely happens to them, and contusions are more common to them, as well as to the vertebrae of the back alsoâ€”then if this happens to someone and you want to know whether he will heal or not heal, look: then if you see his hands have become relaxed, numb, and dead, and he is not able to move them, nor extend them, nor contract them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, then know that in most instances he will not heal, and he is dying.
And if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking in them, then know that the marrow of the bone has been saved and that the patient will heal with treatment.  If the vertebrae of the back are similarly affected and you want to know whether he will heal or not,
look at his legs: then if you see that they have become relaxed and what happened in the hands has happened in them, then if he lies on his back, wind and faeces come out involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily, and if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is dying, so do not trouble yourself with his treatment. If none of this happens to him, the matter is less serious. The treatment of what has happened is that you try to alleviate the hot swelling.
by placing on the injured vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks; apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply to the place one of the strengthening, drying compresses and bind it tightly, and instruct him to remain still and quiet.
And let him not sleep except on the side where he feels no pain, until he is healed. If, at the time of the injury, a splinter or something has become detached from the bone, then it is necessary to incise the skin and remove that bone. Then bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, then treat with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, then you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the rectum.
and set the broken bone with the other hand, as best you can manage and achieve the setting. Then apply the compress and splint, if you need to, and then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, then incise it and remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment mentioned above until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the reduction of a fracture of the hip.
Hip bones rarely fracture; but if they do fracture, it is only in that their extremities are shattered and they split lengthwise, and incline inwards.  The patient experiences pain in the area, and numbness and tingling in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its reduction is that you pass your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only in its extremities, then set that fracture according to what seems best to you in setting it, until its shape resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise, or if it has inclined inwards, lay the patient on his stomach so that the reduction of that fracture is made easier for you.  When you have set it, apply a bandage, then put a splint of wood or leather on it and bind it tightly.
Do not fear for him the displacement of the fracture or the loosening of the splint, and fill the hollow of the hips with what will fill it, until the binding is even. And instruct the patient to lie on his back or on his sound side. If a hot inflammation should occur to him,
desist from its reduction until the hot inflammation subsides, and apply what will alleviate it as previously described. Then return to its reduction and binding as is appropriate. If splinters or shattered fragments of bone appear, they should not be removed.
They are not to be touched, but are to be set from the outside as we have said, and its binding is to be left until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the reduction of a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder. If it is fractured, its reduction is done in one of two ways. One is to take a smooth, curved piece of wood of medium thickness, in this manner:.
And you tie two bandages to its ends, then it is hung from a high place, and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the wood until his armpit is fixed in the middle of the curve of the wood, then something heavy is hung from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with his hands together until he reduces the fracture as it should be. The other method is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is suspended from his neck with a bandage.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and each stretches to his side. And if you want the traction to be stronger, then tighten below the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and each of the servants stretches to his side.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, then the middle of the bandage should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.  Likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then you set the fracture gently without force until the fracture is set as it should be and unites well. Then you bandage it if no hot swelling appears. If hot swelling appears, leave bandaging until the seventh day and apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bandage it. The method of bandaging is to apply the compress to the fracture, then apply a bandage of new cloth over the compress, then bring the arm to the upper arm itself and place the hand open on the shoulder.
And you apply the cloth and bandage to the upper arm and forearm so that the forearm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you from doing so and nothing changes in the broken bone. If you fear that something might change, then use splints.
This is to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint.  Let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then you tighten the splints with the tightening that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your tightening on the place of the fracture should be tighter.
And the further from the fracture, the less the tightening. If you see that the placing of the splints and the tightening as we said at the time of your setting the limb is suitable, then do so.
And if you fear a hot inflammation, leave off the tightening and the splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days, lest there occur in the place chafing or swelling, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the member by reason of excessive tightening.  Correct all this as we mentioned.
But if you are confident that nothing of that sort will happen, do not loosen the bandage except after many days. And let the patient lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a pillow filled with wool and of moderate size be placed under the armpit.
And examine at every time, night or day, lest the shape of the broken member be disarranged or the bandage become loose. Then correct all this with your effort, and let the patient's diet be of the kind we have already mentioned: that the food should be light at first until the bone is about to become firm.
Then it is proper to make his food more substantial. For it is the habit of the arm and leg to become firm in forty days. Then it is proper to loosen [the bandages], and he should use the bath and be treated with the ointments which are suitable for this.  If the fracture is extensive and comminuted,
then do not loosen the bandage from him until fifty days or until two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna; one is small, and it is that which is next to the thumb; and the other is large, and it is situated under the small one from below.  Therefore, perhaps the larger ulna is broken alone, or the smaller one alone, and perhaps both are broken together.
And when the smaller upper ulna is broken, then its setting is easy and its healing is quicker. And when the lower ulna is broken, then its fracture is bad and its healing difficult. And the worst of them is if the two bones are broken together.  If the bone which is broken is the smaller upper ulna,
then it is necessary for the physician, when setting it, to make its extension easy gently until he straightens it.  And if the large ulna is that which is broken, then it is necessary to make its extension stronger. And if both ulnas are broken, then it is necessary to make the extension much stronger.
And it is necessary that the shape of the hand, when it is set and extended, be extended on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be above, higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should sit squarely on himself.
And let the cushion be opposite him in height, lest the patient suffer hardship.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends from above likewise. Then the physician straightens the bone until he returns it to the best shape he can. And if there are fragments in the fracture of the bone, then they are removed.
Return each fragment to its place to the best of your ability.  If a fragment appears that is detached and is piercing the skin and there is no hope of setting it, then incise it and extract it in the manner which we mentioned previously.  If the fracture is with a wound, then I have dedicated a chapter to it.
Then take the treatment of that from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your setting, then apply a cloth with prepared Kypros, rose oil, and white wax. Let it be moderate between thickness and thinness, and bind the cloth on it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the Kypros and apply the prepared bandage of mill dust with egg white. Then apply the splints; and let the splint that is placed on the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger. And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture was in one forearm bone or in both forearm bones together, then make your binding at the location of the fracture stronger and tighter. And whenever you turn the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding a little looser as mentioned previously at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, and not very hard. And let the thread with which it is bound be of special linen, moderate between thinness and thickness, as we described. And examine the limb and bandage after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected, such as itching that appears in the limb, then it is necessary to wash the limb with warm water until that itching subsides, and leave the limb unbound for a night so that it may rest, then resume the binding.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted or something like that, then correct all of that to the best of your ability. And also look, if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive binding, then it is necessary to loosen it a little and leave it for a few days until nourishment flows to it, then bind it.
If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs to the patient, then it should not be unbound except after twenty days or so. Then, suspend the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be straight. And he should carefully avoid agitated movements, and let his sleep be on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all that according to the states of the temperaments and the states of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the hand and fingers.
Fracture rarely occurs in the metacarpus and phalanges of the fingers; rather, contusion frequently occurs to them.  So when a fracture or contusion occurs to the handâ€¦
The patient should sit cross-legged, with a chair directly in front of him, level.
Then he should place his hand upon it, extended. Then the surgeon should extend the broken bones and set them, until they are well-set.  Then a bandage and splint should be applied, if no hot inflammation occurs. Then a splint should be applied over that, according to the location.
And it is inserted into a soft cloth. If the fracture is towards the lower part, towards the palm of the hand, then make a kind of sphere from cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then tighten it with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of soft leather, so that it conforms to the skin with the bending of the whole hand, and tighten it as necessary. If the fracture is outwards, then the splint should be placed above and another splint below in the hand, so that the hand is open and upright.
Then the bandage is applied as the hand rotates, and interlaces between the fingers with the bandage. If the fracture affects one of the finger joints, if it is the thumb, let it be set properly, then bandaged with the hand; and if you wish, you can make a small, upright splint to support the fracture.
And it should not move. If the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, little, or ring finger, let them be set and bound with the healthy adjacent finger, or all bound together, which is better, or a small upright splint is placed on them.
As we said concerning the thumb, and examine during and after your setting for hot inflammation, and counteract it as necessary whenever anything of that occurs, as has been repeatedly described.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the setting of a fractured thigh.
The thigh bone is frequently fractured and is evident to the touch because it moves forward and backward.  Its treatment is to fasten a bandage above the fracture and another below it, the patient lying on his face.
Then each bandage is extended to its side moderately. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone, but if it is near the head of the thigh, then a soft bandage should be applied, either of wool or similar material.
At the head of the thigh towards the groin so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the extension is downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both his hands until he restores it to its natural form.
And the bone unites well. Then the bandage and binding should be maintained, unless a hot inflammation occurs in the limb. If inflammation occurs, leave it for a few days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for binding it, a wide, firm turban should be wound twice or thrice around the fracture, leaving some excess. Then the leg is bent until the heel is at the base of the buttock, and a long thread is inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, and the ends of the thread are brought up from both sides.
Then the remaining part of the turban is wound around the leg and thigh. Then splints are placed on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and one splint is placed on the bone of the leg. Then fill the space between the thigh and leg with soft cloths so that the binding is even.
Then begin strong binding from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns.  The farther the bandage is from the site of the fracture, the less tight and softer the binding should be.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie with it the splints above. Then pass the ends of the thread down to the heel of the foot and also tie the ends of the splints on the other side with them.
So that the bandage does not move from its place. Then leave the binding in place as long as no pain, swelling, or puffiness, etc., occurs in the limb. If any of this occurs, quickly unbind it and correct whatever has happened, as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a penetrating splinter in the bone, it should be straightened if possible. Otherwise, make an incision over it and remove it, and treat the wound as previously described until it heals. This fracture of the thigh may be bound without the leg being included with the splints.
As we mentioned in the case of the humerus and forearm, except that setting it like this does not result in lameness for the patient. But if it is set alone without the leg being included, the patient will necessarily always limp.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more or less, all this depending on the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
Fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but it is often contused. If a fracture does occur, it is either a fissure or a shattering into fragments, and this may be with or without a wound.
You will ascertain all this by touch, and you will set it by straightening the separated fragments with your fingers until they are united and joined together, according to the degree of accuracy, gentleness, and skill you can achieve; then apply a bandage, and apply a circular splint if necessary.
And tighten the bandage appropriately, then treat all the conditions we have described in other fractures, such as acute inflammation, etc., by counteracting each symptom with the appropriate treatment, until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the setting of fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones; one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula.  They are subject to the same types of fractures as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg is displaced in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured lower down, this is something which is not hidden from you.
Therefore, use extension and reduction, and apply splints accordingly, except that if the fracture of the leg is a severe one with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and you should take great care in its treatment.
And in the case of the leg there is something additional to the treatment of the arm: when you have set the splints and completed all your work, take two thin strips of pine wood, the kind used for smoothing rooms, which are placed between the cracks in the planks, or make them from palm leaves or something similar.
Choose those of moderate thickness, not too thin, and let their length be equal to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.  Then wrap each one with a cloth in two turns along its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side.
Let them extend from the knee to the lower part of the foot, then bind the two thin strips in three places, at the ends and in the middle. With this binding, the leg is prevented from tilting to the right or left, and is well supported.  A wooden gutter along the length of the leg may also be used.
It is placed in it to keep it from moving, and this is most necessary to do in a fracture if there is a wound associated with it. Then examine the leg every day and attend to it with great care regarding swelling, inflammation or anything else. If anything of that sort occurs, counter it appropriately until it heals. The bone of the leg knits together in about thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the fracture of the bones of the leg and the fingers.
As for the heel, fracture never occurs in it at all. As for the bones of the leg, fracture may occur in them, and also the fingers; however, fracture rarely occurs in them, but rather contusion in most instances.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the leg and you see that some of those bones are prominent over others, then let the patient put his foot on the ground upright as when walking, then you stand and place your foot on what is prominent of those bones, then tread and level them.
until they return to their places, then apply a bandage and splint from above, and place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot, then bind the foot tightly after wrapping it with cloths and everything else that it needs.
Then, if three or four days pass, untie the bandage, for you will find the bones straight, whether they were fractured or dislocated; the procedure is as you see.  As for if some of the fingers are fractured, set them and straighten them according to what I described to you in the setting of the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured finger along the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than it, then place under the foot this board which I described to you and bind it tightly. If what is fractured of the bones is two or three or more,
then make for each finger a splint of its size, layered in a soft cloth, and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot, and let each end of the board rest outside the sole of the foot to secure it well.
And you should not forget to attend to all that we mentioned to you in other fractures from the symptoms that we mentioned, and treat each symptom as it should be.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubis and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then bend her slightly backward little by little, and let her be held from behind, then let the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it fills it and becomes like a ball in her vulva.
Then the woman is shaken and her loins are raised little by little, for that cotton will come out until it is at the opening of the vulva like a ball, and the fracture of the bone will return. Then a pad is placed on her back, and when she wants to urinate, the cotton is removed gently so that she can urinate.
And return it in the way it was first introduced, then she returns to her first pad. She does this for seven days or so, for it will heal. And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube to its mouth, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva, for the fracture will return. Then pack it with cotton and remain as we described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, it is treated in the same manner.
And its treatment is as we described for the hip bone.  The correct treatment of these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for those with some experience and who understand this book well, is not hidden from you, for the fracture itself indicates the treatment in most cases.
Understand the correct way to treat and bind it. As for the penis, if it is fractured, take a goose's gullet, insert the penis into it, then apply a bandage of cloth, bind it, and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the treatment of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Whoever suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or humerus or similar, should immediately be bled if the conditions for bloodletting, as we have previously mentioned, are favorable.
If the wound bleeds, you should immediately stop it by applying powdered vitriol if nothing else is available. Then treat the fracture that same day, do not delay.
If no hot swelling occurs, then leave it until the ninth day if a hot swelling does occur, until the hot swelling subsides.  Do not attempt it at all on the third or fourth day, for bad symptoms may result.
If the broken bone is protruding through the skin and exposed, you should gently try to reposition and straighten it with your hands and a slight pull. If you cannot reposition and straighten it with your hands, reposition it with this instrument, which is made of iron and is about seven or eight fingers long. And its width should be according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of them, according to what he needs for treatment in each type of fracture. Let them be round, with a little thickness, so that they do not bend when pressure is applied during use.
And let the end be sharp, with a curve at its tip. Let the upper part be thicker, and from its middle to the lower part, much thinner.  This is its form:
It is called in Greek *peron*, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, curved end should be placed on the end of the protruding bone and pushed with it gently, until the bone returns and becomes somewhat straight, then align the broken ends with each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it. If you are not able to reset the bone as we have described, then cut it with a similar cut to those we mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the saws, however you can. Then remove any remaining roughness and thin scales from the bone. If, after resetting it, the patient feels severe, painful throbbing, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. When you have finished setting the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not apply a poultice or anything greasy to the wound.
Lest putrefaction and corruption occur. Then, when you have finished setting the bone, apply the splints and leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, the size of the wound.
Take great care not to bind the wound tightly with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this, causing their patients either death, gangrene, or tetanus. Let your binding be gentle, loose, and unlike the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels troubling pain in the place, then you should not apply splints, but make bandages of firm cloths in place of the splints, and bind it with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus is beginning to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you applied with the astringent drink, then use lint and ointments that we customarily use to treat wounds, such as the tetrapharmacum and similar.  You should loosen the bandage.
Examine the wound every evening and morning until it heals and is cured.  You should position the limb so that the pus flows downwards easily. If many days pass and the wound does not unite and the pus does not cease, then know that there are small fragments of bone.
You should probe the wound with a probe; whatever of those fragments is loose, extract and remove it; and whatever is not loose, and is causing the limb to fester and producing pain, then work at cutting and extracting it in every way that you are able.
If the wound develops a chill, or gangrene, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, you should treat each symptom with the corresponding treatment which has been previously described in its chapter.
Among the things you should heed and keep in mind is this: if a large bone is fractured and projects from the limb, such as the femur or humerus and similar large members, do not attempt to pull or remove it. Death often results from such an attempt; rather, leave it until it becomes putrid; perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see that it is amenable to treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of the complication which occurs after some fractures.
This complication often arises after the healing of a fracture, especially those near the joints; it disfigures the limb and may prevent it from its natural function.
Examine it; if the complication is fresh, use astringent drugs such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, gum mastic, acacia, and the like. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent syrup, or with egg white, or with vinegar.
Apply it to the complication in a bandage and bind it tightly. Leave the bandage in place for many days, then unbind it and reapply another, until the complication disappears, God willing, or bind a tight lead plate to it.
For lead has the property of removing all that is superfluous in the limbs. If the complication has hardened and become severe, and necessity compels its removal, incise it from above and cut away the excess growth, or shave it off with some instrument until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of a fracture, if it is set but the limb remains afterwards thin and not as it was originally.
If a bone fracture is set, but the limb remains thin and weak afterwards, this is due to many causes. One of these is either due to too much loosening and tightening of the bandages, or to excessive tightening of the bandages so that nourishment cannot reach the limb, or to excessive and repeated bandaging, or to excessive movements at the wrong time, or to a deficiency of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
The treatment of this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that his blood increases, and to use the bath and to bring him joy and happiness and the like, then to apply pitch to the limb so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it, or to repeatedly bandage it with lukewarm water. until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-two.
In the treatment of fractured bones if they are set crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
Then if a deformity or a projection of the broken bone or a complication appears in a limb that has healed after setting, and the appearance of the limb is thereby made unsightly, unless the limb is prevented from its natural function; it is not proper to accept the opinion of one who claims that the limb should be broken from the head, and many ignorant physicians and bone-setters have done this in our country.
And this action is very reprehensible, leading to great harm, the least of which is disability; but if the crookedness and complication are recent, then it should be softened with water in which relaxing herbs have been cooked, such as mallow leaves and root, and marjoram, and the like.
And it should be bandaged with relaxing bandages such as well-made diachylon, or the sap of mallow root should be taken and beaten with chicken fat and goose grease and bandaged with it, or ripe figs should be taken and pounded with pigeon droppings and the like of medicines called resolvents.
And sometimes the complication will be resolved by constant gentle massage with the hands, and the movement of the limb in every direction should be used at all times; but if the deformity is old and severe and hardened, and treatment with iron is necessary,
then it should be incised above, and the connection of the bone released, and whatever remains of the complication or bone should be cut with fine cuts, and gentleness should be used with effort and care, then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its place, which hinders movement, disfigures the limb, and causes the patient severe pain and suffering.  Therefore, if a dislocation occurs, one should immediately attempt to reduce it.
It should not be delayed at all, for if it is delayed, the place will swell and become inflamed, making it difficult to reduce the dislocation. Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor should it be moved or stretched while it is swollen, because this often causes the patient spasms and harmful pain.
But if this happens, one should immediately bleed the patient, then leave him until the swelling subsides somewhat. Then bathe the limb with warm water and oil, and then gently reduce it, treating each limb as described in its place.
The chapters on dislocations are also arranged, as in the case of fractures, from the top of the body to the bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
It is rare for both jaws to be dislocated.  Their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their positions, relaxing slightly; or they are completely dislocated, so that they relax completely. towards the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot restrain it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stumbles in speech. But if the dislocation is slight, in most cases it returns of its own accord very easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to hasten its reduction quickly and not delay it at all. This is done by having an attendant hold the patient's head, while the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
or he inserts both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers from the outside, with which he manipulates it, and he orders the patient to relax his jaw and release it to go to every side, and the physician manipulates the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then use fomentations with hot water and oil until their reduction is facilitated, and do not delay their reduction at all, as we have said.  When they have returned and are straight, and the patient's mouth is closed and they are not relaxed, then apply to them bandages of linen with a plaster made from wax, oil, and rosewater.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on his back with his head raised between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left, nor does he strain to chew anything, but let his food be soft gruel.
Until the pain is gone and the jaw is set, let him eat whatever he wishes, and let him use it gently and not strain to open his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws is difficult, if they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then fevers and persistent headache often result from this, and perhaps the patient's abdomen will become distended, and perhaps he will vomit repeatedly pure bile. If you see this, then know that it is damaged.
And many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the reduction of the clavicle and the tip of the shoulder.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection to the chest. It may dislocate outwards, and this is apparent to the touch. Its reduction is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms.
Then press the area with your hand strongly, and it will return. Then apply a bandage and compresses and bind it tightly. As for its end which is next to the shoulder and connected to it, it rarely dislocates. If it dislocates, it should be reduced and set as we have mentioned and as you are able.
Then apply a bandage and compresses and bind it tightly, and instruct the patient to observe rest and stillness until it heals. By this same treatment, the tip of the shoulder is also reduced if it has also left its place.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one is that it dislocates towards the armpit downwards; the second is that it dislocates towards the chest; and sometimes it dislocates upwards towards the shoulder, but this is rare.
It does not dislocate to the back because of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate to the front because of the nerve. It most often dislocates and comes out downwards towards the armpit, especially in those who have little flesh, because it quickly comes out and quickly goes in.
But as for those who have much flesh, it is otherwise, that is to say, it comes out with difficulty and goes in with difficulty.  Sometimes a blow or a fall happens to some people, and the shoulder swells with a hot swelling, so they think that it has dislocated; it is necessary to examine this until you ascertain its truth.
Then you proceed to its treatment.  The dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit: if you compare the dislocated shoulder with the sound shoulder, you will find a clear difference between them; and you will find the head of the shoulder dislocated, and under the armpit, on touching, the head of the shoulder, as if it were an egg.
And the patient is unable to raise his hand to his ear, nor to move it in all movements.  Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find that apparent to the touch, not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  Its reduction is that a servant raises his hand upwards, then you place your thumbs under his armpit and raise the joint strongly upwards into its place, while the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lowers it downwards, and it returns quickly. If it does not return by what we have mentioned, and the dislocation has been some days, then the patient should bathe in hot water, and use things which relax and soften, such as boiling the root of mallow, fenugreek, and rue in water, and using it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and you place under his armpit a ball of wool, which is moderate between soft and hard. Then the physician places his heel on the ball, and holds the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, and it returns to its place. And if you wish, reduce it in this way: you bring a taller man than the patient, and position him at the side, and he puts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand towards his lower belly. If the patient is light, then something else should be hung from him to weigh him down, and the jaw will return immediately. And it may also be reduced in another way.
And that is to fix in the ground a long piece of wood, its head round in shape like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin.  Then it is placed under the patient's armpit, after placing a soft cloth on the head of the wood, and the patient stands along the length of the wood.
Then his hand is extended downwards from the other side, and his body is also extended forcefully from the other side. Then the joint returns to its place quickly. If its reduction is difficult by all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is about four fingers, and its thickness is about two fingers. It has a round head to facilitate its entry deep into the armpit in this manner:
Then a soft cloth is tied to the round head, lest the wood harm the patient. Then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are extended down along the wood, and the wood is tied to the arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbeam. Let it be level across, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. Then the joint enters immediately. If it enters in any way possible, then a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder, as it goes around from above. Then the ball under the armpit is tightly bound, and the bandage is wound over the bandage from above, and his hand is hung from his neck.
He is left without moving his hand for seven days. The patient's food should be light so that the limb may be strengthened, for this hastens healing. Then, after seven or five days, it is untied and exercised. If it is firm and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to dampness or another cause, cauterization with three cautery irons should be used, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint is not stable,
the bandage and binding are repeated many times and it is not stable, and it falls and relaxes and cannot be raised upwards, then know that the nerve in the head of the shoulder is severed, stretched, or relaxed. Then know that the joint will never be stable in its place.
As for the dislocation that occurs towards the chest and breast and to the back, its reduction is by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns. All binding and treatment are used until it heals. If, after healing, stiffness occurs in the limb and movement is slow,
the patient should use the bath repeatedly until that stiffness softens and returns to its former state.
Chapter twenty-seven.
In the treatment of elbow dislocation.
The elbow joint is difficult to dislocate and also difficult to reduce. It dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is visible and palpable, whatever form the dislocation takes.
If you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, it will be clearly evident to you by the deformity of the joint; and he will not be able to bend the arm or touch his shoulder with it. You should hasten to reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot inflammation occurs.
For if a hot inflammation occurs, reduction is difficult, and it may never heal completely, especially if the dislocation is backward; for this is the worst of all types of dislocation, and the most painful, and often death ensues.
And its reduction, if it is possible to reduce it, is done by having an attendant extend his hand with both his hands and arm extended, while the physician's hands are placed above and below the elbow, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both his hands, or with the base of his palms, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is forward, it may be reduced by bending the arm sharply until the base of the hand strikes the corresponding shoulder. If the dislocation does not reduce, then use strong traction, namely, having two attendants extend the arm.
And let two attendants also hold the patient so that he does not move during traction; then the arm is rotated in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or broad bandage around the hand. And if the physician touches the joint with his hands, let him anoint them with oil, so that the joint may be easily moved.
Then he pushes the joint strongly until it returns. After its return, a bandage should be applied which includes compression and desiccation with egg white, and it is tied tightly, and the arm is hung from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is untied. If the joint is firm in its place, untie the bandage and leave it. But if you see that the joint is not strengthened sufficiently, reapply the bandage and leave it also for several days until it strengthens, then untie it. If a swelling occurs after the reduction of the joint and its unbinding,
and there is difficulty in movement, then let him use fomentation in the bath and gentle massage and manipulation until it softens; or apply a poultice of fat mutton to the joint, then bandage it and leave it on for a day and a night, then remove it and place him in the bath. When he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then reapply the poultice once, twice, and three times, with the use of the bath, until it softens.  And if you wish to apply a moist, thin poultice of cow dung with fat and bind it on, do that several times, for it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, except that it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time it dislocates, before the place becomes swollen or a hot swelling appears.
To reduce it, place the patient's wrist on a board, and let an assistant extend his hand; then the physician should place his hand on the prominence of the joint and push it until it returns. However, one should look to see if the dislocation has gone to the inside of the hand.
If the dislocation is prominent towards the outside of the hand, let him place the palm of his hand on the board, so that the physician's hand may fall on the same prominence of the joint. If it returns immediately, well and good; otherwise, bind it with a bandage that soothes the swelling.
Leave it and do not manipulate it again, for it will not tolerate it, and you will not be able to reduce it after several days have passed, except that the joint will remain crooked and will not harm the patient, unless the hand becomes relaxed and unable to grasp anything.
Then you know that the nerve is severed or bruised; there is no remedy for it except cauterization, which may or may not be effective. When the reduction of the wrist is complete, apply the bandage we have described, then tighten it and leave it for five days.
Then unbind it and exercise the hand; if its movement is difficult and there is any stiffness in it, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter twenty-nine.
In the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may be dislocated in every direction; if a finger is dislocated towards the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns, then bind the head of the finger and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight that day. If it is nighttime, bind it in the same manner; continue to unbind it during the day, exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bind it towards that same direction and do as you did before until it heals.  Do likewise if it is dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
If a vertebra of the vertebrae of the back or neck is completely dislocated, or if many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death quickly overtakes the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excrement passes involuntarily, he is unable to retain it, and often some of his limbs relax, either his legs or his arms, or one of them. But if one vertebra is displaced from its position, it often spontaneously returns.
And its displacement occurs in four directions. That which is displaced backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, one should observe: if the hump has occurred since childhood, there is no treatment for it, and there is no cure at all. But that which occurs from a fall or a blow, and the like...
The ancients mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which are not beneficial. I have shortened this, taking what suffices from much of what they presented, through my approximation of the meaning and its explanation, and I have depicted the instrument differently from how they described and explained it.
I say that the hump which occurs in the front of the chest has no remedy and no cure, and the same applies to that which occurs to the sides.  Only that which occurs in the back is treated, specifically by what I describe, which is that the patient is laid on his face.
on a level bench near a wall, and a moist mat is spread beneath him so that his chest is not harmed. Then place an upright wooden post fixed in a hole in the ground at the end of the bench towards his head, and another wooden post towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the wood, which should not be fixed in the hole, and another servant holds the other in the same manner. Then a soft, tight bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under his armpits, and the end of the bandage is extended to the wooden post at his head and tied to it.
Then tighten with another bandage above his hips, above his knees, and at his heels. Then gather all the bandages and tie them to the other wooden post at his feet. Then each servant pulls his wooden post with the bandage, and the two wooden posts do not move from their fixed positions.
except that they are not fixed, as we said.  The physician places his hands firmly on the hump until it returns, or he places a board on it and then leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board about three cubits long.
and a place is dug in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, into which the end of the board is inserted. Then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until he presses the hump.
and it returns to its place. And if you wish to make it with a hand-twisted screw, you insert two pieces of wood, each a cubit long, into the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the shop, and two pieces of wood at his feet at the end of the shop as well.
Let the distance between each piece of wood be a cubit. And a hole is made in each piece of wood through which the screw passes. And all the wood is firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all. And insert a round stick, which is the screw in which the bandage is twisted, into the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole in which a stick a cubit long is fixed by twisting. And the same is done in the other two pieces of wood. Then tighten the bandage that you tightened on the patient's chest in the screw at his head, and the bandages that you tightened on his legs in the screw at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw, twisting his hand with the tool with which he twists the screw, and the physician levels the hump as we have previously explained. And this is an image of the screw, the shop, and the patient:
Then, after the vertebra returns and the place is level, a drying bandage should be applied with egg white, then lint, and then place on top of the bandage a splint of wood, its width being three fingers or thereabouts, and its length being sufficient to cover the area of the hump.
And on some sound beads, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses light food until he recovers. If some protrusion remains in the place at the end of the healing, then it is necessary to use medicines that relax and soften, along with using the splint we described for a long time.
And a lead plate may be used in that.  And a protrusion may appear at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it is thought to be a dislocation, and it is an extra bone that has protruded; do not treat it with this treatment, for death may result.
Chapter Thirty-One.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and they are not subject to what happens to the other joints of slight displacement and depression. The hip joint is dislocated in four ways.
That is, it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards. Most often it is dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.  The sign of its dislocation inwards is that if you compare the sound leg of the patient with the affected leg, it will be longer.
And the knee will be more bent than the sound one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg at the groin, and the area near the groin will be clearly swollen, because the head of the femur has gone there.
And the sign of that which is dislocated outwards is that its symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms. And the sign of that which is dislocated forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he tries to walk, he cannot do so forwards.
And he retains his urine, and his groin swells, and when walking, he treads on his heel. And the sign of that which is dislocated backwards is that he does not extend the knee, and he cannot bend it before bending the groin, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly in the position of the loin. As for the reduction of the types of this dislocation, you should look; if the dislocation is old, having persisted with its sufferer, and no attempt has been made to reduce it, and it remains in its state, then there is absolutely no treatment for it.
So you should not attempt it. As for that which is recently dislocated, whatever its type may be of the four kinds of dislocation, hasten to twist the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and to the left, perhaps...
it will return and will not need any other treatment. If it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong and able assistant who will extend his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around his leg above the knee, and another assistant who will extend it from above.
so that he puts his hands under his armpits, then tightens a soft bandage on the root of the thigh, and another third assistant holds the end of the bandage, and the extension is either from the front, from the side of the groin to the side of the clavicle, or from the back to the side of the back.
And their extension is all at once, until the patient is raised from the ground with his body and remains suspended. For this type of extension is a common type for the four types. If the dislocation returns as we have said, otherwise, each type needs the special treatment that I will mention.
As for its special reduction, if the dislocation is inwards, the patient should lie on his sound side, then a bandage is applied to the root of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the place under the groin.
Then the bandage is extended upwards from the groin to the upper body towards the clavicle, then another strong and able assistant takes it with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a strong pull, then it will return to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced. If it is difficult for you and it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then you should tie the patient's legs together with a strong, soft bandage on the ankles and knees.
And the distance between each of them and its neighbor is four fingers, and the affected leg is extended more than the other by two fingers, then the patient is suspended by the head from a wooden beam in the house, and his distance from the ground is two arms' length.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh, and another boy hangs onto the patient, and the other boy embracing the thigh pushes strongly, then the joint returns to its place quickly. As for its special reduction if the dislocation is outwards.
Then the patient should lie down on the bench as we described in the case of the hunchback, and a bandage is tightened around his affected leg in particular and his chest, then two wooden planks are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head, then an additional wooden plank is placed in the middle of the bench, very firmly secured.
A wet rag is wrapped around it so that it does not hurt the patient, so that the wooden plank is between his thighs so that he does not slide downwards when stretched, then each servant pulls to his side and the physician sets the hip with his hands, if it responds to reduction.
Otherwise, place the plank on him and press down as we mentioned in the case of the hunchback, except that the patient should lie on his sound side, and if the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg should be extended once while he is in this same position on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand, and with that he makes the pressure extend downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backward, then the patient should not be extended downwards.
And he is raised off the ground, but rather he should be placed on something solid, just as it should also be if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we mentioned concerning his lying on the bench on his face with the bandages tightened.
As we said before, and the pressing with the plank should also be used on the place to which the joint has gone.  If the hip joint returns completely as it should, and the sign of its return will not be hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's legs.
If you see them straight and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, then know that the member has returned as it should. Then, bind the thighs together, apply the bandage and tighten it with a turban so that the hip does not move in any direction.
And the patient must remain still for three or four days. Then untie the bandage and compare the leg to the other; if you see them equal in size, then know that the fracture has healed, so allow the patient to walk. And if you see some laxness in itâ€¦
then return and bandage it and tighten it according to your first tightening, and leave it also for three days, then untie it, and let him walk slowly on it for days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter thirty-two.
In the treatment of a fracture of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards, that is to say backwards; it does not dislocate forwards at all.  The sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to bring his leg towards his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. The treatment for all types of its dislocation is that you make the patient sit with his legs extended if he is able to do so, and a servant sits behind him to hold his waist and twist him slightly backwards, then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back against his face and put his leg between your legs, then you grasp his knee with your hands and interlock your fingers on his knee, then you press with your hands strongly on both sides of his knee, and another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh softly and without force. Then bandage it and adhere the leg to the thigh, then bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. And he should only use a little walking for some days until it strengthens.
If you are unable to return it by what we have described, then use strong traction with the bandages which I previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three
In the treatment of the dislocation of the heel.
The heel may be dislocated slightly, or it may be completely dislocated.  Its dislocation is either inwards or outwards, and the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and prominent in the direction in which it dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight displacement, it is easy to return it; this is done by gently stretching it with the hands and straightening it until it returns. As for its treatment if it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you seat the patient, and a strong servant should hold him from behind his back at his waist.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand from below the foot at the place of the heel; then pull the foot towards you with your right hand and then with your left twice; then straighten it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand towards the leg without force.
Do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot towards the leg on the third occasion while you pull at the heel. If it returns in one or two times in this way and you see the foot straight, otherwise repeat the procedure, for it will return.
If it refuses to return by what we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and firmly fix a wedge in the ground so that it lies between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it so that it does not harm the patient; then a servant should hold his thigh.
Then another servant stretches the leg, either with his hands or with a bandage which he ties around the neck of the leg; then each servant stretches in the opposite direction to his companion, the wedge remaining between the patient's thighs holding it so that his body is not pulled downwards during the stretching; then the physician sets the joint with his hands.
And another servant holds the sound leg below, for the joint returns quickly; and when the joint returns and you are certain of its correct return, apply the dressing and bandage and tighten it with firm bandages and bind the foot below with a bandage; and it is necessary to protect the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
lest the bandage be tight upon it and harm it; then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it, then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and the patient must abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be sure that the joint will not be dislocated again and become spoiled and will not then accept treatment; if a hot swelling appears, it is necessary to use for its alleviation what we have previously described in another place in the treatment and cleansing until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the toes of the foot.
It is necessary to set what is dislocated by gentle traction without force; this is not difficult but easy. If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the instep, it is necessary that the patient place his foot on a level surface of the ground.
Or on a board while he is standing as if walking; then you stand and place your foot on the dislocated joint, and then press down on it with your foot strongly until it returns and you see that it is straight and there is no protrusion in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that takes the whole foot, which has two ends; then bind it tightly and securely for three days, then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and the return is prevented, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation which occurs with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When something like that happens and you attempt its treatment and setting, it often results in death.
Therefore, no one should undertake the treatment of such a thing except someone skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, careful, not reckless or rash, and that he should initially use only medications that soothe hot swellings and leave the patient alone.
By God's power, except what you hoped for its safety from harm with mildness of the disease and some hope appeared to you in it, then return it immediately at the first instance before the hot swelling appears.
If the limb returns to what you intended, then use the treatment that soothes hot swellings and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments; if the dislocation is with a fracture and there are detached bone fragments, then remove them. And follow in that what we mentioned in the simple diseases that preceded in their places.
And strive your best and refrain from entering the path of uncertainty, as I previously advised you, for that is safer for your reputation and more secure for your honor, God willing.
